“THE BULLETIN."
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“Tli© Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One .Person, one Vote.
Com ole bo Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.; —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded. ,
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System - one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole;
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility, ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
tlie day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter* that is not
o! general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses-a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in wljich
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian,continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit .by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local arid distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid, newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. ' It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the clabns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red coveris equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations (he cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take.
What are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLjb o,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

Bayonets doubling up “ like leaden bars ”
are reported as the result of a bayonet-contest by
volunteers at Hobart recently. “The backbone
of the Empire ! ”
A German savant says that the red-backed
spider, common about Sydney, is the most
poisonous spider in the world. One twenty-
eighth of a grain of his poison injected into the
blood of a 12st. man would be fatal.
Some time ago a man living in a bush
township went to Melbourne for a week, lie was
a widower living in an isolated house of his own,
and he just locked up and cleared without saying
a word to anybody. Before he went, he lit his
fire and made a cup of tea, arid gnawed the last
remnant of flesh from an ancient ham-bone ; then
got on his horse, whistled to his dog, and rode toff.
Half an hour afterwards a log (that is the theory)
fell out of the fireplace and burnt the house, down
without anybody noticing it. Next day somebody
passed and saw’ smoke, and smelt fire, and reported
to the police. The police “ made enquiries,” and
found the man hadn’t been seen for several days.
So they searched the ashes and found the ham-
bone, and decided that the man had been too
drunk to crawl out of the way of the flames, and
the ham-bone was all that was left of him. They
iplaced'it on a sapling bier, and covered it decently
with a uniform coat, and carried it shoulder-
high to-the doctor, who, after putting some dex-
terous questions, identified it as the scapula of an
adult male about 274 years of age and sft. 7|in. high
(the man wasexactly 27^years of age and sft. 7f in.
high). Then a coroner’s jury sat on the ham-bone,
and returned a verdict of “ Suicide while of un-
sound mind ; ” and next day the ham-bone was
put in an elegant coffin, covered with flowers, and
followed bare-headed by several, townspeople to
the cemetery, where a minister made affecting
discourse over the solitary remnant of the departed,
and pointed out how the remainder of him was at
that moment soaring on muslin wings through
golden streets, where banana-peel never falls on
the pavement. Then the funeral party 1 ' proceeded
slowly homewards, and at the end of the main
street met the corpse just returned from Mel-
bourne. As soon as they-recovered from their
astonishment they told him he was a low liver,
and a drunken sot, and an atheist,, and presented
him with a bill for <£l2 11s. for his own funeral
expenses. He objected; but the ham-bone was
resurrected and placed in evidence against him,
and when he still refused to pay up the police
arrested him for personating the ham-bone under
the 163 th clause of the Elections Act. and he was
sentenced to six months’ imprisonment. Local
public feeling is dead against him, and when he
gets tout he will have to go to Coolgardie.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “ Cannalin ” treatment, large quan-
tities,of winch are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
Dear BiftLhTiN,—A little practical experi-
ence of the London retail “ Australian Meat ”
trade. I left Sydney for England last October,
arid as we' found, on starting to keep house, that
mutttin-chops cost Is. 2d. per lb. our thoughts turned
towards “ Australian Frozen Meat,” the bulk of
which, by the way, is no doubt sold to the pre-
judiced British public as fine British meat at the
aforesaid Is 2d. per lb., for Australian mutton, as
such, is most difficult to get. After much seeking
I discovered the Leadenhall Meat-market a few
weeks ago, and there got a fine forequarter of
Australian lamb at 7jd. per lb. Freezing cer-
tainly had not spoiled that meat, for I had not met
with anything sweeter or better in England. We
kept portion uncooked for four days to test its
“ staying” powers, and found it quite as good at
the end of that time. I have been living at Chel-
sea—a thickly-populated district—where the very
poorest meat is ticketed lOd. On Saturdays,
about midnight, hundreds of people can be
seen waiting outside the various butchers shops
to rush the odds and ends left over, for which they
gladly pay from sd. to Bd. lam convinced that
much of this meat is Australian, but in deference
to the popular feeling it is sold as English-
grown, at lOnglish-grovvn prices. Loudon alone
could easily use treble its present consumption of
Australian mutton of good quality, but if the
growers wish to utilise the market, they must
organise a system of retail shops, otherwise the
present “ririg” of butchers, who are, of course,
coining money out of the business, will go on
deceiving the public. The chief reason why retail
buyers object to Australian meat is that the only
title they fancy they see it is when some inter-
esled butcher shows them some dried-up trash,
and blandlv states that it is a fine representative
spSmien of frozen meat from Australia. .The:
prewlice has been created, and will last until the
clean, Australian-managed, retail shops make their,
«»wei’uwMqp, und then good times will come both
for Australian growers and the meat-buying
British public. By the way, prawns of medium
size and with little flavour are sold here at Is. 3d.
per dozen! Could not these be easily and safely
shipped from Sydney to London to compete with
the presellt supply, wherever it comes from ? My
idea of the immense future before Australia is;
improving ’’every, week,—Dawson A. ViNDIN.
143 Strand, W. 0., London, 30th March, ’94.

SATURDAY, MAY 26, 1894.
The Law “takes its Course.”
It has been formally decided, according to
the good old lag-time statutes of New South
Wales, that the law shall “ take its course”
in the matter of Montgomery and Williams,
the Sydney burglars. This historic phrase is,
in itself,*a serious reflection upon the law,
for whenever it “takes its course” some-
body is hanged as the inevitable consequence,
and when nobody is hanged it is only be-
cause the law has been violently arrested in
its gallop. Therefore, the natural course of
New South Wales law is obviously towards
the soaped noose, and the gallows, and the
stipendiary man-butcher. In the present
case, it seems likely, at time of writing,
that the statutes will go their blood-
stained way without serious remonstrance.
The two condemned men are not of the
kind who arouse much public sympathy.
They are' habitual criminals ; they ate
coarse, illiterate, badly-dressed, and alto-
gether uiuesthetic of aspect, their portraits
have been made to look gratuitously horrible
in the evening papers, and they have never
moved in “ society ” —all of which matters a
great deal. Altogether, nobody—not even
The Bulletin —takes the slightest real in-
terest in them personally. But this paper
takes a serious interest in the legal process
by which they have been tried and
convicted —a process which is yet another
commentary upon the blood-stained and
hideous code which Alfred Stephen, ex-
Chief Cpown Prosecutor of Yandemonia,
was mainly instrumental in fastening upon
the country of his adoption. It is a curious
coincidence, by the way, that the case of the
condemned burglars was tried before Mr.
Alfred Stephen’s son, who, judging from
the result; of his report, seems to have flouted
the jury’s recommendation to mercy in the
case of Williams.
• • •
The crime for Which these two exceedingly
uninteresting persons are condemned is
“ assault with intent to murder.” As every
sane man in the community is fully aware,
the assault was not committed “ with intent
to murder.” It was obviously committed
with intent to get away, and for no other
purpose whatsoever. The fact that the
criminals made no use of their revolvers is
one evidence of that fact ; another is that
when they had knocked down the available
“ force ” and could have murdered it with-
out difficulty, had they been so inclined,
they made not the slightest effort to do so.
When a person has an intent to commit
murder and gets the opportunity to do so
he naturally commits it; if he refrains
the reasonable assumption is that he
had not the intent. This fact is so
obvious that it has not even been seriously
disputed. But the gore-spattered code
of Botany Bay practically arrogates to
itself, or rather its administrators, under
certain conditions, the right to assume an
“ intent ” without any evidence whatever. A
certain illogical charge was formulated, and
the jury was requested to say, in one word,
whether the prisoners were wholly and
entirely guilty or wholly and entirely
innocent. The clumsy law, which New
South Wales dug out from the savage code
of England in its pre-civilisation days, cheer-
fully assumes that there can be no medium
between these two extremes.
* * •
In France, the United States, and various
other countries, the crime of murder is
divided into certain degrees, according to
the provocation given, the amount of pre-
meditation or the absence of premeditation,
and other attendant circumstances. There-
fore, the jury is not only enabled to say
whether the prisoner is or is not guilty,
but also, if guilty, how guilty he is,
and, within certain limits, to fix the
punishment accordingly. In barbarous
New South Wales the jurymen are merely
permitted to recommend the prisoner to
mercy. Then the Cabinet, sitting as a secret
tribunal, either adopts the recommendation
or otherwise, according to the state of its
liver or its “backbone.” The recommenda-
tion to mercy is intended by those who
make it to be as much an integral part of the
verdict as is the verdict itself. It is also as
solemn an expression of the j ury’s opinion as
is the verdict itself. Therefore, when the
Cabinet disregards such a recommendation it
deliberately puts it on record that it doesn’t
regard the jury’s opinion as sufficiently in-
telligent to be worthy of consideration.
Then it proceeds to execute the defendant,
and thereby deliberately puts it on record
that it regards the jury’s opinion as quite
sufficiently intelligent to hang a man upon ;
consequently the assumption is that it takes
much less intelligence co hang a man than to
spare his life. The whole proceeding is
nearly related to lynch-law and half-brother
to judicial murder. If the Government
arrogates to itself the right to accept one-
half of the jury’s verdict and reject the other
half—which is an equally deliberate expres-
sion of opinion—then it might as well dis-
pense with the jury altogether and find its
own verdict.
* * •
A recommendation to mercy is surely not
the empty formality it is commonly supposed
to be. The State, in this case, formally
charged the prisoners with assault and tacked
on to it an “intent ” of which there was no
good evidence. The jurymen, under the
pressure of overwhelming proof, necessarily
found the assault charge to be absolutely
true ; and the recommendation which they
attached to their verdict, in regard to
Williams, was practically the only method
which their oath, and the N.S.W. statutes,
and the evidence allowed them of stating
that they found the alleged “ intent ” to be
false. The Government, however, decides
to hang both prisoners on the first half
of the verdict, which is just about as
bad as hanging them on no verdict at all.
So long as the statutes are so built as to give
a jury merely the right to choose between
finding a prisoner wholly innocent —which is
generally a lie—or finding him wholly guilty
—which is often a lie also—a recommenda-
tion to mercy should be regarded as an in-
tegral portion of the verdict and acted on
accordingly.
» • *
The Cabinet’s decision that the law should
take its course was, in the present instance,
almost a foregone conclusion. The master-
ful Premier Dibbs, by a ghastly display of
political indecency,'practically judged the case
and found the prisoners guilty even before the
beginning of the preliminary inquiry. The
law holds—in theory—that every person is
innocent until he is proved to be otherwise ;
therefore, until Montgomery and Williams
were legally convicted, Premier Dibbs had
no legal evidence that any burglary had been
committed, or that there had been any
encounter with the police, or that anything
unusual had happened. Nevertheless, he
proceeded to the hospital immediately after
the fracas to compliment the police upon
their bravery, which then rested entirely on
their own statements, and to recommend
that they should be promoted and otherwise
rewarded for their valuable services—which
also thenrested entirely on their own evidence.
There are no valuable services rendered
when the police arrest the wrong men by
mistake, consequently Mr. Dibbs was simply
giving it forth as an indubitable fact that the
police had arrested the right men. Also,
there is no bravery in arresting a prisoner
who doesn’t resist, therefore Mr. Dibbs
declared by his proceedings that the accused
had resisted desperately. His action, at such
a time and under such circumstances, was a
public announcement that the assertions of
the police were absolutely true, and that any
counter-assertion or counter-evidence which
might be advanced by the prisoners was
absolutely false. In fact, the Premier almost
announced, a few hours after the arrest, that
the law ought to take its course, and his
present decision that it is to take its course
is, therefore, in most respects, a mere
formality.
* m
It is only when the case of Montgomery
and Williams is placed alongside two others
of recent date that the utter iniquity of the
law, and the still greater iniquity of its
administration, become fully apparent.
These two men did not seek the police with
a view to attacking them ; their crime was
not premeditated ; and their alleged “ intent
to murder ” is fully disproved by the fact
that they made no effort to follow up that
alleged intent when they had abundant
opportunity to do so. Nevertheless, the
law, in one of its blind, unreasoning out-
breaks of panic and blood-drunkenness, fixes
the “intent” on them, and sentences them
to the gallows for a mere impromptu scrim-
mage. The two Broken Hill constables who
recently attacked and slaughtered an aggres-
sive drunk, after he had ceased to be aggres-
sive, deliberately went in search of murderous
weapons, then followed up their enemy, and
shot at him persistently until they killed
him. Yet the law refused to regard all this
deliberate shooting at an unarmed man as a
sign of any intent whatever, and promptly
set them at liberty. In the case of Thomas
Pert, the “ push ” hunted for its victim
day and night, and killed him at the
first convenient opportunity, and in this
instance two of the assailants got a short
sentence, and the others were released. The
inference is, therefore, that, in New South
Wales, when several men deliberately pursue
and attack one and kill him, his death is
sufficient evidence that they didn’t intend to
kill him, and when three men have an un-
premeditated encounter with five or six, and
don’t kill any of them, that is solid evidence
that they did intend to kill them. The proof
that they did intend to do the crime lies in
their not doing it, and the proof that the
other lot didn’t intend to do the same crime
lies in the fact that they did it. The alterna-
tive inference is that the law is strained
in the case of Montgomery and Williams
because they had added burglary to their
other sins—that the alleged “intent to
murder ” is a hollow imposture intended to
protect the sacred interests of Property—
and that the law is to take its course,
not because of the midnight scramble with
the police but by reason of a brutal attack
on the Union S.S. Co.’s iron safe. The
truth lies between these two alternatives
and nowhere else, and the public is left to
select whichever one makes the law seem
less ridiculous or less loathsome in its own
eyes.
Stained Glass Window Education.
Chancellor Manning, of Sydney University,
ex-Supreme Court Justice of N.S. Wales,
recently “broke with reluctance a rule of 56
years’ standing ” and wrote to the newspapers
in protest against the withdrawal of part
of the endowment with which the Univer-
sity is assisted by Government. Such a
tremendous cataclysm of ex-judicial nature
obviously invites investigation, which discloses
the following facts :—Since 1851 the N.S.
Wales taxpayers have presented the University
with £714,877 10s. 6d in hard cash. In return,
the University has presented N.S. Wales with
1126 graduates. In 1892 there were 90
graduates for a total expenditure of £40,083,
or £445 per graduate. Salaries amounted
to £18,625, distributed among 44 professors—
an average of £423, each professor producing
two graduates and one twenty-secondth of a
graduate, at a cost of £206 per graduate. This
estimate takes no account of private benefac-
tions amounting to £409,000. If the students
be charged with 5 per cent, interest on a capital
of, say, £BOO,OOO sunk in the University, and
yielding no income available for direct expendi-
ture, which is only fair, wdien the institution is
regarded in the light of a valuable educational
investment, the cost of a graduate in 1892 was
£BB9. It is probably the consciousness that
they are dear at the money which impels the
undergraduates to throw in extra value in the
form of an annual display of larrikinism on
Commemoration Day.
• * •
•A few vreeks ago a Women’s College was
added to Sydney University. The building
cost £12,652 ; £5OOO provided by the Govern-
ment, and more asked for. With furniture and
fittings, the cost will be nearly £14,000. This
building accommodates 26 scholars ; there are
at present five. The descriptive reporters
expatiate on “ white lonic columns and pilas-
ters,” “richly-modelled frieze,” “vaulted wood
ceiling,” “ tiles of a rich peacock-blue,” and
“ massive hammered iron fire-dogs.” Assuming
that all the rooms are filled, and that each
scholar stays three years, the annual cost of
this (at 5 per cent, interest on £14,000) is £BO
per scholar. That does not include board or
education, so that the female University
graduate is in a fair way to be as expen-
sive as the male. Like him, indeed, she
has the advantage of stained-glass windows.
Stained glass windows are the University’s
strong point. It can only educate 90 graduates
up to its standards for an expenditure of
£40,000, but it has all the kings and queens of
England since the Conquest in gorgeous hues,
with many less notorious personages. The
stained glass window is the typical fruit and
consummation of Sydney University training.
The funds have been lavished oh buildings and
decoration, while the end of education has been
comparatively neglected. The University is a
splendid husk, but there is no fruit.
' The University is for the children of the rich,
not for the children of the poor. As a factoi
in national life it is singularly ineffective.
methods are obsolete ; its results are pitiable
It is out of touch with modern life ; it breeds
academic noodles whose incompetence excite?
the derision of men made in the mould of the
times. University education means wasted
years, unfitness for business and public work
the need to unlearn much that has been learned
toilfully in the heyday of youth. So far from
the State increasing the endowment of such an
institution, it should take avvay the endowment
it already gives Until it has complete control of
the endowment. What people want is not a
University for a wealthy, leisured class, but a
University for the people, free to merit, and
forming the crown of a complete State educa-
tional system. It is indispensable that the
University come in direct touch with the
people, and that its heads be directly respon-
sible to the people’s representatives in Parlia-
ment. At present the University taxes rich
and poor to benefit the rich alone. That is un-
democratic, inexpedient and unjust.
Burglars’ Law.
Ex-Premier Parkes has not forgotten the
days when he read law in J, B. Darvael’s
chambers; neither has he forgotten what
he learned there. His letter on the bur-
glars’ case points out, with judicial precision
the great mistake which has been made
in the trial and conviction of the men, the
result of which mistake is that, instead of
being sent to gaol for life or a term of
years, they are to be hanged by the neck
until they are dead. He also points out
that this mistake originated in the scare
which took possession of Sydney some months
ago, under the influence of which “ the enter-
prising burglar ” became magnified into a
criminal monster whose one object was to mur-
der everyone who stood in the way of his
burgling. But for this scare Montgomery and
Williams would, have been tried under a
section of N.S. W. Criminal Law Amendment Act
which was expressly designed to meet such
cases as theirs—that is, an aggravated assault
committed for the purpose of preventing arrest.
Their assault, however, had the effect of in-
tensifying the scare to such an extent that a
hysterical Government thought it necessary to
arm every constable with a six-shooter, and
to have the men tried on a capital charge.
They were accordingly indicted under a sec-
tion of the Act which does not apply to their
case at all. Let the reader examine the words
carefully, and the intention of the Legislature
will make itself evident
16. Whosoever administers to, or causes to be taken by>
any person, any poison or other destructive thing—or by
any maans wounds or causes grievous bodily harm to any
person— with intent in any such case to commit murder
shall be liable to suffer death.
* * •
The collocation of these words obviously
points to cases in which life is deliberately
aimed at, and in which the destruction of life
is necessary to effect the criminal object—if,
for instance, the burglars had killed some
person who resisted their- attempt to burglari-
ously enter premises. No one gives another
person a dose of poison, other than a temporary
narcotic, unless he wants to destroy his life,
either to obtain possession of his money or to get
him out of the way for other reasons. This being
the class of cases which the Legislature certainly
had in view in this section, it goes on to pro-
vide for cases in which a weapon is used instead
of poison. In either of these cases, unless it is
made to appear that the poison or the weapon
was used for the purpose of taking life,
the section does not apply. Had there been
no public panic at the time, the prisoners would
have been indicted under the 22nd section of
the Act, in which the object of the Legislature
is just as clearly expressed as in the other
case ;
22.—Whosoever maliciously by any means wounds, or
inflicts grievous bodily harm upon any person—or shoots
at any person, or in any manner attempts to discharge
any kind of loaded arms at any person—with intent in
any such case to do grievous bodily harm to any person—
or with intent to resist, or prevent, the lawful apprehen-
sion or detainer either of himself or any other person
shall be liable to penal servitude for life.
* * *
Under either of these sections the Crown
must prove the intent charged in the indict-
ment or the conviction cannot stand. Was it
proved in the present case ? There was no evi-
dence to prove an intent to commit murder,
but there was plenty to prove an intent to
resist or prevent apprehension. In the former
case the test is—was the taking of life necessary
to effect the prisoners’ object ? It was not, for all
that was necessary was to disable the constables.
The Judges in England have always held that
the intention to commit murder must be shown
to have been present in the prisoner’s mind at
the time of the assault. To take one case out
of many, in Regina v. Cruse (8 c. and p. 541),
when the indictment charged the prisoner with
having inflicted an injury dangerous to life,
with intent to commit murder, it was held that
the jury ought not to convict unless they were
satisfied that the prisoner had in his mind a
positive intention to murder, and that it is not
sufficient that it woidd have been - a case of
murder if death had ensued. So here, if the
constable had died from the wound, it would
have been a case of murder; but that is not
enough to prove the intent. t
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Question of Taste.
A DAY or two ago a double-headed deputation
waited on the N.S. Wales Minister of Justice
re the case of Francis Abigail, of Australian
Banking Co. notoriety. One half of the depu-
tation wanted the prisoner released, asserting
that his sentence of five years’ imprisonment
was excessive ; the other half protested against
the proposed remission. The case for this
second deputation was, practically,’ that the
Australian Banking Co. was a premeditated
and utterly heartless fraud ; that the agitation
on Abigail’s behalf arose solely through his
political influence, and that if he had been a
person of no political influent he might have


been sentenced to ten years instead of five
without anyone putting in an appeal on his
behalf; and that there was not one redeeming
feature in the case to justify Government inter-
ference. This second deputation has come in
for much strong language from the “respect-
able” press, the Telegraph, in particular,
breaking out as follows :
The fact that the persons composing this body, or
sanctioning Us object in approaching the Minister, con-
sisted largely of sufferers through the collapse of the
Australian Banking Company, of which Abigail was chair-
man, lends air unpleasant aspect to its representations,
That many persons have suffered loss and bitter anxiety
through the frauds perpetrated in connection with that
defunct institution is true. But no rigour of punishment
that can now be visited upon those chiefly responsible for
what happened will mitigate this loss. The presence of
these persons on such an errand, or their declared
approval of it, is likely to be attributed to a spirit of
.revenge which the administrators of justice can hardly
approve.
Again :
The “ pound of flesh” deputation which waited upon
the Minister for Justice yesterday to protest against the
showing of any mercy td prisoner Abigail displayed the
spirit of anarcfiy in its crudest form. They had a per-
sonal grievance) against the man, and demanded that the
law should be administered so as to gratify their vindic-
tiveness rather’than to serve the public interest. Mr.
Bull, who appeared for the swindled shareholders and
.depositors of the society in connection with which the
Abigail fraud was committed—whatever they have to do
with the question of his sentence -was the only one who
bad good taste enough to be ashamed of his position.
His money was gone, he said, and torturing the now help-
less man who swindled him out of it would not make good
the loss.
This is poor logic. The deputationists
didn’t “demand that the law should be ad-
ministered to gratify their vindictiveness ”;
they simply demanded that the law, having
been already administered on an impartial basis
by an independent tribunal, shouldn’t be after-
wards interfered with through political pres-
sure. Their conduct might be in bad taste, but
the fact remains that they were upholding the
law, and it is a new thing to find the “ respect-
able ” press taking sides against the law’s up-
holders. Even in the matter of taste, however,
they followed a highly respectable precedent.
A few years ago a crowd of influential Sydney
citizens, hailing from the Exchange, brought
strong pressure to bear on Governor Carington
to prevent him granting a reprieve to the Mount
Rennie prisoners, and neither the Telegraph
nor the Herald made any allusion to their
“crude anarchy” or their “vindictiveness.”
These unspeakably brutal persons deliberately
urged the Governor, not merely to refuse a com-
mutation to a short-sentenced prisoner, whose
case can be reconsidered at any time, but to go
ahead at once with a wholesale hanging (ordered
at a trial, the brutality of which, as was
remarked by the late W. B. Dalley, could
only be paralleled in the darkest and
most bloody days of Irish history) and put
the matter beyond all further consideration.
On that occasion the ’ social influence was with
the party which demanded a general strangula-
tion, and the people to be strangled had no
social influence, so the Telegraph was silent;
in the present instance, the social influence
is with the man in gaol, and the people who
were defrauded by his “ bank ” are of no special
account in the community, so the Telegraph
rises to point out that the latter crowd are act-
ing contrary to the “public interest”—how
Abigail’s release would serve the public inter-
est isn't explained. Also, it complains because
itlie deputation was composed of persons who
ihad suffered greatly by the Australian Banking
■Co. swindle, and holds that to be an aggrava-
tion of their offence. Here, at least, the Mount
Rennie petitioners were free from blame ; they
had no wrongs to aggravate them, and for the
most part howled for the gallows from pure
bloodthirstiness and a natural yearning for blood
and butchery. Also—and on this point the
respectable organ comes out especially
strong —the money lost by the Abigail frauds
is gone, and no amount of torture inflicted on
the miserable prisoner can bring it back.
Which statement is undoubtedly true, not only
in this case but in every other, and if it is a
reason for the release of one influential ex-
politician it is also a reason for a general gaol-
delivery throughout the province. All the
money that has been stolen is gone, and all the
crimes that have been committed are done,
and no amount of imprisonment, or hanging, or
flogging will undo them. The Telegraph has
struck a solemn truth; the only pity is that
the truth didn’t strike it till a person of influence
belonging to its own especial political crowd
was concerned.
Insanity and Crime.
Dr. P. H. Todd’s lecture on “Insanity as a
Defence in Criminal Trials,” delivered before
Sydney Articled Clerks’ Association, was in-
teresting and unscientific. It halted between
the legal theory of criminal responsibility and
the scientific theory of general irresponsibility.
Conventional morality, as embodied in law,
punishes crime in the individual; although, so
far as the individual is concerned, vice and
virtue are scientifically equal. It is no merit
in the agent to do good, no sin to do evil. Only
the influence of his acts upon others confers
their ethical quality. In his relations to him-
self every man is an effect; in his relations to
society he is a cause. A logical doctrine of crime
and punishment can only take into account the
individual as a cause. Social praise and blame
have no basis except as stimulants to future
action. They are influences which help to shape the
individual to the mould of society. The punish-
ment of crime, in the ordinary meaning of
punishment, has no justification. The criminal
is the product of causes over which he has no
control; he falls as inevitably as the apple falls.
The judge who imprisons him, flogs him, hangs
him, is, as an individual, just as guilty as he
is. Guilt only comes to the member of a
society. The criminal whose crime hurts none
but himself, and influences none but himself,
is no criminal. Punishment is only justified in
so far as it prevents future crime ; and the
conventional reprobation of crime has no
better footing. Thus, scientifically, the man
who is normally sane and the man who is
palpably insane stand on exactly the same
ground of responsibility. The question of what
shall be done with them is merely a question
how best to make them innoxious to society.
If it is just to imprison them, it does not matter
whether the place of imprisonment is a gaol or
an asylum. If it is wrong to hang the lunatic,
it is equally wrong to hang the sane criminal,
unless it can be shown that he is a greater
danger to society. But these conceptions are
still far ahead of the popular sense of jus-
tice, though that sense is gradually being
elevated in the light of science. A century
ago, lunatics were hanged with the rest; even
now, had “ Jack the Ripper ” been caught red-
handed, his madness would not have saved him.
Deeming was sexually insane ; but that did not
lessen the cry for his blood. Colston was in-
sane—and they hanged him. O’Farrell was
insane—and they hanged him. Dr. Todd
wishes to widen the present legal definition of
insanity, which is doubtless desirable and right.
But in proportion as the definition of lunacy is
widened the liberty of lunatics must be
narrowed. A man’s sanity or insanity makes
no difference in his criminality, and the punish-
ment should depend on the danger which he
focuses as a direct or indirect source of future
crime. Medical evidence must not be inter-
posed as a shield from the consequences of
social guilt.
That profoundly dull personage, R. Hoddle D.
White, of N.S. Wales Legislative Council,
writes to S.M. Herald to demand that the
jury’s recommendation, to mercy in the case of
the Sydney burglars, Montgomery and Wil-
liams, be disregarded, and that they be hanged
for “ intent to murder.” As a reason for this,
Hoddle tells over again, for the hundredth
time, the well-advertised yarn of his own
valour when he met a gory bushranger at some
remote spot about 20 or 40 years ago. Every-
body has heard this story over and over again—
for Hoddle even drags it in at public meetings
of bank shareholders. This time he solemnly
pastes his experiences on the gallows, and inci-
dentally uses the two condemned men as a
personal advertisement—thus :
There are few men in New South Wales who have had
better opportunity, from personal experience, of judtfimj
about this matter and of marking the dire effects result-
ing from a misplaced clemency in the case of certain
criminals. I take the case of Lbb's gmg who stuck up
Mr. Miller and self at Denitiquin, in 1858 or 9. Catching
us unarmed as we left the dinner-table, they rushed us,
put loaded revolvers to our heads, actu dly shaking
against our skins ; and, judging from their conduct, it
was only a miracle our lives were not taken. For this
offence Lee and Drew received 12 years’ imprisonment,
the two others less, Judge Wise informing them they
had narrowly escaped the death penalty. While in
the gaol in Goullmrn they nearly escaped by
tunnelling through the wall ; being removed to Cocka-
too, they immediately instigated the well - known
riots there ; being removed to Berrima, Lee was
sentenced to death for the murder of a constable.
While in Darlinghurst Gaol he threw the warder over the
balcony of the corrider, almost killing him and crippling
him for life. Had capital punishment been inflicted in
the first instance, all this career of crime and murder
would have been avoided. On another occasion, when
assisting the police to capture a desperate ruffian in
Queensland, the man, at close quarters, threw three-parts
of a brick at my head totrv and brain me but, fortunately,
it just glanced off my shoulder as I rushed forward and
captured him. Again, I was attacked by five bushrangers
and fired upon at close quarters. These are only a few of
my experiences whilst gold-buying and conveying bank-
money about in the different colonies.
“Only a few!” The Bulletin strongly advises
Mr. Hoddle White, who has been pursued by
so many shockingly bad shots, to publicly get
the rest of his many penny-dreadfuls off his
chest and have done with them finally. Not,
however, that anybody wants to see them in
the newspapers. The hero might have them
advertised on the tombstones of the Bridge-
street burglars.
Sydney Truth, supposed to be chiefly controlled
by Premier Dibbs’ bosom friend, Nicholas
Willis, M.L.A., professes to give an account
of how the head of the N.S.W. State decided
uponthehanging of Montgomery and Williams
(the italics are Truth’s) :
When the Colonial Secretary read the two statements,
he said, “Damn it, Montgomery is a Man, and I'd be glad
of an excuse to spare his life ; but the Law is definite, and
the circumstances of the case show no extenuating cir-
cumstances sufficient to warrant Ex cutive interference.”
With regard to Williams’ abject appeal, the Colonial Sec-
retary was equally emphatic—“ As to the other cowardly
animal, he is not even loyal to his colleague in crime and
adversity. He says he is a child of God, and therefore ”
(tossing Williams’ statement on one side) “ I think the
best thing we can do is to send him home to his father
with as little delay as possible.”
The Bulletin has waited some days since this
statement was published in the expectation of
hearing that Mr. Dibbs, President of the New
South Wales court of final appeal in the case
of condemned criminals, had risen up and in-
dignantly refuted the foregoing gross slander.
As no such information has arrived, however,
it would almost appear that Premier Dibbs is
countenancing Truth’s defamatory assertions.
If the picture is true, then Premier Dibbs, in
his capacity as arbiter of life and death, and
sayer of smart things, is indeed an unpleasant
spectacle. As head of the final court of appeal,
civilised men expect him to consider cases,
especially capital cases, seriously and solemnly.
His pal’s paper represents him, instead, as
considering them by an airy process of damning
and throwing papers around, and attributes to
him a “blasphemous” joke, which would be
hissed down even at a sixpenny nigger-show.
The picture is ghoulish and wholly disgusting.
Let us hope, for the credit of humanity, that it
is altogether malicious and false.

The Sydney Telegraph wants to have Francis
Abigail released on the ground that the money
which disappeared in his awful “ bank ” is gone
past recovery, and nothing is to be gained by
torturing him further. How does this apply to
the case of Sweetman, the Mount Rennie cab-
man, who was sentenced to 14 years and two
floggings for taking a pavement-trotting girl for
a drive in his cab, said girl having afterwards
left the vehicle and come to more or less grief
among certain rowdy youths with whom Sweet-
man was not in league and for whose actions he
was in no wise responsible? That famous drive
and its after-consequences couldn’t be undone
by the hideous tortures to which the cabman
was sentenced, and it can’t be undone now by
a further continuance of those tortures. More-
over, The Bulletin defies any sane person to
read the evidence in Sweetman’s case and
find there any trace of a crime which, as
the world goes, merited more than a month
or two’s imprisonment—let alone fourteen
years and two floggings. Sweetman was
not dealt with like Abigail, in cold blood;
he was tried while Botany - Bay populace
and press were alike blood - drunk. The
last heard of him is that, previous to his
alleged crime, he had suffered from a cranial
injury which, of late years, developed into
paralysis, and has lately necessitated the sawing-
away of a large sample of his skull. Yet he
remains in prison all the same, and no big poli-
tical deputation demands his release to garnish
Victoria’s birthday ; and even the Telegraph
never once gets up to point out that another
seven years’ torture applied to this miserable
paralytic won’t undo one solitary item of that
bygone horror for which he was never respon-
sible in any reasonable sense of the word.
Barrister Want, who, while in N.S. Wales
Assembly, always acted as if he regarded politics
as a joke and a plaything, announces his inten-
tion of retiring from the House, and in doing so
curses, not himself, but Parliament, which, he
holds, is utterly and hopelessly degenerate. As
an example of its windy impotence, he points
out that in 1883 it met on 59 days, and sat
419 hours, against 116 days and 1096 hours in
1893, and yet the business record of 1883 was
far the better of the two. Mr. Want is wise,
though not especially candid, in selecting 1883
for purposes of comparison. In that year the
Legislature held three sessions, almost the
whole twelve months being fooled away in
prorogations; and it accomplished practically
nothing. In 1881, which would have answered
equally well for purposes of comparison, the
House sat for nine months ; in 1884 it sat for
eleven months, and so on. Ever since there
was a Parliament in N.S. Wales, in fact, it
has kept up a steady average of about nine
months’ talk per annum —barring the one year
which Mr. Want picks out to prove that it is
the Assembly and not himself that is a failure.
The strike and lock-out of Sydney bootmakers
had at least a plain and intelligible issue. In
three of four factories the men struck because
non-union labour was employed; in 20 others
the masters locked them out in order to defeat
the strikers. Each side was perfectly within
its rights. On the one hand, union must be
general to be successful, and in view of the
constant encroachment of employers the con-
dition of the men can only be raised by union ;
on the other hand, the man who refuses to join
a union, and the employer who supports him,
exercise their legitimate individual freedom.
Democratic sympathy goes naturally with the
men in their endeavour to establish co-operative
workshops, which have in the boot-trade, where
duties' are definite and routine exact, a very
good chance of success, provided the manage-
ment is able and unhampered. The monopolist
employer only justifies himself by succeeding ;
the sooner success can be attained without him
the better it will be for the community and the
better the community will be.
The latest from Paraguay is that Lane, the
founder, dictator, apostle, organiser and great
panjandrum of New Australia, has “chucked
up ” the bulk of his followers and the original
settlement, and gone off, with 40 or 50 others,
to found a superlatively beautiful colony of
their own. The alleged reason of the seces-
sion is that Lane’s crowd feel the neces-
sity for a “ higher moral tone ” than that
of the other lot, and their parting is said
to have been “ perfectly friendly.” Con-
sidering, however, the enthusiastic manner in
which the bosses at this end have denied every
unpleasant report until it was impossible to
deny it any longer, the public opinion of their
veracity is at a low ebb, and recent events
don’t point towards much cordiality. It is
only a few days since Lane and his party
were, it would appear, enthusiastically heaved
out of office by a majority vote of the com-
munity, and the sudden departure which
followed looks as if the same majority had
afterwards harried them from the settlement.
The “ moral tone” story sounds like the snuffle
of a (lefeated person with a huge black
eye sitting on an ant-heap in the wilderness.
The next trouble will probably be a civil war
between the moral tone party and the crowd with
no moral tone, and The Bulletin backs the im-
moral lot for all it is worth. People of high
moral tone are seldom any good in a fight.
Meanwhile, negotiations, or bricks, or some-
thing like that, are passing between the
two factions, and the third faction—the lot
originally ejected—are prowling round in the
old famished way and waiting for some-
thing to happen. Also, the crowd at this
end, who have been issuing so many stories
about the beautiful unanimity of the settlement
ever since everybody who wasn’t unanimous
was fired out, now say, as usual, that they have
been convinced for a long time that the split
was ‘ ‘ both expedient and right. ” They are all
going to join the superior lot, which is a beau-
tiful example of egotism, and a first-class advt.
for their own moral tone.
The retirement of Mundella from the Rose-
bery Government deserves to be noted and
remembered by Victorian electors in particular.
A Judge dropped some mildly unfavourable re-
marks upon Mundella’s connection with the
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Co. and immediately
the solemn tribunal known as “British public
opinion ” decided that Mundella was unfit to
have a finger in the control of the Hempire.
Public opinion moves at the nod of Mrs.
Grundy, and these is no commanding place in
British politics for the man who has been
recently convicted of breaking Respectability’s
rnles. One sneers a little at the awful assump-
tion of virtue by Mundella’s press detractors,
one snorts at the popular indignation levelled
against the English legislator whose little sin has
found him out. Possibly the sin in this case
was very small indeed. Yet there is no deny-
ing that hypocrisy makes for purity by main-
taining a sham ideal of Ministerial honour. It
is a healthy kind of humbug which decrees the
casting forth of a Mundella, merely because
he had admitted his knowledge that he was
“directing” a scandalous!} 7 -managed company
by selling his shares on the quiet. Compare
this incident with the position of affairs in
Victoria, where public opinion is hopelessly
tainted, where a James Munro and a Graham
Berry held high positions in defiance of their
busted bank associations, and a James Balfour
still acts as mouthpiece for the blessed council
of churches ! Which political system is the
better for a country —the system of sacrificing
men to an extravagant pretence of national
honour, or that of abandoning every pretence
to national honour for the sake of a few old
identities ?

N.S.W. Full Court decided last week that a
publican has a lien on a lodger’s goods for credit
given, and that the lodger who takes away his
goods without paying his account steals from
the lienor. Chief Justice Darley, who had
tried a case involving this issue at Dubbo, re-
mitted the point to the Full Court. Judge
Innes, in concurring with Judge Windeyer,
who delivered the judgment, admitted that he
did so with much doubt; and Judge Foster,
also concurring, said he would not sanction hav-
ing a man arrested for leaving a hotel without
paying his bill. But, if he even takes a carpet
bag with him, he commits a theft against the
lienor, according to that judgment, and why
shall the publican be deprived of his right to
have him arrested ?
Westralia also is afflicted with the judge-
disease. At Perth Supreme Court, April 4th,
a man was charged with defrauding the Cus-
toms, and acquitted, to the obvious displeasure
of the presiding Judge, who, “ to express his
opinion of the verdict given,” refused bail—
though defendant had been out on bail for
three months awaiting trial! Two days later
he was arraigned on another count, and found
guilty. The Judge said that the evidence in
the first case was equally clear, and “he must
take that case into consideration in estimating
the penalty.” Then he fined prisoner £750.
On the presumption that the Judge held the
guilt in each case equal, defendant was thus
called upon to pay £375 for the privilege of
being declared innocent of the first charge
brought against him. Where does the jury
come in here ? Plainly, if the J udge can go
behind the verdict, and actually fine a man for
being acquitted, the jury isn’t worth a straw.
“ Divorce,” according to Victorian Chief
Justice Madden, “ should only be for meri-
torious people who have, for a long time,
endured wretchedness and misery.” The Bul-
letin invites the C.J. to stop just here and
look hard at his own logic. When he divorces
the meritorious person who has endured long
wretchedness and misery, doesn’t he also
divorce the unmeritorious person who caused
the wretchedness ? And is it not quite possible
that the latter individual gets as mucli satis-
faction out of the divorce as the former ?
Therefore does Mr. Madden want to fix
up a scheme whereby the plaintiff is divorced
while the defendant isn’t —which is much
the same as arranging a plan whereby two
people will stand near each other, only one
of them will be nearer than the other one—or
what does he propose ? Further, why should
it be necessary to endure wretchedness and
misery for a long time before the law inter-
venes ? Life is tolerably short altogether, and
when a long hunk of wretchedness is cut out
of it while qualifying for a separation, there
isn’t much time left to enjoy the blessed relief
which comes after. Also, why should the
law release only the meritorious person and
leave the person who wasn’t meritorious
in all her wretchedness, seeing that it professes
to protect all persons alike regardless of char-
acter ? And, in any case, if plaintiff isn’t
meritorious, there is always the possibility that
it is the long “wretchedness and misery”
which has reduced her to that condition, and if
the said wretchedness is lifted off she may
improve in course of time. The Bulletin’s
learned brother Madden proposes, in short,
only to divorce the person who suffers the
misery hereinbefore alluded to if she is
meritorious under conditions which would
undermine almost anybody’s merit, but he is
quite willing to divorce the person who causes
the misery whether he is meritorious or not.
He is profoundly anxious about the character
of the under dog in the scrimmage, but the
character of the upper one doesn’t concern him.
Mr. Madden’s law may be good—most probably
it is—but his loose eloquence is a dreadful thing
to read or to listen to.
A “silvertail” Women’s Franchise League
has now been formed in Brisbane, the members
whereof visibly shudder when the original
vulgar league is incautiously mentioned. The
cause in Brisbane, however, has received a bad
knock from Mrs. T. W. Foot, the poetess,
about the best representative of female culture
in Queensland. Mrs. Foot has actually said
that she doesn’t want a vote, and she thought
that a woman had quite enough to do in mind-
ing her home and family without joining the
ranks of political blatherskites. On the point
that women were wanted to improve the
moral tone of politics, she told the hor-
rified suffragists that male politicians were
moral enough, and if they weren’t immoral
men were no good as rulers, anyhow. Then she
quoted Scripture to show that Eli, a highly
moral man, had lost the ark of the Lord —the
worst calamity which ever befell Israel; while
David, a highly immoral man, made an excel-
lent king. Then she went to history, and
showed that Charles I. was moral, and yet it
wasn’t safe to leave him his head. And she
wound up with a general panegyric on men;,
whom she declared were very good to women,
and if they weren’t whose fault was it ?
whereat the assembled suffragists dispersed
howling.

The utter incompetence of the first generation
of Australian “ statesmen ” is glaringly shown
by the way in which they have muddled the
land question. With a country landlordless,
with a free hand for political agrarian wisdom,
with the knowledge that haphazard aggression
methods have ruined empires, with the curse
of English and Irish landlordism before their
©yes, they have deliberately woven the web of
private ownership over the land, until the
only brisk business of the country is the dis-
traint auction-room, and the mark of the beast
in the shape of “ ejectment ” notices is as com-
mon on the doors as the “ board and lodging ”
cards in the windows. The fatuity of their
action is remarkably shown in the case of C. G.
Duffy, a whilom rebel against the curse of
English landlordism in Ireland, and the first to
sow the dragon seed of Australian landlordism
n Victoria. One is inclined to think that
inch men, deliberately plotting to secure the
guin of their country, looked over lazar-houses,
a nd, seizing with avidity the most hideous
c ancer they could see, inoculated our young
oountry with the landlord system.

The British nation is weeping tears of joy at a
new lie now going the round of the “ loyal ”
papers:—
One day while walking with one of her ladies in the
quiet lanes near Sandringham the Princess of Wales met
an old woman weeping bitterly and tottering under a load
of packages. On inquiry it appeared that she was a
carrier, and made her living by shopping and doing
errands in the market-town for the country people. “ But
the weight is too heavy at your age,” said the Princess.
“ Yes. You’re right, ma’am. I’ll have to give it up, and
if I give it up, I’ll starve. Jack carried them for me—my
boy, ma’am." ‘‘And where is he now? ” “Jack! He’s
dead 1 Oh, he’s dead! ” the old woman cried wildly.
The Princess, without a word, hurried on, drawing her
veil over her face to hide her tears. A few days later a
neat little cart with a stout donkey were brought to the
old carrier’s door. She now travels with them to and fro,
making a comfortable living, and never has been told the
rank of the friend who has tried to make her life easier
(or the sake of her dead boy.
This is the sort of thing by which the British
monarchy, like all other monarchies, is shored
up. Perhaps it is true, perhaps it isn’t; most
likely it isn’t. Anyhow, it does no particular
credit to the Princess, and is, if anything, an
argument for the abolition rather than for the
perpetuation of lords, kings, queens and
princesses, who, like the fuli-phylacteried and
well-advertised Pharisees of old, make their
pompous offerings. Reason agrees with re-
ligious-sentiment in singling out as the one
woman for praise, the widow who gives the
mite representing a real sacrifice. If the
Princess of Wales ever did give any old woman
“ a neat little cart with a stout donkey,” she
did not thereby abridge her own comforts and
pleasures by one hairbreadth ; and, therefore,
in the eye of God as of _ democracy, her gift
wasn’t worth talking about in the papers.

The N.S.W. Industrial School authorities
are, it is stated, quite sure that the little girl on
whom the recent mysterious stone-throwing at
Enmorehas been fastened is not the true culprit.
She has, as per her confession, had her little
innings of pebble-throwing; but she, it is almost
certain, did not commence the business until the
windows of the house had all been broken by
someone else, and she could not possibly have
thrown the heavy stones. This opinion is strongly
concurred in by an eminent surgeon who has
examined the girl.
The S.M. Herald objects, in the strongest
Lading article which it knows how to write, to
a bill introduced into N.S.W. Assembly by
Dowel, M.L A. The object of this measure is to
alter that section of the criminal law under which
Montgomery and Williams are to be hanged for
an assault on the police, and the Herald holds
that the occasion is “ inopportune,” and that if
Dowel wants the law altered he should wait until
these men are hanged. Otherwise, his conduct
is unjustifiable “interference,” and looks as if
it was meant to save the condemned burglars from
the gallows. How would the same logic have
looked if applied to Dibbs’ Bank Issue Bill, sup-
ported by the S.M. Herald, which was rushed
through in the days of the panic? If Dibbs
waited the law altered on that famous occasion
he should have waited until the four remaining
banks burst and had their assets liquidated, like
ordinary insolvents; before moving. As it
was, his conduct was an unjustifiable inter-
ference, and looked as if it was intended
to save the banks from destruction. In each
Instance it was proposed to alter the law hurriedly
to fit one particularly case; the Herald approves of
this most cordially in the case of the bank, and yet
it disapproves cordially in thecase of the burglars.*
The principle is exactly the same, the only differ-
ence is that the Herald adores Property and sets
no value on the lives of common people.

Alexandra Fitzgerald’s Cirous.
A faint odour of tired-out cynicism per-
vades Sydney Lyceum where “Lady Winder-
mere’s Fan ” is in possession. Oscar Wilde’s
famous play is a curious and lop-sided production.
His characters are mostly of high rank; among 14
people—leaving out a maid and two flunkeys—
there are three lords, five assorted “ladies,” and
one duchess. One young lord and his very young
countess are fresh and good and pure of soul; the
rest of the crowd are the most exhausted-looking,
battered, and worn-out lot on record. They lie
round wearily amid much costly furniture, smoking
cigarettesand blastingotherpeople’scharacters, and
uttering that kind of morally-diseased wit which is
based on the assumption that everybody is tired
and dirty of soul. When the author gets away
from this one assumption his wit is not above the
average ; when he is on his favourite topic he is
brilliant. Still there is nothing visibly immoral
about his dialogue; it is merely filled with the
flavour of satiety and disgust, and old cigar-
smoke, and pervaded with very, very old young
men, who look boredly at the world through one
weary eye-glass in a club. And seeing that all his
characters, barring the pair above-mentioned, have
got this disease so badly, the assumption is that
these two will also get it when they grow older.
This considerably spoils the interest.
* * •
Oscar Wilde’s dialogue being all built on
the one dismal story that all men and women are
hopelessly immoral, his plot naturally follows the
same lines. The one good woman in the play
elopes at two o’clock a.m.—all the other women
seem to have eloped, more or less, before the
story opened—but is followed and saved from
destruction by an abandoned female of twenty
years’ experience. This incident shows the neces-
sity for always keeping an abandoned female on
the premises—no woman who hadn’t often eloped
herself would know exactly the right thing to do
at such a crisis, or would know how to do it in
the small hours of the morning. Also, the awful
facility with which the only good woman of the
crowd goes wrong at the shortest notice is possibly
intended to show that every woman ought to go
wrong when she is very young, so that she will
know how not to do it afterwards. It further sug-
gests, in a vague, irrelevant fashion, that it must
be a depressing and unwholesome thing to be the
author’s wife—unless the faint effluvium of decay
about his soul is only a laborious pretence and not
a reality.
• • •
There are only three characters of any
real importance in the play, and of these Tither-
adge, as Lord Windermere, has a commonplace
part, and is chiefly necessary that he may draw
cheques for the abandoned female. The aban-
doned female, by-the-way, gets through a heap
of money. Lady Windermere is the best piece
of work Miss Grace Noble has yet done in
Sydney. Her representation of the pretty,
pathetic little wife whose ideal has fallen on her
head, is almost perfect, and when she rises in
insurrection against the abandoned female she
exhibits a tragic intensity of which no one has
hitherto suspected her. Mrs. Brough, as the
female aforesaid, is excellent all through, barring
the fact that her evening-dress is of a dreadfully
glaring character which doesn’t agree with her
alleged desire to get back to respectability; and
Cecil Ward is a nice, beautiful, refreshing idiot,
who is gladdening to the soul. The other characters
—with the exception of Mr. Renouf, a mild
production with no especial features—are on hand
solely that they may get clever epigrams off their
chests, and they fill their purpose satisfactorily.
** * &
The “ King of Crime ” still keeps a paying
connection at Her Majesty’s, and other shows are
much as reported in last issue. Owing to Her
Gracious having arranged to have a birthday this
week a small theatrical boom will probably even-
tuate, but at time of writing no aggravated
symptoms have supervened.
** * *
Bland Holt’s recent importations from
Drury Lane are put into the shade by “ A Life of
Pleasure.” This is a strong, compact story, as
melodramas go, whilst its spectacular merits are
striking even on the limited stage of Melbourne
Princess’. The slaughter of a humorous Burmese
army by British heroes is not impressive, but the
military incidents appertaining to that inglorious
massacre have a natural air which doesn’t belong
to the average stage-soldier in mock combat and a
misfit uniform. A notion of active service is ex-
pressed upon an Australian stage for the first
time ; also the piece is well played. Sass, for the
nonce, is the vile seducer who causes Miss Watson
to clothe herself in crimson and prepare for a
desperate plunge into impropriety. Fortun-
ately she changes her mind and takes poison
by mistake, after which she recovers her
health and probably turns religious. The dis-
reputable Sass suicides on his wedding - night
to the joy of Miss Harrie Ireland, who dis-
covers her husband’s true character before the
honeymoon has begun. Sass, however, does not
relinquish his honeymoon without a struggle, and
invades the dressing-room of his bride at mid-
night ; but the lady screams for timely aid in a
manner not at all in accordance with the gospel of
Judge Madden. Hence arises the delicate
question as to whether Madden would sympathise
with the suicide of Sass under these circumstances.
The chief honours fall to the artists named, but
others are equally satisfactory. “A Life of
Pleasure ” is fated to enjoy distinguished patron-
age, and Premier Patterson will move a national
vote of thanks to the brigade of Indian hawkers
who assist Bland Holt to exterminate the dacoits.
“ Friends,” at the Bijou, delighted a poor
attendance on Saturday. Eulogy may be boiled
down to the simple statement that “Friends,” by
Garner’s Co., surpasses the Brough-Boucicault
performance of “ Peril.” This is high praise, and
if so excellent an entertainment is to waste its
sweetness upon empty benches the management
had better abandon high-class comedy forthwith.
Harwood and Annie Taylor are specially happy in
their parts, but it comes as a surprise to find
Edith Blande and Bentley playing out of their
usual lines with so much brightness and ease. The
comedy is prettily staged and deserves to be hand-
somely acknowledged by a degenerate public.
** * *
The experiment of Fitzgeralds’ circus, at
Melbourne Alexandra, promises well. On Satur-
day the big house was full, and pony Commodore
was observed to wink his tail as though he
reckoned that his proprietors had struck another
rich patch. Plainly, more things can be done
with a ring on the stage than are dreamed of in
ordinary circus-tent philosophy. The ring has
been adroitly environed with bits of woodland
glade, and the beasts of prey form a choice zoo-
logical background. Also, the limelight man
contributes largely to the general success by pro-
ducing blue lions with yellow tails, and other
Jim-Jam effects. This affords but little pleasure
to the animals, and the lion who finds himself
turning green is apt to smile bitterly with the top
half of his countenance while a gigantic frown
corrugates the bottom half from N.E. to S.W.,
and makes everybody hope the management will
see its way to advertise four rounds between the
lion and the limelight man as a grand climax to
the show.
** * *
The Sapio-Urso concerts at Melb. Town
Hall have met thus far with far less financial suc-
cess than they deserve, but jpiite as much of it as
could be expected, considering that the business-
manager is a Frenchman who knows naught about
the local “ropes.” Madame Camilla IJrso is a
rare violinist, a great artist, and when she pro-
duces real music her hearers are held enthralled.
Unhappily, it seems necessary for all gifted per-
formers on the violin to prove their technical skill
to other violinists by a series of marvellous scrapes,
in which there is no real meaning or charm for the
common ear and heart of humanity. This com-
plaint holds good against every notable lady-
fiddler. There are times when tjiey torture the
catgut to give forth horrible noises to the glory of
the purely mechanical side of their art. No such
noises can be ascribed to Madame De Vere Sapio,
the soprano singer of this very select combination.
One may say of Madame Sapio that she accomp-
lishes almost as much at the finest vocalists, only
she doesn’t do it so enchantingly. Her voice is
strong and flexible, and highly-trained, but there
seems to be a want of the smoothness which dis-
tinguishes the Pattis of this world. Madame has
a bigger voice than Outtioa’s, but it is, perhaps,
not quite as even, or as pure, or as something to
the same effect. Nevertheless, she is a delightful
singer, and when she warbles to Urso’s accompani-
ment the result is akin to Paradise on a fine day.
A well-rehearsed orchestra of 45 fills in the rest of
the concert, and some pretty little compositions by
Signor Sapio obtain the usual encore.

At Melb. Royal they found “The Circus

na-Pogue.” Probably the season will conclude

** * *
The jockey who carefully tumbles into the
water-jump, in “The Prodigal Daughter,” gets
paid a few shillings extra per week. On the first
night, at Melb. Princess, the boy nervously fell
off his horse before he reached the little tank, soi
fearing that he might have to forfeit his “bit of
bunce,” he crawled under the hurdle and gave
himself a soaking in the mimic brook. Talking of
this—when Dr. Carver was playing at the Alex-
andra they had to get a super for the job of
jumping from a considerable height into the big
tank. A _ likely-looking young applicant was
selected without any mention of terms. When
Dampier suggested “£2 a week” the man
couldn’t articulate a word for several seconds.
Joy had made him dumb. Then he turned as pale
as a sheet and gurgled, “God bless you! sir.”
He would gladly have dropped twice the distance
for half the money.
** * «
Four parsons, of Warrnambool (Yic.),
waited upon the local mayor recently to protest
against the desecration of the Town Hall by a
Sunday sacred-concert company. One of the
reverend quartette objected to the concert because
it was “essentially a commercial undertaking,”
and as such was unfit to take place on the pro-
perty of the burgesses. The other three com-
plainants stated that their spirits were chiefly
bruised by the idea of a charge being made for
admission. Then the mayor reminded them that
clergymen had used the same building for Sunday
services, with a view to taking up a collection,
and to settle the difficulty he would request the
concert company to run their commercial enter-
prise on church lines. As the parsons had now
nothing left to complain about they sorrowfully
withdrew, praying inwardly that Christians would
rush the seating accommodation, and reject the
offertory plate with scorn.

Ethel Haydon has the ring of passion in her lower
speaking tunes, whereas her joyous, girly innocence is
expressed in high notes, which, though musical enough
are hard and unfeeling. Melbourne sassiety of any grade
is a bad school for the voice.
That a Premier should have personal power to determine
to what use a theatre, or other licensed house, should be
put on Sunday nights is just as tyrannous as that he
should have power to say what should be printed in a
newspaper or what said from a hustings. There should
be a definite law on the subject enforceable, like the dog.
act, at the police-court.

Jenny Hill, the goddess of the London music-halls
W d ®r2 ne A f^ pe ? ra ?? e recen J t . 1 J r on the stage at Johannes-
burg (b. Africa), It was a dismal occasion. The goddess
has suddenly and unaccountably crumbled away. A few
months ago she was young and giddy and gushful; then
like Clark Russel s ‘ Frozen Pirate,” she seems to have
fallen oyer something and become 150 years old almost on
the instant. She had no middle age to let her down
gently; she simply woke to find herself a haggard old
child of no particular date, but with a general aspect of
having been left behind by the Romans. The Johannes,
burg audience found her a pallid, bony, despondent
wreck. She couldn t sing-, or dance, or even stand, but
she sat in a big chair, and whispered a feeble, ramblino-
oration about things in general, while the cockney part of
the crowd cheered and wept by turns. Then the wreck
was helped away, leaving behind a faint flavour of pathos
and a vague idea that Mrs. Skewton had arisen from the
grave. It is a solemn thing to grow old under any circum-
stances, but to grow old suddenly in tights must be a
ghastly event.
John Bull doesn’t enjoy a monopoly of patriotic “blow.”
Pans just now is exulting over a drama called “ The
Treasure of the Rajahs,” depicting the defeat of a British
army in India by a limited number of Frenchmen One
heroic son of Gaul captures a British battalion by merely
pointing a revolver at the colonel’s head, after which the
low comedian comes along and blows his nose upon the
inglorious Union Jack. At another theatre the British
navy is nightly exterminated by one small French
schooner, and the gem of the piece is the scene in which
a truculent small boy kicks an abnormally large British
sailor until he howls for mercy.

Ethereal sprite, deliojous fay,
Unsubstantial Philomel,
Do not on my homage frown.
Be no chary thistledown !
Smile upon your sentinel ;
Pluck me, rose-bud, while you may!
Tender, slender, timorous thing—
From whose throat, all virginal,
Float in luscious melody
Dulcet wood-notes, coy yet free
Take my love for coronal;
Sit upon my knee and sing 1
Fly to me on wings of light,
Tiny, tricksy Ariel 1
While I, raptured in a kiss,
Stagger ’neath my weight of bliss.
O’er the fields of asphodel;
Delicious fay, ethereal sprite 1

A Yankee manager, realising that many posiibie lady
patrons are kept away by the baby, proposes to attach a
nursery to his theatre. Each mother will receive a ticket
for her infant when she leaves it; offspring returned on
production of voucher after the performance. Competent
nurses will be engaged, and every possible effort made to
prevent the children getting mixed.
Maori girls have keen wits. When Major Dane, the
Yankee lecturer, was doing the hot lake district and
went to see Tikitere, the most wonderful of all the volcanic
curiosities, Murita, prettiest of pretty Maori girl guides
was reserved for the party. When they arrived at
Tikitere the Major’s first expression, “Nothing to Yellow-
stone I’’ did not over-please Murita. She showed him
the most wonderful mud-holes, geysers and coloured
springs, but he kept repeating, “Nothing to Yellow-
stone 1” This went on for about an hour until Murita
asked the Major to feel how light was a great piece of
pumice-stone stuck in the bank just off the track
Suspecting no jokes from a bare-legged Maori girl the
scornful lecturer lifted it off. The moment he did so a
startling report was heard and the Major’s beautiful
tourist suit was smothered in smoking, stinking yellow
mud. “Good Heaven ! ” he yelled, “what do you call
this ? “ That’s Yellowstone, I think," quietly replied
Murita.
An importunate dead-head once begged Edward Terry for
an order. Terry pretended that he had no card, but
generously wrote an “open sesame” on the order-hunter’s
shirt-cuff. The door-keeper was “put up” to the trick
and insisted upon the presenter giving up his wrist-band
as a voucher. It now figures in “ Goody’s ” collection of
theatrical bric-a-brac.

It has always been said, and believed, that Hawtrey
cleared over £BO,OOO by the "Private Secretary,” which
he promptly "blewed” on the turf. In his late bank-
ruptcy examination ho swore that £lO,OOO represented his
trains over that piece. A wide difference—for Thornton
made more than this in Australia ! Hawtrey also stated
that his trains during the last eight years were £14,000,
whereas his losses on theatrical speculations during the
game time were over £24,000,
Critic Archer on “The Bauble Shop”;—"The whole
conception and execution of the play is incurably naif
I feel bound to protest against Mr. Jones’s notions of
human nature. From the middle of the second act on-
wards a voice within me keeps on crying out in spite of
myself: ‘Nol no! things do not happen like this ! These
are not men and women, but sentimental situation-
makers 1 Theatrical 1 false, false, false 1 and not
very cleverly false either.'” Which is a glorious
example of the awful oocksureness of an inflated critic.
“ Things do not happen like this,” says Archer’s “ inward
voice.” A very moderate study of the papers ought to
convince Archer that in real life things happen anyway or
every way, and that the utter unaccountableness of every-
body makes it absolutely impossible for any one to say
how things ought to happen.
