846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
May 22, 1894.
My Dbab Moobabinda—
If it hadn’t been for the peacock gowning of
the Second Mrs. Tanqueray, “ Lady Winder-
mere’s Fan” would certainly have led the dress-
ing of the present B. and B. season at Sydney
Lyceum, and a very good thing, too, for the
“Fan” doesn’t absorb your attention so com-
pletely that you don’t notice what the women
have on. Some of the “ new ” dramatists take as
much dressing as a French salad. It seems to me
that a modish couturiere is just as solemnly im-
portant a factor in the society play-up-to-date as
the stage carpenter is in melodrama.
Quite the most attractive confection (though
strictly on the safe side of conventional effect;
there are few pioneers in the paths of daring
colour-contrasts) was an albescent robe worn by
Lady Windermere (Grace Noble) in the second
and third acts—a dress of white satin spangled
with silver jaunty paniers and full train
of white satin with a quilted lining. The bodice
was girdled about with silver, and The Fan of
soft white ostrich completed the picture. By the
way, a puff of Miss Noble’s hair came down in the
matrimonial skirmish of the first act, and she
stopped to put it up, though the scene would have
gained much in realism had the heroine played it
out with her wool in her teeth. Not many women
would marshal their hairpins in the exigencies of
the first connubial quarrel, it seems to me.
And, talking of Lady W., why didn’t Miss
Noble wear a wedding-ring ? In the melodrama,
you know, the heavy father in search of his erring
che-yild always explores her left hand to slow
music when he finds her in St. John’s Wood, but
in the “Fan” we have a high-toned heroine guilt-
less of the badge. .
In the middle acts, Mrs. Brough, in blue-corded
silk cut like a pinafore and trimmed with silver
and a court train edged with blue fur, the whole
toilet aggravated by a thing like a shaving-brush
in her coiffure —well, she didn’t please me a bit.
Although there are said to be no old women in
London society, still Mrs. Erlynn was altogether
too fresh for a lady who was “29 with pink shades
—3O without ”on her own admission. A coarser
and more visible fresco and a wig stiff with gold
wash would have been nearer the author but,
then, Mrs. Brough is as wedded to her own hair
as Sarah Bernhardt, though the “Divine” does
occasionally ring the changes among her dye-
bottles and lias been almost every colour but pie-
bald. In the last act, Mrs. Brough’s pale grey-
green rep silk with jaunty jacket of emerald green
velvet and lashings of steel passementerie, small
bonnet en suite, was as effective as Miss Noble s
fluttering white muslin was the other adjective.
There is altogether too much fleeciness about
Lady Windermere. An ' actress-, who is white
three acts running deserves to be turned to a pillar
of salt. "
Titheradge, as Lord Windermere, in a curry-
coloured and curly wig, reminded me of how a
certain Sydney dancing-master would look if he
powdered his hair with ginger. And, talking
about the he-actors, is it the latest London snip
for men to put on their white gloves after they
have shaken hands with their hostess? At the
ball almost every man who came in dragged his
•gloves on in the middle of the reception-room, and
I would like to know whether it’s the caper in
“town” or merely a stage version of the glove
question adopted to.give the actor-man something
to do with his paws ?
The pr<smi6re of the “Fan ” acquired a more or
less society interest from the presence of an
unbroken draft of damsels sent on as ‘ guests, to
judge of their promise by B. and 8., who seek an
ingenue of their own growing and are becoming
convinced that it isn’t necessary to import long-
legged English talent at a high figure just to wear
simple frocks and look impossibly innocent in the
unearthly style of ing&iuedom. Miss Dudlay
(she travelled hitherwards as Miss Ward) is the
latest juvenile forwarded to the care of B. and 8.,
and a damsel more like a baa-lamb on stilts it
would be difficult to imagine. Even in a ball-
gown inches too short in the front she didn t look
ingenuous—only absurd ; and it seems lucky that
Brenda Gibson hadn’t sailed for Ome, as she
threatened, and is still handy as a juvenile if the
worst comes to the worst. Imagination reels at
the thought of Miss Dudlay as the short-skirted
tomboy in “Niobe”; still, for all the world
knows, she may be a Sara Bernhardt.
Among the lady-guests aforesaid, on trial before
preparation, in a manner of speaking, the pretty
daughter of a well-known Sydney doctor pervaded
the ball-room scene in a fluffy pink frock, and
with a graceful ease born of much society experi-
ence. She looked the most promising of the
muster, to my eye, though none of the B. and B.
"colts” shaped extra badly as the guests of Lady
Windermere.
People may argue that the “ Fan” is improbable
on the ground that a man doesn’t want to marry
his grandmother, and that even a jealous wife
wouldn’t get on edge over a woman literally old
enough to be her mother. But experience teaches
us that in life few things are impossible. It
happened, not an age ago, in one of the wretched
kawlinies, that a man introduced a he-friend to
his blushing bride-elect, after which the friend
faded away for a year or so, and when he turned
up again he found the lover a Benedict in decent
sequence.
But the wife puzzled him. Her features,
colouring and hair resembled those of the fiancee,
but making full allowance for the expansiveness
of matrimony and the carking cares of being one
flesh, there isn’t enough “ stingo ” in two years of
it to transform a willowy maiden into a well-
seasoned-looking woman, rather worn round the
edges, and he, puzzled, sought an explanation.
Like all explanations it was the simplest thing in
the world. The girl had turned Tarter after
marriage, and Benedict had cut adrift from her
and married her ma.
A feature of “ Lady Windermere’s Fan ” was
that, in one scene, nearly all the principals carried
swell posies. Now, I wonder is this the fore-
runner of a more open and candid policy in regard
to floral “ managerial ?” Will they come on
frankly from behind in future, and be no more
guilelessly hauled over the floats?
I have received a musical composition called
“ United Australia,” descriptive waltz by A.
Hermes—with music for oboes, horns and other
wild instruments, and words here and there to
keep the enthusiasm up to concert-pitch. There
is much noise and Chinese opera about the
patriotic fantasia, but it has very little merit. It
is a port of battle-of-Prague-and-water, with the
cries of the wounded boiled down to the shrieks
of one gouty invalid.
From a feminine aspect the new dance (it comes
ball-marked from ’Ome) is really taking. There
are airy flights in it and seductively waving arms,
which, however, make one apprehensive that if
smart society takes to practising it outside cafes
by gas-light our up-to-date belles will resemble the
weird vision Tam O’Shanter saw.
Trinity Church, Kelso, the oldest ecclesiastical
building over the Blue Mountains, and built at
Kelso instead of Bathurst at a time when the
“ free and bond ” were divided by the Macquarie
River, the “free” occupying the Kelso, and the
“ bond ” the Bathurst side of the stream—was the
scene of an interesting wedding last week, when
Lieutenant Abdy, R.N., took unto himself Edith
Mary, third daughter of George Lee, M.L.C.
This makes the third generation married in the old
church, the bride’s father and mother, and grand-
father and grandmother having been wedded in
the same unaltered building. Many interesting
reminiscences hang round the spot, and many
pioneers of the plains rest in its old-fashioned
cemetery. In the Lee vaults rests “Ted” Lee,
Crown-prosecutor, sportsman, and generally good
fellow, who died somewhat young, after a merry
innings.
There was not an embarrassment of fine feathers
at the Factory Girls’ ball. Lady Duff, in black
silk and jet draped dowager-wise, with cream-lace,
was half hidden by a committee-bouquet. Mrs.
Edward Hutton in opaline shades of pale-green
and heliotrope, etherealised with cobwebby old
lace and a handsome display of diamonds, must be
regarded as the top-weight of fashion on this occa-
sion among the plain dressers.
Among the poudrd people, Mrs. Prendergast, in
ivory-satin with pale-pink roses, diamonds and
suggestion of grey velvet on the bodice, presented
the most refined ensemble, but ah the powdered
brigade had not been so light on the cosmetics by
a long, long way. How should we blame the im-
pressionists of the ballet when belledom en mas-
querade, which ought to know better artistically,
gets its face up as if the laws of beauty were two
lovely black eyes and sanguineous apoplexy?
Owing to there being so many fancy sets, every
man who had a brass button or a tinsel collar-
band among his assets came out in uniform—
down to the spurs in the case of the military
officers, some of whom were wedged so firmly into
their garments that they looked as if they had been
hard-boiled in their wearing apparel. I really
think, do you know, that the pert bob-tailed shell-
jacket ought not to be worn without reservations.
When a son of Mars shows a tendency to outpace
the weighing-machines it appears to me to be
about time that warrior left off figuring in a red
Eton jacket. Under such circumstances the
temptation to stick pins into the military isnt
wholly monopolised by the calves of the Highland
regiments. “ Uncle,” in a red-shell jacket (it
suits his style of physique), headed the warriors,
who were scattered over the ballroom with the
sunsetty effect of a pint of prawns. For orna-
mental purposes, one plain rank-and-file soldier
will fill the eye more than half-a-dozen admirals.
Apropos of hops, owing to a printer’s error, it
appeared last week that the Fresh Air League
ball is to be on 26th prox., whereas the ball in aid
of that most deserving charity, the Tempo refuge,
dates its tickets (which are selling like rockets, by
the way) for the 26th. On 21st prox., a grand
public ball, in aid of Sydney University Sports
Union, takes place in Sydney Town Hall, under
distinguished patronage ; and, on the 14th June,
a re-union in aid of the Randwick R.C. Church
Fund holds the fort at Paddington Town Hall,
which, as was the case last season, is much to tha
fore as a fashionable ball-room.
“ Ouida ” depicts the English lord as a wicked
Sybarite who drops diamonds into the champagne
of his lady friends and lives in reprehensible mag-
nificenoe. But then he is, after all, the sort or
duke one reads about—he doesn’t often leave nx»
own country, apparently. Anyhow, a lord on tno
globe-trot doesn’t travel de luxe —he does most ot
it on the moral support of bis title, so to speak.
Only the other day a society girl, who had been
one of a party invited to “ mangle garbage witn
a real live lord (he has left us, by the way),
unfolded her dire disappointment. She had go ll ®
expecting something epicurean and original, but,
alas ! this ineffective lordling dropped no pearls m
their coffee-cups—simply sooled them on to a good
square meal with ordinary trimmings, and looked
quite pleased with himself. .And not only this
recent lordling, but nearly all itinerant aristocrats
cut things pretty fine in the hospitality way in this
tinpot country. If “ Ouida ” wants to study the
reverse of her prodigal peers, Australia s her
workshop, it seems to me.
Madame Belle Cole, the third deep-0 warbler
who has tested the weight-carrying capacities of
Australian concert platforms in the last two years,
is a queen-like —Queen Viotoria-like artist with
plenty of lung-space for her well-oiled vocal organ,
and not a bad notion of how to dress herself
either. To the exceedingly plump, few garments
are possible, and Belle Cole (as I saw her) in white
brocade slashed with dark-green velvet on the
skirt and a good show of diamonds, looked as
undulating as one could reasonably expect. A
contralto voice appears to be as antagonistic to
willowy outlines as a seat in the Upper House.
By the way, the second Cole concert in Sydney
Town Hall was advertised as a V.R. night.
Governor and Lady Duff had _ promised to be
present, but a cluster of cane furniture on a square,
like a Moslem’s prayer-party high and dry in the
middle of the floor space, was all that told of the
expected presence of vice-royalty. However, the
charming major-generaless, in a warlike scarlet
opera-cloak, lent tone to the reserved seats and
made an excellent substitute.
To Madame’s rich and friendly voice I give my
almost complete approval. I don’t say I would
rush through the rain without any goloshes to hear
her sing the “Lost Chord,” which she warbles
with her head on one side and far too much vocal
colour, but in any music where a luxuriance of
feeling almost verging on the melodramatic is
necessary, Madame Belle Cole is ahead of the
field. And you like her for the way she effaces
herself. Most much-puffed artists look so hideously
conscious of the beautiful language used about
them in the papers that you quite forget what
they’re singing about. However, despite all
these attractions, Sydney hadn’t commenced to
tear its gardens up by the roots and hurl them at
the feet of Madame or any of her co. “at time of
going to press.” I never saw so flowerless an
entertainment as the second concert.
Of the Cole company Emily Spada has a fruity
soprano very artistically handled, but without
much dignity of tone. I heard Miss S. sing
“Angels Ever Bright and Fair” as if she was
winking at an archangel, though towards the end
she remembered to be more devout. Mr. New-
bury, the robust tenor, looks like Deslouis on the
platform and sings with baritone force and fervour
but a clogged articulation as if he rolled the
words under his tongue ; and basso Magrath ha.s a
rich but jarring voice which impresses you with
the notion that it will loosen his front teeth some
day_it certainly shakes his music-sheet. With a
more tranquil production and a less fervent belief
in himself Mr. Magrath would shine in any com-
pany. The remaining member of the co. is the
blonde Mdlle, Murkens whose baby-dimpled elbow
best excuses her appearance as a professional
violiniste.
The amusing feature of the Factory Girl’s Club
ball, at Sydney Town Hall the other evening, was
the frantic way in which people who had the
foggiest connection with the management scorned
plain evening dress, and grabbed a May-pole, or
tied a 0.M.G.-looking ribbon round their torsos,
or merely agitated the flour-dredge over their
devoted heads—anything to show that they were
the kernel of the show, so to speak. One com-
mittee youth had a wisp of green and yellow paper
tied round his arm to distinguish him from the
ordinary simple-minded visitor, the green and
yellow tissue aforesaid coming off one of the
“Lampshade” fancy dresses—French fowl-like
confections of green and yellow (as if woman’s
next idea was to reconcile Home Rule with the
L.0.L.), made up of crinkled paper frills in the
two colours, and meant to be rendered dazzling
by a head-dress of electric points. But the lamps
only worked successfully in one instance, tho’
coatless electricians lurked in the corridors and
tried to renew exhausted batteries, with the effect
of Howard Vernon in the “ Mountebanks ” wind-
ing Ophelia up.
By such means as these the society people who
foster-mothered the G.F. ball made themselves
into a pleasing masquerade for the ordinary, open-
mouthed onlooker, and caused the evening to pass
like a pantomime to the galleryites, I rather
thought the whole ball played to the gallery. The
first sign of it was the Swedish danoe performed
by the full strength of the crime de la crime, while
the others looked on. As I hear, by the way, that
this aforementioned dance—a good deal like Sir
Roger de Ooverley, with aggravations—is going to
be the barn dance of ’94, I may as well remark,
for the benefit of the dancing-man, that it cannot
be got through in glove-fit inexpressibles without
hair-raising apprehensions on the part of the
wearer. Kneeling on one knee is not generally
allowed for in this practical age by a man’s tailor,
and the ball-room he-butterfly who isn’t quite
sure whether the moth has been through his ward-
robe or not had better pause before he shakos a
loose leg in the Swedish danoe.
Yours apprehensively,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
“Melbourne, May 22, 1894.
Latest Mildura gossip is, that young Cltaffey
i(son of the founder of the settlement) is engaged
to one of Railway Speight’s daughters, who has
been up there visiting her sister, the wife of Lord
Ranfurley’s manager.
The Sapio and Urso concerts are not well
attended so far, except in the plebeian parts of the
house. Madame De Vere Sapio is, in appearance,
pale and rather colourless, and, when dressed in
white satin with gloves and shoes en train and her
hands clasped in front, she looks as demure as a
girl going to her first communion. This negative
exterior doesn’t prepare one for her splendid sing-
ing, which, in brilliancy of execution, at all events,
surpasses anything hitherto heard in Melb. since
di Murska. Mdrae. Urso is short and very stout,
and doesn’t look in the least graceful playing her
violin. However, hers is another case wberu
appearance counts for nothing. Chopin’s “ three
minutes valse,” tripping from under her bow, is a
measure so ethereally dainty and fairylike as to
sound almost out of place in a sordid and common-
place world.
Mons. the manager of the concerts, is a
handsome man, with clear-cut features and wav-
ing silvery hair. He is said to be something of a
martinet, and keeps everybody connected with
the entertainments well up to the mark. The
audiences also come in for a share of his attention.
If he spies a lady in the “ dress seats ” wearing
even the most unobtrusive of hats, he despatches
an attendant with a polite message which soon
produces the desired result. M. Lucre is the sort
of person sent by Providence to uphold the
majesty of showman business, and on vice-regal
nights he must, I’m sure, wear a halo and look
like one inspired.
At a recent Cathedral crush the clergy’s seats
were rushed by women, who refused to resign
them to the rightful owners pro. tem. Vergers
were repulsed with parasols and scorn, and sweet-
voiced precentors found their blandishments fall
on deaf ears. Even when a. massive parson
approached with an exoommunicative frown, one
dauntless female held her ground and remained,
calm and defiant, among the surplices until the
end of the service.
Again this week a deputation of poor, timid
souls interviewed the Minister of Lands and in-
formed him, among other things, that upon a
specified occasion he “ got drunk and said
naughty words.”
A man, visiting Mr. Mclntyre with this pleas-
ing intelligence, would probably be like Long-
fellow’s Benedict, “who knew by the hob-nailed
shoes it was Basil,” but women, entrenched behind
the traditional sancity of their skirts, proceed to
slang male opponents with impunity. One of the
deputationists, after treading on the Minister, dis-
played extreme solicitude for her own personal
dignity. She declined to be addressed as “ Mrs.,”
on the ground that “Madame” was her correct
title. Mr. Mac. might have replied aptly with an
accent on the “ Mad.”
Professor Marshall Hall, they say, looks for-
ward to founding a Conservatoire in Melbourne.
His scheme will inevitably raise the ire of local
musicians, most of whom have little sympathy
with the professor. One of M. H.’s pet theories
is that, if necessary, an artist should do, suffer, or
sacrifice anything for his art. If bread-and-cheese
sons of harmony raise the domestic household
question the answer is, “ A man must leave wife,
children, father, mother, etc., and cleave unto his
art,” i.e,, don’t absolutely let them starve but regard
them as minor considerations in the fattening of
art.
However, the doctrinaire himself has apparently
solved the problem of both art and mammon, as,
besides being a genius, he is the recipient of a
comfortable salary, and can be soulful on the
solid foundation of .£lOOO a year. And local
nymphs of the urn do say that if Marshall Hall’s
coffee is not sufficiently strong, he reveals an
unexpectedly mundane side of his character. His
allegro movements are also full of subtle meaning
when the milk gets scorched.
Lalla Miranda, who left for London, the other
day, spent her last evening in Melb. at the Bijou,
in company with Mr. and Mrs. Turner, of exhibi-
tion concert fame. The songstress wasn’t looking
her usual bright self, partly owing to a sombre
black gown, and partly, perhaps, to a preliminary
twinge of home-sickness. The Ormuz passengers
should have a pretty cheerful time with lilting
Lalla and dramatic Miss. Bensusan, of Sydney,
both aboard.
Miss Mattie Thompson, second daughter of the
Leviathan bookmaker, and well-known as a taker
of prize after prize at Melb. rink carnivals during
the skating boom, was married on 10th Feb. last
to Mr. H. E. Topping (a son of the well-known
English ringman).
VAN M.
An old Maitland identity has just passed
over, aged 92 years—Eliza, relict of the late
Henry Incledon Pilcher. She has left behind her
some brainy sons, amongst them being C. E.
Pilcher, Q. 0., M.L.0., the pallid and cold-blooded
gentleman who, when he really puts his heart into
a jury case, civil or criminal, has not and never
has had, any superior as an advocate at the Aus-
tralian bar.
The saddest thing which has happened to
The Bulletin for 13 years past arrived last
Monday, when a bard from Neutral Bay sent in
the following letter; —
I beg to enclose for publication in your next issue, a
few lines written for the approaching anniversary of Her
Majesty’s birthday. I know that the sentiment generally
expressed in your paper is not of as loyal a nature as I
should wish to see, but I feel that, at heart, you must be
as loyal as myself, for instance, and that, like the Pistes
of Penzance, with all your faults you must still love Her
Most Gracious Majesty. I trust that the accompanying
few lines may serve to bring forth that loyalty which I
feel sure is only latent in your heart.
The following unspeakable slush is “the few
lines ” in question :
Hark, the merry joy-bells’ pealing
Richly freights the eager air !
Born aloft, the loyal feeling
Trembles o’er the landscape fair.
Far and wide each heart is throbbing
With the pray’rful, happy lay.
Which we, blissful e’en to sobbing.
Sing this twenty-fourth of May.
“ Hail Regina, Dei gratia I”
Great Australia humbly saith,
"Ever proudly filta tua,
Queen, Defender of the Faith!
" May thy subjects still deserve thee—
Thee so marked with Sorrow’s hand.
And thy loving,, tender name be
Ever great thro’out the land.
And this is The Bulletin’s reward for 13 years
of consistent Republicanism J

Personal Items.
M‘llwraith, K.C.M.G. —Kind of Cove to
Make Grants.
Two lawyers are working on a Vic.
labour-farm. People go from miles round to see
them.
Thereare two Parkeses—Harry and Henry.
Both men of note, but one is also a man of pro-
missory note.
“Duckenfield” Bales, M.L.C., the well-
known horse-breeder, lately deceased, left some-
thing like .£200,000.
According to cable, Duncan Gillies is
occupying his time in “visiting various establish-
ments.” Same old game.
Gov. Kintore returned to Adelaide last
Saturday (May 19), but henceforth neither wife
nor child will enliven his vioe-regal exile.
Victoria has had 16 Prime Ministers, < f
whom nine are dead, seven of these having led the
first 12 Ministries formed since the introduction
of ResponsiMe Govt, in 1855.
Mr. Lamonby, well-known as a Melbourne
sporting pressman, and now connected with Lon-
don Field, contests a Parliamentary seat against
Wilfred Lawson, the teetotal punster.
The enemies of Mundella (their name is
legion) have always protested that his father was
an Italian organ-grinder from Jerusalem. A. J.M.
is a hook-nosed person, whose countenance invites
distrust.
The Bulletin hopes that Robb. Reid, the
missionary from Melbourne, will be knigbted as
soon as possible. It would be a pity to have any
delay in the case of this blatant and deserving
drummer.
Haddon Chambers, who fancies himself as
a boxer, lately “took on” a man much heavier
than himself, with the result that he had to retire
for some time into the country to get rid of
ecchymosis.
Pearson’s racehorse, Commotion, waslargely
responsible for filling the See of Armidale with
the Melbourne-trained personality of the Right
Rev. Green. As to which, a tale when the mitre
has settled down.
Of the Dora Dora blacks, “Jacky’’goes
into penal servitude for life, and “Willie” for 15
years—which practically means life, as no Aus-
tralian aboriginal has yet been known to live in
gaol more than seven years.
Governor Duffs youngest son has a taste
for astronomy, and takes lessons from Mr. Russell,
N.S.W. Government Astronomer. He sleeps
alone in the Tower of Government House, where
he has fitted up a small observatory.
During Lieut.-Gov. Way’s reign at Ade-
laide Government House about 80 per cent, of the
guests entertained were clergymen. Which greatly
disappointed people, who had anticipated an im-
provement upon the frigidity of the Kintores.
Ex-Premier Parkes is to be banqueted on
28th inst. at the Hotel Australia, to celebrate the
circumstance of his entering upon his 80th year.
It is hoped that, despite their recent rather acrid
correspondence, Premier Dihbs will be present.
Among those who asked fur mercy for ex-
banker Abigail was Frank Wright, M.L. A. Every
man has his own idea of revenge. How
malevolently did Abigail pursue Wright in that
ineffectual criminal prosecution some years ago !
Alleged in Adelaide that Wood, defaulting
S.A. public trustee, could recently have been
arrested in Brazil, but the opportunity slipped for
reasons which everyone in Adelaide knows.
Wood’s arrest would cause a flutter in many dove-
cotes.
A former Blackheafch (N.S.W.) clergyman
has been commissioned by the S.P.C.K. to write
a history of the Church of England in Australia.
Wonder what he’ll say as to Sam Marsden and
the application-of - torture-to - induce - confession
affair?
Dear Bulletin, —Look at “Diplomacy”
written for you in January, ’93, and then look at
any chapter of “The Yellow Aster.” Then
answer, who wrote “ Diplomacy ? ” “ lota ? ”
If not, the hand that wrote the novel penned the
sketch. —P.W.
Robert Louis Stevenson’s mother was in
Melbourne last week. She says that since R. L.
wrote “ A Footnote to History ” he has been
severely cut by all the German officials at Samoa,
and the German naval officers are not allowed to
call on him as they formerly did.
Robert Louis Stevenson is second cousin
to the sanctified James Balfour, of Melbourne,
The father of that humourless elder was own
brother to Stevenson’s maternal grandfather, so
the novelist’s mysticism would seem to be a poetic
development of old Kirk bigotry.
Sladen says that when he went to America
he was introduced everywhere as the Australian
Laureate. At first he objected faintly, but a
Boston editor told him that “everybody in
Boston has a label; that’s your label; and you’d
best let it be.” So Sladen let it be. So like
Sladen!
Since his elevation to the Yic. Premier-
ship J. B. Patterson has cultivated an extra
ordinary newness. In the matter of tailoring,
collars, cuffs, boots, gloves and shave, he is the
newest man in Melbourne, and half his power is
in the dazzling lustre of his brand-new silk hat.
Politically, he is even shabbier than ever.
In the deputation of Pentridge and Mel-
bourne gaol-warders to the Chief Secretary, which
was formed for the purpose of protesting against
the proposed reductions in their salaries, the five
members thereof were all Irishmen—Ryan, Don-
aghue, Murphy, Fitzgerald, and Stone. What a
time the Scotch prisoners must have, to be sure !
Mary Gaunt, whose Australian story has
been so favourably reviewed by the Melb. daily to
which she contributes, is the daughter of Judge
Gaunt and inherits a large share of the family
beauty. Her brother is a lieutenant on the
Sydney naval station, and her fiance is Dr. Miller,
of Warrnambool, one of the handsomest and most
rollicking of Victorian medicos.
The first man to persistently use “our
Beautiful Harbour ” as a bold advertisement was
Tommy Stubbs, the old-time Sydney auctioneer.
He used the words to some point in his evidence
before the “ Monetary Confusion ” Select Com-
mittee of 1843. Tommy was as guileless as the
rostrum knights usually are, but the whole of the
Harbour water won’t wash off his memory the sin
of inventing the phrase.
The lady mayor of Onehunga is not getting
a fair chance. A man mayor, being continually
assailed by temptations to wrangle and lose his
head, would undoubtedly cut a much meaner
figure than Mrs. Yates has made of herself
hitherto. The ordinary mayor is a raving maniac
when baited by his brethren in private. This
seems to be a case of the public rolling up to assist
at a pre-arranged rumpus.
“ Mr. Geoffrey Eagar, once a prominent
figure in N.S.W. politics,” says Sydney D.T.,
proposes to re-enter public life and will stand as a
candidate for the Glebe as a Freetrader,” &c.
Now, the Mr, Eagar who used to figure in N.S.W.
politics has long been dead, and the present
candidate is his son. And, by the way, the
Geoffrey Eagar was an enthusiastic Protectionist,
like his colleague, James Martin.
N.S.W. Minister Suttor takes with him
to Canada at State expense a secretary (Mr. 0.
Lyne) and a messenger. “Messenger” is good,
and, of course, means valet. By the way, a certain
exalted N.S.W. official makes the departmental
messenger help him pull on his shirt. You
couldn’t expect Charley Lyne to pull on the
Minister’s shirt, and Heaven forbid that so illus-
trious a person as Suttor should have to pull on
his own shirt.
When bushranger Moonlight was charged
with murder, before Mr. Love, the fussy
Gundagai (N.S.W.) P.M., he harangued the
Bench for three hours, until at last the pangs of
hunger wore out poor old Love. “If the
Attorney-General, or any other barrister learned
in the law, were to insult me half as much as you
have done I would order him to be turned out of
Court.” “I wish to God,” retorted the’ranger,
“ that you would turn me out then.”
The grand idea of Premier Patterson’s
life is to have the week-day life of erring humanity
regulated by calm Sabbathian principles, so that
Sunday will be practically the same as Saturday,
and not a bit different from Tuesday or Wednesday.
Patterson, it appears, has elevated his own daily
life up to this standard. Existence is one eternal
Sunday with him, and he sees no reason why
banquets should be restricted to six-sevenths of
the week, since he is prepared at all times to gorge
to the glory of God,

daughter of Madame Belloc (cousin to ex-Premier
Parkes). She is an exceedingly clever and charm-
ing young woman, half French, half English, and
wholly pretty. Petite and plump, she makes a
delicious picture (to the masculine mind, at any-

David Christie Murray, the gay Lothario
of 1894, writing ten years ago, rhapsodises in
“Coals of Fire and Other Stories”
Love works strange metamorphoses. To be in love is a
delicious and an altogether admirable thing. I would be
in love again to-morrow if I could. You should be
welcome to your foolish laugh at my raptures. Ah me ! I
shall never know those raptures any more ; and the follies
you will laugh at in me will he less noble, less tender, less
innocently beautiful than those of young love.
Yea, David, verily thou wast a true prophet.
A number of nasty paragraphs are being
written re the domestic affairs of David Christie
Murray, lately sued in London for maintenance
by his wife, who, poor woman, is naturally wild
because she has been left with .£1 a-week, or less,
“ to work a sewing-machine for from ss. to 12s. a-
week,” while David, and the nice, musical girl he
picked up in Melbourne, occupy diggings for
which he pays .£3 3s. weekly. However, accord-
ing to the newspaper reports, Mrs. Murray ad-
mitted in court that she had been confined in an
asylum for grog and morphia victims. Much may
be forgiven a man whose wife takes either grog or
morphia—provided that he didn’t drive her to it.

bearded jockey, got charged the other day with

Another Jacky Dow story. Swaggy asks
work from Dow, who, to “ ascertain his bonaj
fides,” sets him at the homestead door and tells
him to bow. “Who to?” asks swaggy. “Never
mind who to, my man ; all you have to do is to
bow,” was Dow’s reply. And so, all day, in the
heat of the sun, poor swaggy bowed to the distant
horizon. At nightfall he explained to the station
hands that his (adjective) back was broken in
various (adjective) places. When Dow appeared
upon the scene next morning, the light bower
was a speck in the far distance.
Dear Bulletin, —I am tired of the puffery
accorded by you to Meteorologist Wragge. Vanity
is Wragge’s mainspring, and he has no works.
When a man prophesies every day of his life,
some of his predictions must come true ; people
remember the few truths and forget the fictions.
When the big flood of ’93 rose Oft. over Brisbane
bridge, Wragge didn’t predict that, but three days
afterwards he publicly ukased that if it kept fine
the water would continue falling. A few weeks
later he did predict the greatest cycloneon record,
but it hasn’t come yet, although chimney-stacks
were secured, and respectable merchants held back
orders, expecting galvanised-iron to rise (in two
senses). Wragge gets a fat screw, has had two or
three daisy trips at the country’s expense attend-
ing conferences and examining black gins for
scientific purposes, and is altogether an over-
boomed individual.— Disgusted.
Chief Justice Madden has broken
again. This time Sir Oracle, K.C.M.G., deal®
with dipsomania, and puts his little say-so agains C
the opinions of experts, with the bovine confT
denceof an important personage who likes himself
very much, and doesn’t care who knows it. Ob-
serve :
Drunkenness is no more a disease than any of the other
defaults which are the subject of investigation in the
courts. It may just as well be said that adultery is a
disease, for it might also arise from a diseased and un-
disciplined will.
His Honor abolishes the idea of a hereditary
taint in one act, also the possibility of con-
cupiscence being a disease or the effect of a disease.
Evidently there is more in man and woman than
is dreamt of in Madden’s philosophy. He is not
even conscious of his own ailment, which is an
aggravated case of swelled head.

Dear Bulletin,—ln your article “The
Victorian Bar ” you omit an incident which goes
to show that a member of the blood royal is not
ipso facto an inherent ass. The story is as
follows ; —Some brilliant member of the then most
fashionable Sydney club, at 4 p.m. one day, pro-
posed the boycotting of the leading counsel for
the defence in Regina v. O Farrell (for shooting
to murder the Duke of Edinburgh). At 4 o’clock
next afternoon, to the chagrin of all the dudes,
who should come down the block arm-in-arm but
the Duke and Aspinall, Q.C. !—0.8.M.
The crowd who waited on N.S. W. Minister
of Justice Slattery to ask for the release of bank-
cronk Abigail, ex-N.S.W. Minister of Mines,
afforded a lovely study. Hardly any of them
were really friends of the prisoner—for people
who have got into gaol never have any but obscure
friends. Half the deputation went to the Justice
office only to work a political rig, and had its eye
on the Orange vote and the general election all
the time ; another lot went there because it knew
very well that it ought to be in gaol itself, and so
thought bluff the best policy ; and a third division
appealed for Abigail, not because it cared a curse
for him, but because it was working for the
emancipation of Peter Howe, ex-M.P., or Miller
or some other of the brigade of bank and building
society crooks. Abigail, as an ex-Minister of the
Crown--poor Crown !—is, as it were, the loco-
motive of the whole trap-full of rogues. When he
starts, the train follows.
The Sydney representative of a big Lon-
don publishing house confessed the other day that
his firm made, proportionately, far more money
out of “ Australasian literature” than out of. any
other line. Which reminds us that Louis Becke’s
charming South Sea stories which have appeared
from time to time in The Bulletin, are being
republished in London by Fisher-XJnwin. They
will form one of the Autonym (uniform with the
Pseudonym) series, the first of which will be by
Marion Crawford. The Bulletin predicts for
Mr. Becke’s stories a success unparalleled in South
Sea literature. At any rate, they deserve it.
IS HE TO BE “SIR” ARTHUR?—TO DAY IS THE DAY.
Dear Bulletin, —Have you noticed how
the cheap and natural “zinco” process for illus
trating books, papers, etc., is playing up with the
lord-loving proclivities and religious loyalty of a
devoted people? Since the adoption of this
process by the jingo-ridden English illustrated
weeklies, the “orty” British aristocrat has
dwindled to an insignificant five-feet-four mostly,
and is found to have a wry spine, and two large,
obtuse front teeth, which give him the intellectual
aspect of a boiled rabbit; also, the Prince of
Wales has grown suddenly fatter and balder, and
loyalists are inexpressibly shocked to find that he
has no legs worth mentioning above the knee.
Lately a London illustrated, run for years exclu-
sively for the glorification of the Royal Family,
the nobility, and the military, came out with a
picture of Queen Victoria that resembled a canvas-
covered merry-go-round with a hat on top. I
want to ask shouldn’t the process be put down as
treasonable and seditious, and calculated to under-
mine the throne, burst up the constitution, and
bring the House of Lords into contempt?—S.S.

The Australian’s Recklessness.
The Englishman and the Scotchman, and
especially the latter, have much to say in and out
of season about the recklessness of the Australian,
the ease with which he throws aside work for play,
and his general improvidence. Especially against
the bush Australian do they level the accusation
that he is, with few exceptions, a periodical
drunkard ; that he can never earn a cheque without
itching to spend it; and that improvidence begins
and ends in him. To judge superficially as the old
worlders have done, without any knowledge or
appreciation of Australian conditions,- all the
foregoing charges are true. But treat the subject
intelligently and you find the Australian is jiut
the reverse. All that is wrong with him is dys-
pepsia at the beginning, and later on aggravate d
kidney diseases. The menu of the bush is un-
varying, which is in itself a bad thing, and of
poor quality, which is very much worse. Damper
to the writer, and such damper as he used to make,
is a substance constituted merely of flour, water,
bicarbonate of soda, if you have it, no bicarbonate
of soda if yon haven’t any, a little salt and a large
fire. Six months of such a diet finds the bnshman
surcharged with bile; his stomach coated with the
tannin of the unspeakable tea ; his throat caked
with the soot of sheep-wash tobacco ; then lie gets
his cheque and winds up at the shanty. We have
numberless instances of bush dyspepsia and fever
having been completely cured by a few doses Of
the Oure. It gently stimulates the jaded system,
and reorganises the disorganised functions.
Warner’s Safe Cure is the one bush medicine.

Editor Bulletin, — The Pastoral Times,
a Freetrade and squatting newspaper published at
Deniliquin, N.S.W., runs a job-printing office.
The owner and editor, who are eternally preaching
Fret trade, print in their issue of 12th inst. the*
fo lowing :
Do not encourage town travellers for printing orders,
but support the local office, which spends its-money in
your midst. The town men merely want your money
without helping your district.
Is not that the kernel of the Protectionist
doctrine ?
An Australian Premier observed recently :
“ I can do something with a mercantile man—
there’s the Treasury; I can fix up a lawyer—
there’s the Attorney-Generalship; I can send men
of plain commonsense to Education, the Post
Office and other billets ; but what can I do with a
journalist? Nothing; I tell you, nothing. I
never met one in politics who didn’t try to be
omniscient, with a break-down in every five
minute’s talk. Take one of them into the Minis-
try ! Not I! He’d spend his office-hours writing
leading articles, damaging the prestige of his
colleagues and glorifying his own attributes.
Rev. W. H. Daniels, a peripatetic Yankee
parson, at Adelaide Methodist Conference the
other day: —
If Australia wants population she should send to Japan
for shiploads. The Japanese Government would be only
too ready to send them off. Let them be located in the
uninhabited parts of this continent; then send Methodist
preachers amongst them to teach and minister to them,
and in about two generations there would be a population
that would be a credit to the place.

That most ardent of Freetraders—the
Sydney housewife —acknowledges that “things
were never so cheap ” as now under Protection.
She finds fault with but one Act of the Protec-
tionist Ministry, their abolition of _ the tea-
duty. A specific tax on any article is against
inferior quality, as it will always bear a larger
proportion to the lower cost of producing the
cheaper rubbish. Thus a 6d. tax on a fid. article
would be 100 per cent., the same tax on a Is.
article would be only 50 per cent. In England,
when the remission of the, malt duty took place,
the market was flooded with cheap foreign barley
previously unsaleable. Sydney is now flooded
with cheap and nasty tea.

In a Maoriland court, the other day, a
husband and wife were charged with sly-grog-
selling. Their lawyer arranged that the cases
should be tried separately. The man was charged
first, but the lawyer proved so conclusively that
the offence was committed by the wife that he
was acquitted. Then the woman was charged,
before another jury, but the same lawyer rose up
and proved beyond any possible doubt that the
offence was committed by her husband only, and
the jury discharged her also. Yet if any layman
had called that lawyer a liar it would probably
have been actionable.
A few months ago Mr. Moss, British
Resident at Raratonga, and John Webster, of
Opononi, Hokianga, interviewed the Governor of
Maoriland “on island matters.” _ Possibly this
had something to do with Seddon’s idea of taking
over the Government of Samoa from the Three
Powers. Webster, by the way, is an old South
Sea wanderer. He was with Ben Boyd in his
fatal cruise in the Wanderer yacht. An English
paper says :
Mr. Webster’s connection with South Sea affairs goes
back to 1851, when he was on board the yacht Wanderer, 12
guns, with the celebrated Ben Boyd, cruising in the
Pacific, with the intention of founding a South Sea
liepublio. The murder of Boyd on the island of Guadal-
camar put an end to the expedition, but in 55-6 Mr.
Webster had a narrow escape of becoming “ King of the
Cannibal Islands,” when a fresh idea was conceived of
forming some of the islands into a confederation under the
protectorate of the Government of the Sandwich Islands.
Mr Webster was appointed sovereign chief of Eastern San
Ohristoval; Mr. Charles P. Juliet), N.S. Wales, of Marau ;
and Mr. C. G. De Reeves, of Malo. In 1859, King Kam-
mehehay (sic) created Mr. Webster a Knight Commander
of the Order of Arossi, to which only four appointments
were ever made. The Confederation of San Ohristoval
through various causes never became an accomplished
fact, and England and Germany of late years have
divided the Solomon Islands between them.
The “founding a South Sea Islands Republic ” is
good. Boyd simply went to the Solomons on a
blackbirding trip in a piratical vessel. The
Wanderer returned without him, and the captain
reported that Boyd had been killed. As a matte^.
of fact, no one saw him killed. He went on shore
and never came back again, and the captain of
the Wanderer didn’t waste time in looking for his
chawed-np fragments. The yacht was wrecked
off Port Macquarie on her way back to Sydney,
and proved a godsend to the people of that dreary
village. _ >

Only the initiated (remarks an English
paper) were aware that the “ Dr. Howard,’ who
for the last few years has been one of the residents
of South Kensington, was no other than the
spy Le Caron, whose evidence in the Parnell
Commission trial proved so valuable. _ After
that historic period the Major was in receipt of a
handsome annuity from the Times, which further
insured his life at a very large sum; this, by the
Way, will give the Major’s very attractive
daughter quite a comfortable fortune. He Caron s,
or rather Dr. Howard’s, life at the last was, if
anything, quite as much threatened as at any
period of his adventurous career, so graphically
related in the well-known volume he published
some years since. His house was guarded by a
posse of detectives, for the fate of Carey, there is
no doubt, awaited Le Caron had he relaxed for a
moment his vigilance. x
The second gathering of traders connected
with confectionery, baking, and grocery, an-
nounced in our advertising columns, to be held in
London, England, in September next, will be earned
out on strictly trade lines, and will be similar to
that held every October by the brewing and dis-
tilling trades. Bread and confectionery-making
were last year carried on at this exhibition in a
very large way, and over 1000 loaves a day were
given to the poor of London. The first show was
attended by over 125,000 traders, and the
engineers and other exhibitors who filled the
Royal Agricultural Hall (which is the largest
building in London) were more than satisfied with
the number of orders they received. A number of
American and foreign firms have taken up space
for this year’s exhibition, and those interested in
the trade will find it well repay them to make a

The click of the new Hammond type-writer
is as sweet as the glug-glug of the iced claret-cup
which fertilises the dreams of the brick-red Cool-
gardian. The writing is rapid and excellent, with a
record of 181 words per minute ; and anyone who
wants a good machine should see the agents,
Messrs. Reid and Wilson, at Victoria Arcade,
Sydney.

Society
A fashionable Australian tailor has filed
ids schedule as the only means of collecting out-
standing debts. His estate is likely to realise far
more than 20s. in the £. The poor devil daren’t
dun. Some time ago he sent the nephew of a
social bigwig a final notice. The amount, about
JlBO, was paid under pressure, but the “bad pay,”
in revenge, probably," used his influence so that the
tailor lost all his custom among the members of a
club. So, consequently, he deems it better to file
than to ask for his own.

The latest Melbourne swindle is ingenious,
yet beautifully simple. Jones, the big wholesale
draper, is rung up on the telephone by a man who
professes to be Smith, the retail draper, and who
orders £5OO worth of goods. Goods sen t accord ingly.
Then Smith is rung up in his turn by a man who
professes to be Jones, saying that the goods have
been sent by mistake, and the carter will call
round and remove them at once. Goods removed
accordingly, and never heard of again.

AS. A. Parliamentary party which recently
visited Renmark and Mildura, on arrival at the
latter alleged temperance settlement found a cargo
of brandy on the wharf. As a temperance settle-
ment, Mildura can give points to Maine.
* ♦ *
Chinese laundries are rapidly increasing in
Melbourne, and are,to be seen with startling fre-
quency in the streets of city and suburbs. As a
plaintive appeal from their cheap and leprous
Eopularity one can now see “ The Englishwoman’s
laundry,” “ The Australian Native’s Laundry,”
and other melancholy attempts to wean the
affections of the lady of the house from the yellow
curse to her white brothers and sisters. Why are
there no Chinese laundries in Sydney ? And why
do they sell apples by the pound in Melb. instead
of by the dozen ?

The S.M. Herald’s argument in favour ot
the early release of Abigail is, that that offender
committed his crime at a time when financial
gambling was the rage in Australia. But its
argument for hanging—the Herald is always for
hanging —Williams and Montgomery is that
burglary was becoming too common an offence at
the time they took to the business. The Herald
should set a man to think this point out.
# * #
Dr. Bancroft, of Brisbane, protests against
isolating lepers, on the ground that the practice
is both barbarous and useless, his opinion being
that the disease is either not contagious at all or is
the least-contagious of all diseases. But Dr. B.'s
opinion is only an opinion. Until certainty is
arrived at the white world will give itself the
benefit of the doubt.
* * #
Chief-Justice Madden is surprised to find
that a considerable proportion of his customers, ab
Melb. Divorce Court, have been married by Rev.
Nat. Kinsman. But it is also a fact that a largo
percentage of the happy couples, who go market-
ing on Saturday nights, were joined in wedlock by
that same ordained furniture-dealer. Every time
Madden meets a jubilant father pushing the
perambulator he can safely take eight to one, or
even shorter odds, that Kinsman gave religious
sanction to the baby’s birth. Likewise, when
Justice Madden observes a pair of dark forms
hugging one another in the gloaming, he may
wager his 30s. bell-topper to a 12 guinea dress-suit
that the amorous objects will presently swear
away their freedom in old Nathaniel’s back
parlour. After which he may estimate their
chance of flying to Madden for relief from bond-
age. Ere committing himself to further remarks
on the psychological aspect of Kinsman marriages
the learned Judge had better know for certain the
number of Melbourne people who get spliced by
anybody else. Meanwhile, he will oblige The
Bulletin by noting that Smith, Brown, Jones &
McKenzie are favourite names for co-respondents.
# * *
“1 notice that these marriages of Kins-
man’s are the most soluble that come here,” savs
Chief Justice Madden. Is his Honor justified in
advertising Kinsman’s business in this bare-faced
manner?

• * * _
The public demonstration at the opening
of Lismore-Tweed (N.S.W.) railway was an amus-
ing affair. The locals spread their procession two-
deep, and single-file, so that press correspondents
would wire that it covered several miles or less of
streets. The Bishop led the procession, in advance
of the school children, as he would an ordinary
funeral. Then came a cart with some sugar-cane,,
which represented “ district agriculture,” followed
by a tinsmith making a tank on . a lorry, and a
little boy on another waggon making lollies, repre-
senting “district manufactures.” Next in point
of precedence to the Minister came a tip-dray load
of aboriginal children (they were as white as the
school children). It is not known exactly what
they represented. Probably they were also local
products.

Dear Bulletin, —You say “The first
thing Jaboz Balfour asked for after his arrest was

You object to Hingland taking credit for Scotch

Cakes’ hypocrisy, parsimony, gloom and illegiti-
mate bairns a present of its typical human output
—Jabez Balfour? —Yours, English.
# « #
Mr. Alf. Smart, a Melbourne architect, is
off to London with a patented process for burning
pictures upon wood panels. The. novelty of
Smart’s invention lies in the fact that the hot
point of his hollow drawing-pencil (made of
platinum) doesn’t touch the wood. Delicate
cauterisation is effected by hot air; the artist
draws his decorative pattern, or picture, with
heated atmosphere, modulating his tints by the
distance between the pencil and the wood. One
of the best samples of the inventor’s own skill is a
character-portrait of George Lauri, who has a
share in the little syndicate formed for working
the patent. This new hot-point process admits of
much finer artistic work than the old “ poker ”
method, which it is bound to supersede. Further-
more, it should enlarge the sphere of burnt-wood
panelling, and carry culchaw into many a numble
’ome.

Dear Bulletin, —I am one of four un-
married sisters, and want to know if you will help
us to get husbands. The other day the City
Employment Committee of Pittsburg, U.S.,
issued notice that they would employ only
married men. Next day the marriage-license
clerk was at work from 9 a.m. till dark. Every
unmarried Italian, Pole and Hungarian in Pitts-
burg hunted up a wife. Now, why can’t this be
done in Australia? If the Government would
only make a law that no single man would be kept
in the service, what thousands of hippy-homes
there would soon be ! —Yours, Amelia McGaunt.
* * •
The Pitcairn islanders are having bad
times nowadays. Eighteen months ago a party
of American Seventh Day Adventists exploited
the Society and Paumotu Groups, and in their
schooner, the Pitcairn, visited the lonely little
island and converted the, people from•“ hard-cased
Christianity ” into Seventh Day Adventism. The
new religion has proved too much. The mission
schooner brought, in addition to the new brand of
Terewth, dysentery, diphtheria, measles and in-
fluenza ; and now comes the news that many of
the miserable survivors* (about 70) have been
attacked with malignant typhoid, to which 12
persons have already succumbed, amongst them
the well-known Rosa Young.

to a Melb. doctor, offering to run a partnership.
“In recognition of your endeavours,” said the

that he has been “grossly insulted.” As if any
iffan could be insulted by a liberal commission-
er even by a moderate commission,

RE THE NEW-CHUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
■ There’s something in the new-ohum’s face,
Who just has got his letter,
Suggesting nights of purple pace
And wish to know him better.
There’s something in his wrinkled “ bags ”
When letters have been stopped
Suggesting kinship with the vags.
And friendship quickly dropped.
There’s something in the new-chum’s lapse,
Though drink his nature steep,
Suggesting mother’s love, perhaps.
And making angels weep.

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding’ and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with t lie merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at

A curious trouble has arisen in Melbourne
between a German named Yunker and another
German named Gurmppenberg. Yunker couldn’t
speak English, and he fell in love with a girl who
couldn't speak German. Consequently, he
engaged Gurmppenberg as intermediary, and for
A WOMAN’S REVENGE.
[?]USBAND: “What! You bid 255. for a meat safe that isn’t worth more than three half-
crowns?”
WIFE: “Mrs, Jones was bidding against me, and I owed her a grudge.”
two weeks that Teuton sat beside the lovers and
translated their tender endearments in a gruff
voice to one another. At the end of the fortnight
they dispensed with Gurmppenberg and got
married, and their agent sued for £4O for work
and labour done, and was nonsuited. The Bul-
letin is distinctly of opinion that if there is any
justice on earth Gurmppenberg should have got
his money. If the girl was pretty then the job of
making love to her for two weeks on behalf of
another man, when he naturally wanted to be
making love to her himself, was a severe strain on
the constitution. And if the girl was ugly
the position was even more trying. Any man
who has sat for two weeks along with a red-haired
girl with a wart on her nose, and told her she was
a beautiful gazelle, knows the feeling, and when a
man has to tell her instead, in a hoarse, unim-
passioned voice, that another man says in a
foreign language that she is a gazelle, all the in-
terest goes out of it.

The shareholders of a Southern coffee-
palace lately appointed a committee of investiga-
tion. The committee found k locked room, which
the directors would not open. A committee-man
insisted, and there was found a goodly stock of
wine. It was explained that, under medical
advice, an official had to take a bottle daily for
dinner.

Dear Bulletin, — It would appear that at
least one Sydney stipendiary (at an annual salary
,of £850) has been in the habit of exacting a guinea
fee from confinees for every visit paid by him to
Darlinghurst Gaol for the purpose of granting
bail. To this extortion the Attorney-General has
put a stop. But it may now happen that when
the fiiends of a prisoner wait upon a stipendiary
after office-hours, they may be told that he is
“out,” and the detenu may have to wait in gaol
for bail until next day. Why cannot the prison
visiting justice reside in the neighbourhood and
attend to all bail cases—or why should not the
gaol governor be a J.P, with power (bail having
been given at the police-court) to accept bail
bonds?—F.

The school-children who, by the recent
collapse of the floor of a sanitary-house attached
to Erskineville (Sydney) public-school, were pre-
cipitated into the cess-pit below, are only offered
damages by the authorities to the value of the
clothes spoilt. The parents of two children, how-
ever, stand out for further compensation, failing
which they go to law.
* #, *
The hangman business is evidently falling
into disrepute. Roberts, the new Vie. strangler
and gaol-flogger, and the widow of his prede-
cessor, Jones, were recently accused, in Mel-
bourne, of taking a gold watch from a drunk and
putting in his pocket instead the brass “ ticker ”
of the late lamented hangman. Roberts was dis-
charged, the evidence being insufficient. Also, on
the same day, the two-year-old child of the
deceased was charged with being a neglected
infant. If the gallows business is ever going to
be made respectable the salary must be raised to
<£looo a year, and the offender must always be
hanged in evening-dress, with a flower in his
button-hole. Also, the hangman must appear in
evening-dress and white gloves, and wear his
rings outside his gloves.

TWO LETTERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One was a garland of flowers from the bride,
And one was a wreath from the dead,
From the brow and the breast of two sisters, inside
The frailest of caskets they sped.
And they came unto me in the still eventide
When the glory of sunset had sped.
The darkness drew round and I bitterly cried,
In the night with the stars overhead,
©’er that garland of flowers from the brow of the bride
And that wreath from the breast of the dead—
And with me till death’s-day must ever abide
That mpmory of anguish and dread.
One told how in gladness and pleasure and pride
The fair Muriel had been wed,
And “dear little Marie hath sickened and died’’
Was all 'that the other one said—
Ah! one was a garland of flowers from the bride
And one was a wreath from the dead.
N.S. Wales.
H.
A GOOD STOVE.
A WORD OF CAUTION.
Everyone knows what a good stove is—at least,
the women do ; and every woman knows that, of
all the treasures of the household, a really first-
rate, reliable cooking-stove is the greatest desider-
atum in a house. A great many stoves are
advertised every year, many of which are like
jerry-built houses —thrown together to sell. A
word of caution is therefore necessary to intending
buyers, because there are few things so annoying
and vexatious as to buy a stove and then find that
it does not possess all the qualities that are neces-
sary to give complete and lasting satisfaction.
Now, the points in choosing a good stove are:—
1. Strong oast-iron frame.
2. Good draught.
3. Facilities to burn either wood, coal, or
even turf.
4. Simplicity of parts, and thus, easy to clean.
5. Duplicate parts kept in stock, so that
bieakages can be replaced at small cost
and without delay.
And if, in addition to these five points, you can
get a stove that is also an ornament to the kitchen,
all the better.
Price, of course, is a point, but that is a matter
which need scarcely be mentioned, since, if the
above points are combined in any stove, it would
be cheap at any price within reason.
It only remains for us to say that, with con-
fidence, we can honestly recommend “The
Housewife,” sold by Holdsworth, Macpherson
and Co., 250 George-street, Sydney, from -£3 10s.
upwards, as one that fully and entirely “ fills the
bill”
We should suggest our readers writing to this
firm for an illustrated catalogue, with further
particulars. * = .

M'Burney, Wallis & Co.'s Report.
M'Bumey, Wallis and Co. report: -The sales this week
were characterised by brisk bidding and, if anything, a
slight increase in prices. The wool offered was of the
description usual at this time of the year—viz., country
and local scoured and odd and inferior lots of greasy.
Judging from cables just received from our London
agents, Sydney prices are slightly-in excess of the home
market, and we fear those who shipped their wool last
season will have cause and leisure to repent their action.
The time lias come when “ Sell in Sydney " should be the
wool-growers’motto.
A considerable quantity of Queensland woq! wasoffered,
but owdng to its wasiy condition did not receive much
attention from buyers, and a number of lots were passed
in, ow ners clinging too milch to values received at this
time last year, when the market was firmer and the wool
in much more attractive condition. We did not sell this
week, but will offer a good catalogue in the next fort-
nightly series.

The Wild Cat Column.
Almost all the Australian reconstructed banks
reported enthusiastically that the first call had met with
a splendid response; in the worst oases it was paid up
in full, and in many instances considerable sums were
received on account of the second call. That second call
is now about due in most instances, and the enthusiasm has
ail evaporated. By the time the eighth call comes along
the annual meeting of the average reconstructed bank
will be a pandemonium, and amid the general frenzy a lot
of strange circumstances will come to light.
• • • •
At a recent general meeting of shareholders in a
certain re-constructed bank, a bondholder clenched his
speech—an all-round onslaught upon the management—
with the following:—“Our general manager. Mr. Blank
Dash, is hated by the bank’s clients, feared by the officials
over whom circumstances have put him, and is regarded
generally as the weakest item in the bank’s constitution.”
However, the newspapers don’t report such things; it
destroys confidence.
• • • •
A member of a well-known firm of Southern
solicitors was lately proceeded against on a serious finan-
cial charge. There was a desperate rally of lawyers to the
rescue, the case promptly “ fell through," and the
•• great” newspapers said not a word about it. If this
sort of thing goes on we shall have ordinary defendants of
unproved charges bringing actions for malicious persecu-
tion against journals that report them. Suppress for the
goose should bo suppress for the gander.
• e • •.
A disturbing rumour in Melbourne is to the
effect that Price’s Mounted Rifles are petitioning Govern-
ment, in the name of public morality, to dissect the Bal-
four-Elliott insolvency and make things livdv for some of
the directors of the Australian Freehold I..vestment Co.
The Council of Churches, it is said, will also be invited to
enquire into these worldly matters and report thereon.
• • • •
Larkworthy, ex-managing director in London
of the N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Co., is rough upon ex-
Premier Shiels, who proclaimed the company’s stability in
fervent language at a general meeting: “I don t accept
any statement a man of that sort would make at a publio
meeting,” said Larkworthy. What sort?
* * • •
Certain unpleasant facts came to light at the
last annual meeting of the New Zealand Fire Insurance
Co This is a big institution with a reserve fund of
£235,000, which fund, it transpires, is largely invested in
shares of various companies. These shares are mostly
of the ordinary contributing kind, and when the com-
panies begin to burst up, and the calls come in, there
will be exceedingly pleasant times for the N.Z. Co.’s
shareholders. In one 00. alone they are contingently
liable for £230,000 in calls, and as the concern in question
is in rather a depressed condition the prospects are not
any too bright. This original way of investing the reserve
fund makes that alleged reserve a huge liability instead
of an asset, and as the N.Z. Fire Insurance is an un-
limited 00., the shareholders may, in some time of
panic, find themselves with their savings and their capital
all gone in one act, and their 00. burst up, and a million
or so of liabilities on hand as well.
• e • •
The chairman of the Bank of Australasia,
orating to the London shareholders, re his gloomy fears
of last year, said: “Thank God, ray anticipations have not
been realised.” Any association of the Almighty’s name
with Australian banking smacks strongly of blasphemy.
• • • •
The Grand and Federal Coffee Palaces, Melb.,
are clubbing to put a short bill through Parliament,
enabling them to get liquor-licenses. One of these saintly
places now makes the fortune of a neighbouring hotel.
• • e e
Story for reoonstructed-bank directors Perth,
Tuesday.—Alice Drew, a widow, committed suicide at
Albany by Joutting her throat. She had contemplated
suicide for some time. After cutting her throat, she
walked across the room and sat down, apparently, when
she reached the door, and called for help. The room pre-
sented a terrible sight. A verdict of suicide when tem-
porarily insane was returned. Two years ago the
deceased deposited £2OO in the Commercial Bank for her
boy, to he paid when he reached 21. When she became
destitute she attempted to get back some of this money,
but without success.

• • * •
The Colonial Mutual Insurance Society got an
excellent “ad.," the other day, in London. A wealthy
citizen lodged a proposal for £5OOO, but when on his
way to the office to be medically examined he fell under a
train and was killed. The society’s prospectus was found
in his pocket, and was duly mentioned in the papers
among the other particulars of the sad ooourrenoe.
Then the management naturally wrote to correct the
altogether unfounded rumour that the proposal was
made under table J when it was really under table A, or
something to that effect, and drew more attention to
Itself. All of which means business. You have to adver-
tise even on a corpse, nowadays, if you want to keep
abreast of the rush.
• • • •
The Bank of Australasia announces a dividend
of 74 per cent., and shows a satisfactory profit of £60,606.
This is not quite up to some of the brilliant results of
former years, hut it is a remarkably good showing at such
a time as the present. The bank has £4,000,000 in cash
and Government securities, and of its reserve fund of
£BOO,OOO, half-a-million is invested in British consols,
where it can be readily found when wanted. The Austra-
lasia has always been an intensely conservative institu-
tion, and its conservatism has done it good service during
the crisis. There is a trifling shrinkage in business, as a
matter of course, but there is no sudden and awful drop
In the assets, as has been the case with most banks of
late, and the shares are remarkably steady.
• * • *
The spiritualists who started boring for coal at
St. Hilda (Melb.) on the strength of a medium’s revela-
tions are beginning to lose faith in their informant. It is
one thing to be a general believer, but quite another
thing to back your spiritualistic opinion in a particular
case.
• • • •
The mining-talk of the day centres round the
Mount Huxley, Tasmania, alleged discovery-indicated in
The Bulletin some weeks ago. The syndicate consists
Of 300 paid-up shares of £250 each, whereof the original
owner, Mr. Barker (of Ebsworth and Barker, Sydney),
takes 100 for his interest, with £BOOO cash from the
£25 000 subscribed by the public, to whom 100 shares were
•old’. The other hundred shares are held by Mr. P. P.
Samuel, the promoter. The shares were last week quoted
in Sydney at £l2OO, and fractional interests only were
being sold by the “ velvet ” brigade. The money was
fully subscribed at the first meeting, but shareholders will
be entitled to a refund of their cash, minus a small
amount lor expenses, in the event of the experts sent over
by them not confirming the original reports. Various
Statements are being made as to the mine s richness.
Late news is that the Tasmanian Government
geologist has gone to inspect the show, which, it is said,
has long been known as ground running about 3dwts.,
and which would only pay if treated on a huge scale.
Should thls'be true everything will depend on how much
there is of it. Twelve shillings a ton in gold is quite good
enough where there is plenty of dirt and you are able
to shove it into the mill at Is. a ton.
Should Mount Huxley only turn out a 3-d wt.
affair instead of 10-dwt., as believed by the promoters,
the ill-luck of the children of Israel In gold-mining will
receive yet another awful illustration. But should it pay
10-dwt., then about 40 sons of Judah will take an early
departure for Park Lane.
The publio would shriek with laughter if they
only knew the exact style and qualifications of the
“ experts ” who are occasionally allowed to boom mines
in big type.
• * • *
Three mines at Belmore, N.S.W., including the
Bluejacket, have been amalgamated and floated in London
for £150,000. How much working capital this means is
unstated. In the district, at Boney Rocks, there is a
mine called the Lady Burdett Coutts. Which somehow
recalls that when Pritchard Morgan first went to England
betook with him a Charters Towers mine called “The
Scotchman,’’ and set about floating it, “Scotchman I”
said the broker, “Scotchmen aren’t popular in London,
Call it the Disraeli.” And he did,

Bayley’s output stands at lOOOoz.; last escort
from the field brought 25000 z. It is wired that, “ owing
to the prevalence of crime here, it is generally believed
that the escort will be stuck up, sooner or later,’*
Bayley’s shares are rising.

What is said to be a very big thing in gold is
being carefully shepherded in a remote region of North
Queensland. It was found by a man now dead and a
Victorian crowd have fitted out an expedition to size it
up. But somebody has got ahead of them.

• • * *
Junction (8.H.) makes a profit of .£916 10s. for
half-year to March 31. Previous half-year’s surplus was
£4267. Ore raised during the half-year was 550 tong car-
bonate and 5957 tons sulphide - 2240 tons sulphide were
sold abroad and 2698 tons, with only 483 tons carbonate,
were smelted. No other Broken Hill mine has turned its
sulphide to such good account.
* * * *
The J unction Co. recently effected an indepen-
dent 10 per cent, all-round reduction, the second since
the strike, and, curiously, the larger mines have not yet
followed suit. Said that the Proprietary directors,
during their recent visit of inspection, discussed a further
reduction and that Manager Howell opposed it, wherefore
it was abandoned ! In the face of Western allurements,
says Howell, it is already difficult enough to retain good
men.
* * « «
South Australian Government has reduced the
rates for carriage of sulphides from Broken Hill to Port
Adelaide from 21s. Bd. to 15s. 6d. per ton, carbonate rates
to remain at the old figure. The reduction gives an
impetus to sulphide treatment at the seaboard and
export, and will be compensated for by the increased
tonnage of ore carried. Even 15s. 6d. per ton for 333
miles is high. S.A, only relaxes the screw on the score of
expediency.
« • • •
Block 10 Warren, who has already one costly
and elaborate sulphide experimental plant hung up, is to
the fore with an improved jigging process, for which he
claims the practical solution of the sulphide problem.
The difficulty is, as usual, with the slimes. The long-
suffering shareholders, who have provided the where-
withal for many experiments, will be rejoiced at the
prospect of the profitable treatment of their immense and
at present valueless sulphide dump, especially as the rich
carbonate in the mine is nearly all gone now.

* • • •
As Wyalong goes up the crushing average goes
down. The 3oz. and 4oz. stone is getting scarce, and
Charters Towers may have a chance in the race yet. The
latest Barmedman returns are 5560 z. from 407 tons, but
“ some owners decline to furnish their returns for publi-
cation ” —doubtless because they don’t want to be
pestered with speculators anxious to buy the shows.
However, there is ample warrant for hope that good work
will return good profits once the skiting corps and the
booming brigade are silenced.
• * * «
Gibbons and party, prospecting on Bolte’s
selection, about 2J- miles from “old” Wyalong, struck
something which, up to date, seems the nearest approach
to a well-defined reef yet found ; it shows good gold and
is 2ft. thick. Bolte’s claim it was that showed such
a splendid blow a few weeks aero. Wyalong threatens to
be at least an excellent “ tucker’’-field.
• • ♦ •
The first death on Wyalong occurred on Mon-
day, May 14th. Andrew Mathieson, the deceased, suc-
cumbed to enteritis, possibly caused by bad water. The
bands of Perry’s and Montgomerie’s circuses led the pro-
cession, playing the “ Dead March in Saul,” and about
400 persons followed the corpse.
• • « •
Some idea of the exodus from the east can be
gained from the fact that 106 tram-conductors and grip-
men have cleared to Coolgardie from Melbourne alone.
None of them had less than £4.0, and each withdrew his
£5 deposit lodged with the company, which had thus to
payout £530. The flesh-pots of a tramway company are
no great shakes, but they are no doubt sighed for by this
time. A Bulletin correspondent saw two gins cleaning
knives at the back of a leading Coolgardie hotel the other
day. Gne was spitting on the knives while the other
polished them on the board. The best bungle the Govt,
water-supply officials have made yet seems to have been
sinking the large new dam on an abandoned lease. An
enterprising miner has jumped the lease, applied for
forfeiture, and enclosed the dam. He appears to have
law on his side; and though the water-supply is franti-
cally scratching its head, the miner remains firm.

The Jubilee, Croydon (Q.), is, we hear, working
an 8-ft. 15dwts. lode. Capital and machinery are ajj
that are wanted (with economical mine-management) to
make It a valuable property. At present It is in the hands
of tributors.
• • • •
Mountain Maid United, Croydon (Q.), on which
nearly £20,000 capital has been expended in seven years,
besides another £3OOO proceeds from sales of gold, at last
threatens a div. Last crushing, 130 tons, averaged
24dwts. gold worth £3 3s. Bd. per oz. from 4ft. vertical
reef. One of the very few mines on this field which is
being properly opened up.
• • • •
By the end of June the largest cyanide plant in
Australasia will be completed at Croydon (Q.); capacity,
4000 tons per month. It is to treat 60,000 tons tailings,
the property of the Croydon Quartz C. Co. Previous
assays and practical cyanide tests promise a gross return of
28s. per ton. Only 3600 odd shares in 00. which have risen
from £2 to £4, buyers.
• • * •
The excellent yields from the Brilliant Block
mine are infusing new life into Charters Towers, remarks
Mr. Alan B. Bright. The April returns were 1680 tons
for 2602 oza., and a div. of £2500. The May dividend of
9d. (£3750) has been declared, and there are £2OOO at
fixed deposit and a credit balance in current account
besides. It is probable that the directors will restrict the
dividend to 6d. a month (equal to £2500) in future and
build up a strong reserve with any surplus.
• * • *
About 100 tons are being raised monthly in the
Brilliant Extended, 0. Towers. This is the first stone
treated in Queensland from below 2000 feet.
♦ ♦ * *

Australian gave 725 ozs. from 388 tons.

Mill’s Day Dawn United yielded for March
3615 tons for 3579 ozs. ; divs. were as usual, £7500, Reef

3600 and 4000 tons.
* ♦ • *
The Old Imperial, a 25-acre lease, says Alan
Bright’s circular, has provided the sensation of the month
on 0. Towers. The upper levels were little more than
payable, but after water level the stone improved greatly,
and the last 430 tons crushed gave 9700z5., and provided a
div. of 6d., equal to £1250. Another 250 tons are now at
grass, and it is expected to put through 1000 tons before
stopping.
* • • •
With the big cyanide plant at work during
second half of year, Croydon should surpass all previous
years’ outputs during 1894.
* * * ♦
True Blue block, Croydon, is working a big
2Joz. reef, averaging 4ft. The value of the gold, however,
is most unsatisfactory, proceeds from last crushing only
averaging 335. per oz. A couple of hundred to credit at
bank, and 600 tons stone at grass, give hopes of 3d. div.
n about a month.

The Bulletin counts nineteen new gold-saving
machines on the market.
• • • •
Quartz and alluvial discoveries are reported
from Albury—and almost everywhere else. The gold-_
revival is a godsend. Gold-mining is perhaps the only’
industry that does not compete with any other industry.
• • * *
From five to seven years only is the lease of a
life spent constantly at the Mount Morgan dry-crusher or
at the open chlorinating vats—
“ The strangest sight (the teacher saith)
The present age is giving—
A man must work himself to death
To gain a simple living.”
* » * ♦
At present there are not 50 miners employed as
wages-men on Croydon (Q.). The mines are all worked
on tributes or by working shareholders.
• • * •
Kapai-Yermont gold-mine, M.L., is erecting
dry-crushing and cyanide plant. An enormous quantity
of stone is in sight. As the cost of mining and treatment
is reckoned at but 255. per ton the claim should soon be
on the div.-list.
« • • *
Last Saturday, at Parke and Lacy’s ore-crush-
ing works, Sydney, saw a working test of the Storer-Lacy
filtration process. Meant for use in the wet extraction
of gold (by chlorine or cyanide) it was claimed, by the
joint inventors, that by means of their mechanical
improvements, they could work off in a week what another
plant of similar size would take a year to do. The trials
—under the auspices of the Engineering Association of
N.S.W.—were successful, and it was generally recognized
that Storer-Lacey had upheld their statements. The
mechanical idea is simple and has not a moveable part to
get out of gear. There is scope enough for such things,
and should further trials maintain the result of Saturday’s
experiments many a struggling mine will arise and call
the inventors blessed.
