A letter from Geraldton, Westralia, to a Sydney
man :—Dear Sir, —Arrived yesterday after a tiresome
journey, via all ports. Steamers coming this way are
packed like herrings in a barrel. Well-dressed and clean-
looking women were buttonholing men in the streets of
Melbourne to pay their passages to the new'field, and with
a marked degree of success. I got a passage in the s.s.
Adelaide, which, being a “swagger” boat, soon filled
from stem to stern with men and women of all sorts and
conditions. One lofty lady who kept aloof all the voyage
showed her hand at Freemantle. Some of her luggage
being missing, she declared that if the blanked box didn’t
turn up, the double-blanked captain would have to blanky
well pay for the blank thing. An old iniquity present
suggested that it must be her medicine-chest, at which
she broke out in a fresh place and much delighted the
company. The Great Australian Bight was at its worst,
and an Orient liner 24 hours before us had a fearful hand-
ling. The Bulimba, eight hours later, had to stop her
engines for 24 hours. We kept going all the time, but
were dead to the world for 36 hours. When things
smoothed, we began to take one another out of twist.
Not more than four persons on board who didn’t make a
full statement of their affairs, several times repeated.
There was a scarcity of old-digger element, not more
than a dozen. The rest comprised amateur syndicate
men, sharks and prostitutes. A tough lot, so much so
that one of the officers publicly stated that we were the
hardest-mouthed cargo to date—could only hope, at the
most, that some of us would keep out of gaol. Of the 990
odd dumped this week about 50 go to Cue ; all the rest to
Coolgardie. As far as I can observe, the coastal slope is a
howling wilderness-sand everywhere, and plenty of it.
They tell me there are fertile patches w ithin, but, judging
by the quantity of produce imported, I beg leave to
doubt it. Such stock as I’ve seen are nothing but
hat-racks. I now understand what has retarded progress.
Without minerals, the coast from Albany to Geraldton,
some 500 miles, is simply impossible. I’m told Cue is
distant 270 miles from Geraldton. Of this, 15 miles are
by train, 25 by contractor’s trucks to Mullew a, where they
fire you out on a sand-plain to do the best you can. Here
real trouble begins. There are three modes of transit
from this point to the field. First, by mail-coach in nine
days, £7 10s.; assist with the horses, find and cook your
own tucker. Next, by camel In 16 days, 3d. per lb.
for swags, find your own boots and walking-power. Last
by horse-dray, at current rates. In very few cases can
you obtain a cooked meal on the road. I have chosen
camels as the best and cheapest, but this must be the
subject of my next letter. While at the office of Messrs.
Falz and Thag Mahomet, I was shown some quartz from
Fortune of War mine. It is mean, white, sugary stuff
but, by Jove, there is gold in it. You will see by
papers sent that they are heaving forward. The natives
here resent our coming, call us generally “T’other
Siders,” and abuse us generally. They damn gold, the
diggers, the eastern colonies, and, above all, the competi-
tion that came with the rush of people. Formerly they
ran a monopoly, and were masters of the position—money
came In without exertion. The new order is crushing
them out, and they are Just mad. In the near future the
eastern people will run the show, Government,and all.
Since leaving the mother colony, I’ve met nothing but a
legal system of highway robbery without arms. In King
William-st., Adelaide, I saw a varied assortment of
specimens from nearly everywhere, and it is quite true
that Hill End takes the cake.—Yours, etc., D. M‘K.
The Sydney boot-strike ended almost as
spon as begun. The recalcitrant non-unionists
yielded ; the masters gave an assurance that wages
would be maintained, and everything is cheerful
again. A decided triumph for union principles.
Thus Brisbane Courier: “Labour de-
tached from Liberalism is political Anarchism.”
Listen, Courier : “ Liberalism detached from
Labour (in Bananaland, at least) is a political
cipher 1”
There is a pleasing contrast between the
reasonable, conciliatory tone recently adopted by
Glassey towards Lilley and the democratic-party
he represents, and the ill-judged tirades against
all who are not “straight Labour,” indulged in
by certain Queensland Labour-members. Glassey
recognises that “support for concessions” is a
motto likely to gain more in 12 months than
“Socialism in Our Time ” will gain in 12 years.
The latest clever capture by the police is
their finding of John Sivindell, of Mungundi,
(N.S.W.). Sivindell broke gaol, where he
was confined on a charge of owning a horse
which didn’t belong to him, and the police started
out after him. They didn’t find him, but one
morning Sivindell wandered into their camp, and,
mistaking them for a party of shearers, he gave
them full particulars. When he had finished, the
officers rose up and made a really brilliant arrest.
If he hadn’t given them the aforesaid particulars
they would probably have given him a square
meal in their own kind-hearted fashion, and sent
him on his way. The fact that, out of the eleven
policeman who met the supposed Barraba mur-
derer, no less than six fed him as he passed, shows
that the N.S.W, “copper” is a very decent
Samaritan, whatever else he may be.
According to the cable, somebody, signing
himself “ A Sydney Worker,” writes to London
Spectator stating that 99 per cent, of the misery
in Australia is due to strikes. That “ worker ”is
probably the same corpulent landlord who used
to sign himself “The Wife of a Forced Striker”
in Sydney Telegraph. The person of the W. E.
Abbott type is much given to this kind of foolish-
ness, and has a vague idea that the public is
deceived thereby. Some day, when the present
writer has time to spare, he is going to fix up a
frightful diatribe of the most violent Domain or
Yarra-bank orator stamp, demanding the entire
abolition of private property. Then he will sign
it “A Sydney Capitalist,” and send it to the
papers, and await results with a grin of fatuous
contentment upon his countenance.
The collected “ poems ” of E. B.
Loughran, a Melb. journalist, have been pub-
lished by Melville, Mullen, and Slade. The bid
for immortality is unwise as the “poems” are
unworthy. Commonplace in thought, awkward
and inharmonious in treatment, they are merely
the mechanical utterance which anybody may
compass with a certain amount of culture and a
certain amount of pains. Interest enough to
fertilise the arid columns of the average news-
paper does not justify addition to the terrors of
below-the-average books. “ Sonnets ” with seven
rhymes in fourteen lines (p. 31), abominable and
unacknowledged paraphrases of Brunton Stephens
(p. 57), and Kendall (p. 118), with imitations of a
score of others, couplets like (p. 94): —
With hopeless anguish Dante mourns for his Beatrice,
By Musa’s magic crystal again his love he sees !
give to Mr. Loughran’s book some'right to rank
as a literary curiosity, but cannot give it literary
merit.
Dear Bulletin, — The Law’s first object is,
is, or shonld be, not to make punishment, severe,
but to make it certain. Capital punishment fails
as a deterrent, firstly, because deliberate murderers,
like other criminals, reckon either on not
being found out, or on not caring a rap when they
are found out (this according to whether their
motive is gain or revenge), and, also, because all
men think themselves immortal until they hear
the handcuffs click. To what end would the
execution of Montgomery and Williams conduce?
It wouldn’t stop burglary any more than hanging
used to stop larceny when the stealing of 40s.
worth was a capital offence; it wouldn't make
burglars (who are, and must be, men of nerve and
resolution, playing a desperate game) any the
more ready to surrender when they got into
a tight place—it would but make them kill where
now they only stun, in that one may just as
well be hanged for a sheep as for a lamb,
for killing as for half-killing. It would only make
jurors, when they .had presented to them by an
adamantinely-unelastic law the alternatives of
murder and perjury, commit the lesser crime, which
is perjury, and which, under the circumstances,
would not be a crime at all. N.S. Wales and
Tasmania are the only white-man’s countries where
the law exacts the death-pebalty for an uncom-
pleted homicide. It would, therefore, appear (1)
that the law-makers of these countries are more
brutal than the rulers of other countries, or (2)
that the people are so brutal as to need brutal
treatment. There is a third alternative—that,
broadly speaking, the two provinces are ruled by
hangmen and populated by criminals. As a
matter of fact, the existence of this law is entirely
due to the circumstances that New South
Wales began as a convict colony and that
its socially, politically and legally-powerful ex-
Chief-Justice who, with his legal apprentices
and hangers-on, engineered the present blood-
soaked code through a Legislature so ignoralht
and brutal as to be worthy of the most
savage traditions of Lagland, was Solicitor-
General of Yan Diemen’s Land in the bad
old days of 60 years ago. People don’t
generally know that in New South Wales
—where a decent man can, on the simple
dictum of a couple of mere magistrates, be triced
up and skinned alive for writing “John Smith ”
in lead pencil on the wall of a public building,
the offence of “defacing a public building ” being
floggable—there are more offences punishable by
death (nine capital crimes are in the Botany Bay
code of 1884) than are thus punishable under the
united criminal codes of all Europe and of all
America put together.—Yours, Deeming W.
Gabbett. " ’

To Let—Melbourne.
“ Widow has furnished bed and sitting-
room for ladies or others.” — Melb. Ad.
Q. agricultural lecturer, Shelton, “ would
substitute for all the existing Land Acts this single
clause: ‘ Any citizen of Queensland, 21 years of
age, the head of a family, shall be entitled to 160
acres of Crown lands, wherever found, after a
payment of survey fees, and five years of bona-fide
occupation and cultivation.’” Good; but it
doesn’t go far enough. The average citizen would
simply sell his holding as soon as he got the deeds,
and start to cultivate another. Free land should
go with permanent residence for small areas ;
with permanent cultivation for large; in default,
forfeiture.
G. W. Pope, one of the seceded New
Australians, writes to S.A. Register :—
One man named Kempson, who was on board with the
pioneers, but was put back on account of measles in his
family, decided on reaching Asuncion that he would not
go any further. He is a man of sixty years quite, with a
young wife and four children, all of whom are under six
years of age. He went to Lane and asked him for a trifle
of money with which to face the world again. The old
chap had paid into the association £143, but Lane told
him that he would not give him a red cent, whereupon
the old man promptly knocked him down.
Pope writes that they have discovered quite a new
disease in Paraguay, “ chu-ehu,” a form of fever-
and-ague which kills off foreigners in a most
reliable and expeditious manner, but this has
probably been “expelled” by order of Lane.
Pope is evidently a bitter pill, and has laid him-
self out to burst up Rue-Australia. His letters
are appearing in papers all over the place.

open for sale until the 22nd June, 1894, which date has been appointed as the time upon or after which

in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale.of such land, to satisfy the principal
and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and interest up to
the time of such sale.

“ His Honor the Honourable Sir John
Madden, Knight, Chief Justice,” is the style pre-
scribed by the present occupant of the Vic. Chief
Justiceship. His predecessor was “George Higin-
botham, Chief Justice.”
Cable announces that the English State
Church parson, who habitually blew a whistle in
the pulpit, has been declared a lunatic. And yet
the English State Church depends almost entirely
on the assumption that some one will blow a
trumpet presently.
Property and the Tasmanian Club were
badly beaten in the return of F. W. Piesse for the
Legislative Council district of Buckingham, over
the head of Dr. Butler, who ran with all the club,
family, and landed influence that could be
roused in the little island.
The lady who asserted that the Yic.
Minister for Lands was drunk based her assump-
tion on the statement that he had asked someone,
“ What the hell he was doing there I” To
those who know Maclntyre, this will not be taken
as proof. Mac., who swears like a trooper, has
been known to curse a village-settler into outer
darkness while he was feeling in his own private
pocket for half a sov. to give him. Victoria might
advantageously change her Premier, but not her
Minister for Lands.
Rev. J. Chalmers telegraphs, with a proud
and holy burst of joy, that he has been up the Fly
River, New Guinea, and hasn’t killed any
natives: —
He had no arms, and landed at each village, viz.—
Pirsame, Adura, Throga and Baiaasarara. The natives
were armed everywhere, or had their arms handy on the
hanks, but nowhere was a single arrow fired. The real
fighting chiefs Mr. Chalmers had on board for two days
and one night, and they introduced him to other tribes.
Everywhere they say that they did not want to fight the
several expeditions, but were simply bearing down on
them to be the first to get a piece of cloth or a bit of iron.
Mr. Chalmers, with the Miro, “simply steamed
amongst them.” Brave man ! Governor Mao-
gregor has also simply steamed among them on
four different occasions, but always with fifteen
or twenty truculent Fiji policemen, armed with
Winchesters, and itching to hang some poor black
devil who has fired an arrow or hove a spear at
the “ Kovana.” The missionary also took care to
mention this
Each expedition had to use- firearms, and by each
several natives were killed.
Years ago, when the trading steamer Vesta was in
New Guinea, her chief means of defence was
steam-pipes laid along her sides. When the
niggers, in canoes, got troublesome, steam hissed
fiercely from hundreds of perforations, and scared
the would-be boarders. Mr. Chalmers might de-
vise some means by which the Word could be
fired at the Fly River people—per rocket-tube, for
instance.
Sydney Telegraph has another fearsome
groan on the evils of Protection :
A fine illustration of whom Protection protects just
comes to hand from the Richmond, where the local
industry of sugar production is swelled out to a much-be-
lauded fatness by a£s a ton duty. In the Lismore Court
the other day there was a case involving the earnings of
sugar-farm labourers, when it came out that the average
pay for white men working in the caneflelds is not more
than I6s. a week. The Police Magistrate, after the facts
had been put before him, took the responsibility of saying
that these workers are worse off than “ any in the world,”
which, considering the kind of labour they have to per-
form and the limited purchasing power fof their wages, is
probably true. Shearing wet sheep at £1 a thousand is
bliss in comparison to cutting sugarcane and dodging
snakes oh the sweltrous delta of a North Coast river at
16s. a week. Yet this is the w T age yielded to labour by the
one great New South Wales industry which protection
has to boast of. To keep up the sugar-growing industry
every member of the community, from the fashionable
consumer of afternoon tea at Potts Points to the swag-
man boiling his billy out in the Never Never, and from
the jovial toddy-swigger at the swell club to the school-
boy with his penn’orth of lollies, is put under tribute.
The result is that “ employment is found for local labour”
at pay which, according to those who ought to know, is
lower than that earned by any other class of white men
in the world. Where does the £5 per ton go to, then?
The interesting point just here is that the afore-
said £5 a ton was one of the old Freetrade duties
put on by the party which the Telegraph has
boomed in season and out of season—mostly out of
season. Therefore the question is—why did the
Telegraph not point to these same facts three
years ago as a ghastly evidence of the futility of
its own revenue-tariff policy? The sugar duty is
the Telegraph’s one awful example of the horrors
of Protection, yet if it is Protection then the old
Parkes-M‘Millan gang were Protectionists, and
the Telegraph is trying all it knows to boost
ihem back into office. This discrepancy wants a
lot of explanation. Also, by the way, the wages
are not “16s. a week,” but “16s. a week find
rations,” which is bad enough, but, all the same,
makes the above yarn a very mean perversion.
One of the most prominent and adventur-
ous South Sea traders, the taciturn Captain
Donald Macleod, is dead. Bub a few weeks ago
he was in Sydney and bought a fine vessel, the Sir
John Franklin, and the news of his death at
Noumea startled those of his friends who knew of
what tough stuff he was built. Macleod was,
probably, the best-known —and, in some quarters,
the best-hated—man in the Western Pacific.
With the natives his influence was extraordinary,
and but for him the French New Hebrides co.,
which, till lately, was practically Donald Macleod
himself, would never have acquired its present
local prestige. The old man’s undeviating support
of French v. British interests aroused the bitterest
hostility against him, particularly from the mis-
sionaries, who many years ago tried to have him
deported from the group, and Captain (now
Admiral) Markham, a blustering naval officer,
once visited the New Hebrides to seize the famous
old trader for alleged blackbirding in his equally
famous schooner, the Donald Maclean. Hearing
that charges had been made in the Commons
against him, and that a man-o’-war was after him,
the old man, in his quiet, sombre way, went aboard
and, to the speechless indignation of the mission-
aries, returned to his vessel again without having
been eitjier hanged or shot. His escapes from death
in his earlier South Sea career were many and
narrow. Once, while taking some trade out of a
box amongst a crowd of natives, he just raised his
hand in time to save his skull_ being cloven by a
tomahawk, and came off with the loss of one
finger. Although he had another tomahawk buried
in his shoulder he got his boat’s crew away in
safety. With the natives, in after years, the
name of “Maklau” was something to conjure
with—simply because he was a man of iron nerve,
whose straight-dealing policy was thoroughly
understood by them, although never by his
many jealous white colleagues —who, however,
were always eager enough to invoke his aid when
their skins were in danger. He accumulated a
considerable fortune, but lived hard and never let
anyone, white or nigger, do him out of a cent.
His death will be hailed with joy by that section
of the white community in the New Hebrides who
live in dread of French annexation, and are
always making frantic appeals (through the dour
and pious Australian syndicates that own them,
body and soul) for Australia to arise and wipe out
the ruffianly Frenchmen who want to have a finger
in the New Hebrides pie.

The Clifton Distribution.
daeliitg- dowits,
QUEENSLAND.
THE winner of the first prize in the Clifton
Distribution will obtain a large fortune and
be set up for life, the prize being the famous
Clifton Head Station, with 4203 acres
magnificent land, the finest on the Darling Downs
two capital houses, cottages, stores, stabling;
horse, cattle, sheep and cow yards; gardens,
cultivation 30 acres ; 7000 sheep, horses and cattle.
The land is divided into six paddocks; splendid
water supply ; 2 miles from Clifton township and
railway station, and 6| hours journey from Bris-
bane. Valuation, £21,400 or £17,000 net cash.
The second to the ninth prizes vary in value from
£4400 to £1096, and are grand and valuable prizes
from which a large return can be obtained. The
tenth to eighteenth prizes are from £9BB to £226,
and would form beautiful homesteads. The
nineteenth to thirty-sixth prizes ar a farms within a
quarter of a rni e of Nobby Railway Station, every
acre ready for the plough ; value £5O each. The
thirty-seventh to three hundred and fifty-sixth prizes
value £5 to £2O each can be sold for cash less 10
per cent, by the winners. The agents guarantee
those amounts. So long as the subscriptions cover
the value of the first prize, it will he drawn for
with other prizes; to be publicly announced before
the drawing. The drawing will be a public one, on
Tattersall’s system; due notice will be given of
time and place; all shareholders admitted. The
agents also desire to say that the committee-
consisting of the Hon. J. C. Heussler, M.L.C.;
Arthur Morgan, Esq., M.L.A. ; Thos. Alford,
Esq., J.P. —are gentlemen of high standing in the
community. Our solicitors (Messrs. Thynne and
Macartney), and our auditors (Messrs. Turner and
Strong) are in the very front rank of their pro-
fessions. Our bankers, the Royal Bank of Queens-
land, Ltd., one of our best Queensland banking
institutions(through which shares can be obtained);
and the agents, one of the oldest-established firms
in Brisbane, would not countenance or assist
anything that was not absolutely legal or bona-fide.
The of the assistance of those mentioned
above in this undertaking is because they consider
that by its means many people will be settled ori
these beautifullands at small expense to themselves,
and thus helping what should be the great national
policy of Queensland, settlement on the lands.
Any shareholder who may be dissatisfied with
any of the arrangements of the distribution can
have his money returned upon application before
the drawing, the agreement as to titles for the
land, &c., can be inspected at the office of the
agents.
Register your letters and send 2 addressed and
stamped envelopes for reply and result. Drawing,
June 14th.
Arthur Martin & 00., Ltd.,

H. A. BRETT, Manager.
William Epps, secretary of N.S.W. National
Ass, lias published a valuable abstract of the
Land Systems of Australasia. The peculiarity
of the book is that it isn’t finished. Mr. Epps
piles up such suggestive facts as these :-r-
There is no country in the world with a similar popula'
lion in which such immense areas [of land] are held by
private individuals [as in Australasia].
The operations of the land systems of these three pro-
vinces for 65 years in ontf province [N.S.W.], and a little
over 50 years in two others [M.L. and S. A.] have resulted
in 1250 persons securing almost one-half the total alienated
area, while 105,000 others between them possess only just
*bout one-fourth of the total extent alienated.
While the population of all the provinces has increased
rapidly, the public estate has been passing at a terrific
rate into private hands, cultivation has been practically
at a standstill, and the number of persons engaged in
winning frbm the soil an increase quite dispropor-
tionate to the increase of the total population.
Then Mr. Epps stops short. He sets down the
sum and doesn’t add it up. He strips and spits
on his hands and doesn’t show fight. He never,
never dares to say that these things shriek for
the direct taxation of land monopolists. It is a
bad thing to be tethered to the tail of the
National Ass !
“ They who, under certain circumstances,
do not lose their intellect, have none to lose.”
Cairns suffered terribly during the recent
storms and floods. Many verandahs were blown
down, also a building tenanted by the Young
Men’s Christian Association. The only contents
of the latter building (remarks Cairns Argus) was
a pack of cards. Another delusion gone!
The use of shearing-machines in Australia
has so far failed to pecuniarily advance the
squatter. Over a wide average of stations it has
been found that sliearing by machine -coats some-
thing over Is. Gd. per 100 more than by hand. In
time it is hoped machines will be worked more
economic illy, but" up till the present they fall
greatly short of expectations.
Dear Bulletin,— The champion mean
man! Some months ago, a half-starved swag-
man, passing a selector’s house, saw a man wheel-
ing bar row-load after barrow-lpad of quinces from
an orchard to a pig-stye. The stone-broke watched
him hungrily awhile, and at last plucked up
courage to ask for one. “ Begad,” was the reply,
“I can’t; I want ’em fer me pigs.”—Muff,
Bundarra.
“ H.”: “Higinbotham and Michie did not
suffer in their business through their politics.
Higinbotham made it his, perhaps, Quixotic prin-
ciple to do no private business when in office. As
soon as he returned to the Bar, while the acrimony
was yet high, he floated up again to his right
pos’tion. Michie, in ’6B, date of the cause celebre
mentioned in your article, got his full deserts. In
many fights, litigants don’t care twopence about
barristerial politics. Look at Syme and Furves.”
Can a greater blasphemy be uttered than
the following from W. T. Stead’s hysterical book
on Chicago? “If Christ came to Chicago and
were still of the same mind that he was when he
walked in Judea, he would have said the same
thing (as I, W. T. Stead) with probably greater
emphasis.” Stead, by providing against a possible
change of political and social views on the part of
Christ, proves that his reverence, of which he boasts
so much, is not even skin deep; and his ascription to
Christ of a probable lapse into the use of emphasis
of a pronounced character where he, Stead, was
able to hold control over bis language recalls the
maxim of the French critic: “Intellectual sots
are prone to emphasis.”
The riot in Sydney Presbytery must have
come as a painful surprise to all intelligent thinkers,
who assumed that the “ verbal inspiration of the
Bible ” theory had long ago been buried beside
the whale that swallowed Jonah, or thrown into
the apocryphal furnace which didn’t burn Shad-
rach. Tliese Bible idolaters can no longer excuse
their conduct on the score of ignorance. The
Book of the World lies open before their fairly
educated eyes. They cannot avoid looking at it,
and their affectation of stupidity is sheer impudence.
The Bible is a work of garbled Jewish history and
florid Eastern imagination, mostly written by
religious cranks, very badly translated, and altered
by all sorts of artful holy fathers, and still in
urgent need of a respectable editor. Creation was
already old-established before any Bible appeared
on the scene, and its various origins were so vulgar
that no heathen man of letters ever tried to collect
material for a preface.

“How politicians can dream of a united
and homogeneous Greater Britain, with the case
of Ireland staring us in the face, I do not under-
stand. England and Ireland will never be one, in
the sense that Wales and England or Scotland and
England are one, until there is dry land between
Li verpool and Dublin. Historians still attribute
the loss of our American colonies to the addlepated
policy of George 111. and his Ministers. A mis-
take ! America, by dint of her geographical
position, was bound, sooner or later, to drift away
from us, as in due time will Canada, Australia,
and the splendid South African States of which
Mr. Cecil Rhodes is now laying the foundation.
The Australian and the Afrikander are going to be
as typically different from John Bull as the
Yankee, or, for the matter of that, the Irishman.
There will be the bond of language, of course, but
that, as the example of America shows, is really
no bond at all. What binds Australia and South
Africa to the mother-country still is the fact that
the great majority of the colonists are British-
born and look upon England as “home.” Through
the flow of emigration Canada is in a similar
position. It will be otherwise when those great
dependencies of ours will have grown their own
population. There will arise in due time a king
who knows not Joseph. In other words, the
great-grandchildren of the English immigrants in
Canada, Australia and South Africa, owning, as
they will do, a strong admixture of Other blood,
will ask why their local interests should be sacri-
ficed to other interests which they hear of dis-
tantly from over the sea, and it needs no great
insight into human nature to know what answer
they will make to their own question.”— London
Referee. ■ l - - ■

OFF TO THE DIGGINGS !
Slash —lash—onward we dash,
And away to the diggings we go.
From thb East and the West on our fortunate
quest—
There is wealth for the winning, we know.
We’ll work like a black on the gold-hunting tack,
And when we’re all stiff with the toil,
To l>e supple and spry we know where to fly
To the aid of ST. JACOBS OIL.
It isn’t all beer and all skittles,
This digging for gold in the mine,
With a shortage at times in your “ wittles,”
And the water as costly as wine.
When tropical rains bring rheumatical pains,
Your chance of enjoyment to spoil,
Then the miner’s best friend, from all pain to
defend,
Is a dose of ST. JACOBS OIL.
There’s plenty to do and to suffer
Ere a sight of the gold can be won ;
For your claim may pan out a rank duffer,
And yield but a grain to the ton.
Disappointments in life are distressingly rife,
And fortune your efforts may foil.
But there’s one thing we trust though all else may
go “bust”—
We refer to ST. JACOBS OIL.
The Miner’s Friend,
St. Jacobs Oil is the Premier Pain-Conqueror.

HERR RASSMUSSEN, PH.D.
A Few Sketches of His Interesting Life and
Work.
Herr Rassmussen, who is known throughout
the world as 44 The Celebrated Danish Her-
balist,” was born in the little kingdom of Den-
mark, shortly after its last disastrous war with
Germany. Mis parents being wealthy, and
belonging to the most influential class, he
received a first-class education, and displayed
exceptional talent in the science of botany and
botanical chemistry, and very soon surpassed
everyone of his fellow scholars in those sub-
jects, and eventually made the science of botany
his life-study.
Finding his native country too small, and its
flora too limited to extend his botanical re-
searches, he left for Australia, and landed here
in the beginning of 1879. His first object was
to learn the English language and become
familiar with the customs and habits of his
adopted country, as well as with its botanical
history and medicinal flora. Herr Rassmussen
had quite his share of the troubles and difficul-
ties which usually befall a newcomer in Aus-
tralia ; bub he soon overcame every difficulty,
and after five years’ travelling through all the
colonies, investigating every known Australian
medicinal plant, and discovering many new
ones, he eventually settled down permanently
in Sydney in 1883, and then commenced a
most noble mission—that of turning his most
wonderful botanical' discoveries to the benefit
and relief of suffering humanity. Herr Rass-
musseu succeeded in this gigantic undertaking
far beyond all expectations, and his name very
soon became a household word, not only in
every Australian camp, village and city, but
also throughout Europe, Africa and America.
Space will not permit to mention the
numerous cures effected by his now world-
famed Alfaline Remedies, suffice it to ■ say
that thousands of original testimonials may be
inspected by anyone at his Head Depot, 547
George Street, Sydney, many of which may
also be seen in "his celebrated work, 44 The
Natural Doctor. ”
There is no doubt whatever that Herr Rass-
mussen deserves all the success and praise which
he has met with, because he is a most scientific,
conscientious and genuine herbalist—his last
ten years record being so wonderful that his
remedies mupt have cured more human suffering
in that period than is usually effected by a
hundred average doctors in a similar number of
years. He does not bold any consultation
similar to .a doctor ; his whole business is
purely a Proprietary Herbal Remedy Business.
-He: simply-prepares a genuine herbal remedy
specially for the nerves, and another for the blood,
and so on, which can always be relied upon.
He prints on the box containing the remedy its
price and all advice as to doses and diet and
other particulars, arid advertises same, which
is the fairest, way possible to conduct a business
of this kind. Any information or particulars
desired are given or. sent absolutely free of
charge. Pamphlets are sent post free to any
person desiring one,’ containing all particulars
and many useful and valuable hints on health.
Herr Rassmussen has very large branches
of his extensive business in Melbourne,
Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth, Wellington (N.Z.),
and throughout the world. He is an
honorary member of several scientific socie-
ties, a gold fnedalist, diplomee and Hon.
Corr. Mem. ofMdie Acadenrie Parisienne des
luvenleurs,'France ; last year "'he received the
Honorary University degree of Doctor of
Philosophy ; in 1887, he was presented with a
magnificent gold nnedal by several of Iris cured
patrons in Sydney, and also awarded’ a silver
medal in Denmark, 1888, a bronze medal,
Melbourne, 1893 ; and in 1891 he issued his
44 Natural Doctor,” a herbal medical work of a
high standard for which he received great
praise from the press, and which is now trans-
lated into many languages.
Dear Bulletin, —The champion mean
man is a Northerner, whose servant - girl
took first prize at the show for scones, and he
claimed half-a-sovereign, on the grounds that she
used his flour and oven, and made the scones
during working hours.—M.G.
Human nature. Many Judges, who when
barristers took clerks’ fees (a kind of blackmail)
without a npise, and growled much when
they were not forthcoming, lose no opportunity of
speaking scathingly of the unseemly practice when
it comes before their judicial notice.
An .enthusiastic German has erected a
statue of Heine on the border-line of the German
territories in Africa. There the poet stands and
looks out over the voiceless desert, and the itching
lion comes and scrapes himself against the lone-
some monument. It is only a German who would
put a monument in such a situation. The Teuton
is a strange mixture of beer and sauer-kraut and
sentiment. . =
Melb. Herald is aiding the knock-out of
its evening rival by spending money lavishly.
Standard hasn’t improved its prospects of living
by changing its size and style, nor by employing
boy-reporting labour. Of the latter this is a speci-
men;—“lf people saw any line kinds of printing,
they turned round and said it was American ; but
there was just as good men here, who could do the
same work.”

yer say las’ week as ’ow 4 Yer young blank—
I’ll’ave yer run in yet?’’’ “I did not,’’ confi-

yer ses as ’ow the ’oss was covered with sores.”
“ Yes.” “Qn jvpt part of the ’oss was them
sores? ” “On his knees, on his hocks and on his

“ Not altogether.” 44 Was they runnin’sores ? ”

missed.” Boy, to Bench: “I s’pose I kin ’ave
my expenses.”
Editor Bulletin, The other day a
tornado burst over a N.Q. mining - camp.
By a tree which fell on a miner’s tent one man
was killed in his sleep; a second died later,
and the third was terribly mangled. The
nearest doctor was 30 miles away through a
N.Q. scrub. Creeks were in flood and the tropical
rain was pouring. A gang of miners set to work
to get their mate to the doctor. An advance-
guard, with scrub-hooks and axes, cut a way
ahead ; the bearers following with a stretcher on
which lay the cripple. For nearly two days the
men were without food. At length they reached
a selection and got out of the scrub. The floods
had left the farmer nothing but a bucket of
sweet potatoes. There were not enough to make a
square feed for all; so those of the party who
were knocked up refused the food that those who
were going on might have the more. At last the
bearers got their mate into the doctor’s hands, and
at latest accounts he was getting along well.
The papers haven’t yet recounted any 44 splendid
deed of heroism.” All the same, if heaven is
full of chaps like those, it must be a proper
camp.—X.
Editor Bulletin,— Why should 44 mug"
wumpery be henceforth named Heatonism,” a 8
per recent par. ? I protest that I • have no
connection whatever with the advertising Hen-
niker. Do you know who I am ? I come from
the States, and was born in N. Y. Tribune, Feb.
16, ’77, when J. H. Bromley in an editorial called
John A. Bogan 44 the Head Centre, the Hub, the
King Bin, the Chain Spring, Mogul, and Mug-
wump” of a current piece of corruption. In the
Gieveland-Blaine Presidential campaign of ’B4 the
N.Y- Sun took me up and applied me to the Inde-
pendent party ; and Horace Porter went so far as
to say that “a Mugwump is a person educated
beyond his intellect.” But I meant then, and mean
still, the man who sits on the rail, the trimmer;
and as such am an object of vulgar scorn, although
as Halifax, Charles ll,’s great trimmer, pointed
out, truth doesn’t lie in extremes, the best climate
trims between the frozen zone and the torrid, laws
trim between liberty and license, and God Himself
trims between mercy and justice. But 44 for public
life,” as Lytton says in “ The Caxtons,” a man
should be One-sided ; he must act with a party,
and a party insists that the shield is silver when,
if it will take the trouble to turn the corner, it
will see that the reverse is golden. Woe to the man
who makes that discovery alone while his party are
still swearing the shield is silver. Why ? Because
profanum vul<jus, which odi as much as Horace did,
is always in extremes, and hates the man who sees
the faults of both sides. - But you rnusn’t use me
as country, editors do the Yahoo, and call un-
deserving people by my name merely because it
sounds nasty.—Yours, Mugwump.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A returned Sydney man says the N.S.W. inter-
provinoial eight was completely outpaced, but in any case
they wquld have had to be lengths faster than the winning
crew to have a show in the Sydney boat, which is next
thing to broken-backed. He lifted one end of her six
inches before she moved off the next trestle.
• • • •
How is it the Sydney interprovinoial eight-oar
crew is, year after year, so lamentably managed ? Fuller,
the Vic. builder, offered the N.S. Wales crew the loan of
a new boat, said to be the best eight-oar ever built in
Australia, but they declined, and rowed (probably for fear
of offending the donor, Mr. Sam Hordern) in a boat fully
501bs. heavier than the Victorians, which, after a mile had
been covered, sagged to such an extent that the middle
men could not clear their oars, and wriggled through the
water like an eel.
• • • *
Dear Bulletin, —In your last you say you have
been asked to warn seafaring men of the state of the
shipping-trade at Singapore. But why go so far afield?
The colossally-rich owner of a yacht (resident south of the
equator) advertised for a skipper, one holding master’s sea-
faring certificate. The at one time master of a big
steamer, sick of want of employment, applied for the
billet. The owner told him the wages were three pounds
a week ; he would be expected to clean the brass-work on
board, wait at table when company were around, and
when nothing was doing at the yacht or the house, he
could put in a hand at the owner’s place of business.
“ You hold a master’s certificate ? ” continued the owner.
“ I do," replied the wrathful mariner, “ but 1 wish to God
I’d been a mentioning the owner’s line of trade.
-W.
• • * •
The schedule of a well-known Melbourne sport-
ing journalist, recently through the court, makes extra-
ordinary reading and provides plenty food for reflection.
Assets, 10s.; liabilities, £1243 10s., of which £784 is for
money borrowed from friends. The friends number 63
and consist of racecourse proprietors, stipendiary stewards,
jockeys, trainers, barristers, handioappers, bookmakers,
and even newspaper men found a fiver now and then to
force upon this taking journalist. Victor Oohen, manager
of the ’93 Australian Eleven, lent him £B, but the schedule
does not state whether it came out of Vic.’s pocket or the
general fund of the Eleven. The sums borrowed run from
£7O from an auctioneer to £1 from Julian Thomas, “ The
Vagabond.”
• • * •
In certain circles it is considered mean to bid
against the owner when the winner of a selling-race is put
up. A little dispute over a matter of this kind is
engaging the attention of the Vic. turf.
♦ • * *
Her recent achievements stamp Utter as one of
the very best animals on the Australian turf. She is
about the only one of the Kirkham lot that has done any-
thing since the breakup sale. Allowing for sex, Mr.
•Scan puts her down as equal to Marvel.
* * ♦ •
One objection laid against the tote is that
it would prevent owners giving their friends the
“straight griffin.” What odds? No backer is so un-
fortunate as he who gets information that the stable
money is on, when, as a rule, it isn’t. “ Good informa-
tion ” and “straight tips” are the curses of the average
backer.
• • ♦ »
A Maoriland scribe states that the totalisator is
like all gambling machines—if you play long enough, the
bank will have all the money. True, but to lose all your
money, you have to play longer with the tote than with
the bookmaker.
* • * *
The Bulletin, three years ago, ventured to
expres-j an opinion that it cannot yet retract. The totalis-
ator will not suppress the better-class bookmaker.
Owners, trainers, and their friends, all, in fact, who
possess good information, will insure a good price instead
of trusting to the chance of having it shortened by the
participation of outsiders.
• • * *
A smart N.S.W. trainer has got hold of another
rich “ tug.” “The backers come and go like snow upon the
mountains.” How many of them has that trainer “passed
out ?”
• • » •
Not one of the leading Adelaide bookmakers
opened straight-out books on the local Birthday Cup
Meeting. They allege that as they cannot bet on the
course, anything but double books means losing money.
There is a steady immigration of pencillers to Westralia
and the other provinces. And now S.A. parsons (like the
fools moat of them are) are leading a crusade against the
more rational totalisator.
• * * ♦
Australian football. Port Adelaide (S.A.) Club
complains that it only received £97 on May 5, as its share
of the gate. Why does an “ amateur ” football club want
more money for one Saturday afternoon’s' exercise by its
“ gentlemen ” players? This is surely introducing pro-
fessionalism into the game. Which is writ sarcastic.
• • * *
Nothing so illustrates the influence of betting on
sport than the decrease of distances in “ foot ” and horse
races. Sprinting has taken the place of mile contests and
six-furlong dashes of the old-time heats. The reason is
that, other things equal, a short is a better betting event
than a long race.
. -» * * *
On Saturday last, Mr. D. Webb won the
N.S.W. lawn-tennis championship for the third successive
year, and Dan Cooper’s 50-guinea challenge cup is now
his absolute property, to boil down or otherwise dispose
of as he may see fit.
• * • *
Wonderful how banging a horse’s tail will alter
its appearance. A well-known Sydney trotting mare,
recently “ rung in” in Maoriland, was, before she left
Sydney, held for half-an-hour by the man who had had
the handling, of her for months, and not recognised—
because she had had her tail banged. She won a couple
of races in Maoriland before they bowled her out.
j\. • * * *
A member of the last Australian Eleven was
recently describing a game of American football, at which
they were present in the States. It is a sort of bastard
Rugby, played with eleven a side, with no “ off-side,” as
in the Australian game. The college teams, once purely
amateur, are now almost wholly made up of retired prize-
fighters, and the “ butting,” necessary in the game,
causes the players to allow their hair to grow in a chrysan-
themum-like mop for protection. In addition to those
playing, a full team is kept in attendance, to take the
places of the killed and injured.
Melbourne bookmakers got a nasty knock over
the recent Hunt Club meeting. Ilium won the Handicap
Flat Race from Chiron, but Chiron’s party entered a pro-
test against the winner for crossing. The stewards met
and dismissed the protest, whereupon the “books" pro-
ceeded to pay Ilium. Nothing more was heard of the
protest till Monday morning, when the papers announced
that an appeal had been lodged with the V.R.C., and by
night the race was awarded to Chiron, and Israel knew its
doom. Now, as bets follow stakes, and Chiron gets the
stake, the bookies must pay his backers, and as for getting
back the “oof” which they have paid over Ilium—why,
it's as easy to extract butter from a dog’s throat than
money from a cash punter.
« • • •
“ The Flying Pigeon ” is a recent spieling-trick
which “takes down" both “tug" and “ hot member.”
The operator, apparently in a boisterous condition,
appears amidst a bar full of people. From the ticket-
pocket of his coat dangles ostentatiously a piece of red
tape. The “buttoner,” with many winks at the assembly,
and much apparent finesse, tugs out the tape, to which is
attached a folded envelope. The operator then tempor-
arily quits the scene, and the “buttoner” remarks: “I
wonder what that boozy cove has tied up here.” All
draw round, and the “ buttoner ” finds the packet to con-
tain a threepenny piece. This he hands to one of the
lookers-on,*and saying “Now for a lark,” rolls up the
envelope and drops it on the floor. The operator returns,
looking about disconcertedly for his lost packet, spies it
at last, and then joyously exclaims: “1 wouldn’t have
lost that for a tenner. It contains a coin given to me as a
keepsake by my girl.” The “ buttoner” tips a wink to
the company, and promptly bets the operator that it
doesn’t contain a coin. The pigeons chip in, of course,
betting on a certainty as they think, but when the
envelope is opened a second 3d. concealed in a corner is
brought to light and everybody “ falls in."
• • * *
Sandow, the world’s champion strong man, has
had his arm put down in a contest with a Broadway (N.Y.)
chemist. The strong man was astonished, and attributes
his defeat to “a trick.” After the exploit, Sandow’s
opponent tore through no less than 1523 pages of the city
directory at once.
• • • •
Zimmerman, the Yankee cycling champion, last
season won in all prizes to value of 11,848 dole.—including
85 diamond pins, rings and studs, 15 bicycles, 12 sets of
silverware, six clocks, eight watches, 11 medals, one piano,
two road buggies and a pair of horses, two bronze figures,
two cups, one dressing-case, one gun, one suit of clothes
and a set of tyres. He competed in 70 open events, and
won 66. He won eight of 14 handicaps, conceding long
starts ;of 10 international events he won eight; and he
was not beaten in any lap race. Nine out of twelve
championships fell to him, and he fell in two of them.
To capture these prizes Zimmerman had to compete at
32 race-meets in five different countries, and covered in
all 174 miles on the track in actual racing. A record.
• * * *
Spofforth has one good yarn in his “ Retrospect
of Australian Cricket.” When the first Australian Eleven
went to England in ’7B, via America, they were caught in
a big storm off M.L. coast. Charlie Bannerman, though
a good swimmer, was very frightened. “Once let me
get on dryland," he said, “ and I’ll never leave it as long
as I live.” “Well," said Spofforth, “s’pose we are
wrecked, what will you do?” “First,” said Charlie,
“ I’ll save Alick, then Murdoch, then you.” “ And what
about the Victorians ? ” asked Spofforth. “ Them ! Let
them drown. D’you think I’m going to risk my life
for them 1 ”

Vic. Football Association this year decided to
substitute chance drawing tor the arrangement of matches
in place of the previous system, whereby all the big clubs
annexed the profitable dates and left the simll fry out in
the cold. When the “lot” was announced, it was found
that Queen’s Birthday, always good for 2000 spectitors,
was (as hitherto) the monopoly of the Essendon and South
Melb. clubs. And now the minor clubs fancy this is piling
on “ the long arm of coincidence ” with a shovel, and are
growling aooordingly.

Three million pounds to be spent in cele-
brating the Empress of China’s birthday ! This
is a staggerer to people who never knew there
was an Empress of China, or never dreamt she
had a birthday. The mean, pathetic Chow who
coaxes the chaste cabbage to arise and stalk out
of a collection of nondescript effluvia, will gain at
least an inch in height, and the joyous and
corpulent Mongolian who runs an opium-den will
grow two inches, by connection with a lady who
can afford to blew £3,000,000 on her birthday.
Just think of it!—£3,ooo,ooo worth of big bangs
and little bangs, fizgigs and fiery jim-jams.
Why doesn’t somebody tell the Empress to pur-
chase £3,000,000 worth of Marshall’s Tonic
Nervine, the great nervous remedy, obtainable
from the manufacturers, corner of Regent and
Well-streets, Redfern, and corner of Park and
Pitt-street, Sydney ?
“To-day”: The sweepings of yesterday.
Dedicated to Jerome K. Jerome, Esq.
The London Times has arrived at the
mournful conclusion that “Australia is disposed to
solve the labour-question on principles involv-
ing the negation of the sacredness of con-
tract, thus imperilling the stability of private pro-
perty.” This meek little platitude has come all
the way from Hingland by cable, and it doesn’t
look worth the trouble spent on it. Contract has
never been sacred anywhere, and pretty nearly
every contract The Bulletin ever heard of has
been broken sooner or later. Also, private pro-
perty has never had more stability than a quick-
sand, for if the man who has it doesn’t lose it, his
son does, and then his grandson makes a precari-
ous livelihood by digging drains for the Corpora-
tion or something. If Australia can find any way
to make private property more unstable than it
always has been then it can effect a miracle.

The Man Who Ran Away.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We all liked Bloxome, and when Hayes, who was
newcomer in the district, told a very discredit-
able yarn about him, most of us disbelieved it. It
was a fact, however ; and when Bloxome found
out that it was known he quietly left, without a
word of denial or explanation. Hayes had come
up to manage the only sheep-station in the dis-
trict for a large Sydney firm, and a dashing wife
accompanied him. A bright-eyed beauty with
thin lips, marvellously unpleasant to my mind.
The story was this. A few years before there
was a determined attack made by the blacks on a
station some distance to the south of the district I
am writing of. There were two runaway troopers
amongst the natives, and all hands on the station
were within an ace of being massacred, but, after
a hard fight, they beat the niggers. The station
belonged to Mrs. Hayes’ father. She and a
younger sister—both then unmarried—were on the
place and so were Hayes and Bloxome. The
shame of the thing was that Bloxome took the
only available horse on the place and rode away
on it, leaving the others to their fate.
To those of us who had learnt to know and like
the man this seemed incredible ; but there was no
blinding the facts, and Bloxome took his punish-
ment without demur. For my part, I put it down
to one of those unaccountable tits of panic which
are recorded as having seized, at times, upon men
of undeniable courage. Nobody could ask Bloxome
for an explanation, and he offe red none.
I did not like Hayes, nor did I like his wife, but
as they were my neighbours we kept on terms of
civility until I, too, left to go out West.
Three weary years passed and 1 came in for a
spell. The road we had traversed going out with
the cattle as a mere bush-track was now well
cleared and worn with traffic. Wayside pubs, had
sprung up as the advance-posts of civilisation, and
the mailmap with his pack-horse had given place
to the coach.
One evening I met and camped with an old
friend, and during the long night’s yarn we spoke
much of the old district.
“You will find a former neighbour of yours
keeping the pub. 30 miles from here,” he remarked.
“That swell manager, Mr. Hayes.”
“The devil!”
“ Yes. The firm he was managing for went
broke, and after many ups and downs he has
found his proper level.”
“ And his sweet wife ?”
“Oh ! she’s there, and a nice life she leads him.
The only time he can call his soul his own is when
he gets mad drunk.”
“ I think I shall pass without calling.”
“But who do you think is groom and wood-
and-water-joey ?”
“ Ask something easy.”
“ Bloxome.”
“ Never 1”
“Fact. He tried to fight that yarn down and
gave it up, I suppose, for he is now nothing but a
walking rum-cask. I can’t understand it; there
was something behin 1 that we never found out.
However, he and Hayes get maudlin drunk
together, then Hayes starts in to beat his wife and
Bloxome defends her and takes the thrashing.
Three such wrecks you never saw. You ought to
call, just for an object-lesson.”
As it turned out, I did call; one of my horses
fell lame and I found it convenient to stop at the
pub. that night.
I pass over my meeting with the people whom I
had known under such different circumstances.
Mrs. Hayes alternately smirked and railed at her
husband. Hayes tried to turn it off, and took
refuge in drink. Bloxome was sodden and stupid.
About midnight I was awakened by screams and
curses in the bar. Half-asleep, I and two
bullockies, who were there, tumbled into the
room. _ Hayes, with a revolver, was threatening
his wife, who seemed undetermined whether
to stand her ground or run. At our appearance
he raised the weapon, shrieking, “I’ve sworn to
do for you, you , and now I will I”
We all saw he meant ft and rushed forward;
but the woman would have been as dead as
Pharaoh before we could interfere, had Bloxome
not staggered in from somewhere and got the shot
himself.
I shall never forget that scene. A whiff of the
sulphurous smell which the discharge of a firearm
in a room always causes, brings it back to me at
once.
The squalid bar, the handsome she-devil, half-
frightened, half-defiant; and the ghost of Bloxome
reeling against the counter and then slipping
helplessly on to the floor.
The bullockies and I had the revolver out of
the madman’s hand in no time. Then I examined
Bloxome and we carried him in to a bed. I
managed to stop the bleeding but I knew at once
that a man in his state could never recover. X sat
with him all night, and the bullockies took turn
about with Hayes, who had the horrors—bad.
Just before daylight Bloxome recovered con-
sciousness. He was quite sensible and his
memory clear. He knew he was dying and was
glad to have a friend with him.
“I’ve squared accounts with her,”hesaid, “and
now I’ll tell you all. That woman, as you can
guess, has been my ruin. She took a fancy to me
when I was on J station, and she made no
of it to me. I was gone on her sister
Winnie, and would have none of her. There’s
some Latin saying bearing on the case, but I for-
get it now,”
.He stopped for a minute, for he was weak. I
did not try to prevent his talking, for the poor
fellow was bound to die, and I wanted him to
clear himself.
“ Of course, she guessed about Winnie and I,
but if it made her worse she never showed it.
■Then came the evening when the blacks tackled
us. Hayes had ridden up, and his horses were
tied to the fence. She (lie never mentioned her
hy any other name) suddenly came to me, crying
that Winnie had gone up the river to the old
plum-tree only a short time before —a big, shady
tree, about half-a-rnile up the river, where Winnie
and I had often met. ‘Take the horse,’she cried,
‘and, if you love her, go after her quick.’ God
help me ! I never doubted her for an instant. I
rushed_ out to the horse, and next minute was
galloping off. When I could not find her, I
thought she had heard the blacks coming and
planted. I searched and called until all the fight-
ing was over ; then I went back, to find that she
had never left the house, and—her sister knew it.
I was damned as a coward. Even Winnie would
not credit a word—the only one I said —in my
defence. After that I could do nothing. I
left, but my story haunted me, until I
gave it up and tried to drink it down.
Somehow I drifted here, and I have taken a
curious pleasure in knowing that she hates me
more and more for living here, a witness to the
degradation of her husband and herself. I saved
her life to-night, and that was the bitterest blow
of all, for I am going where she cannot vent any
more spite on me. On the whole, I think we’re
quits.”
Just then she peeped in at the door. I guessed
she had been listening and gave her such a scowl
that she drew back. Bloxome saw her and smiled
at me. He died, very calmly, just at sunrise.
Poor old fellow, I felt so glad that I had been
there to ease his heart by hearing the truth.
BINGHI.
Lee Kee has been fined at Sydney for
working a crippled horse—the first occasion within
Thk Bulletin’s recollection in which a Chinaman
has been so charged. “As fat as a Chinaman’s
horse ” has passed into a proverb.
There seems little doubt that the illness
which has led toS. A. Agent-General Bray’s resig-
nation is the insidious and much-dreaded general
paralysis.
Coleinane & Sons’ Eucalypte Ex. is the strongest.
Chief Justice Madden adds consistency to
the list of his virtues. He spends one day de-
nouncing the Shiels Act for loosening the moral
fibre of the community by its facile divorce pro-
visions, and the next day in abusing unlucky
respondents and regretting that, in addition to
freeing the poor petitioner from the odious tie, he
is not able to “ triangle” the respondent as well.
Both series of remarks were in cases of desertion
where no adultery was proved.

‘Tales of the System.
GIRLS OF THE RIGHT SORT.
My daughter, unless you van work the ship off the
coast, she will soon strike the rooks, and we shall all
he lost."
So said tlie captain of a fine merchant vessel to
his daughter. He was right; it was their only
c anoe. The bark Anina, 709 tons, was bound
from Ouxhaven to Rio v ith a general cargo. She
had scarcely left port when the captain was
disabled by a broken leg. A mutiny followed.
Under threat of bad weather the Anina anchored
in a bight of a bay on the dangerous coast of Corn-
wall. Here the officers and all of the crew deserted.
A furious cyclonic south-west gale arose. The
anchors dragged, and the girl burned a flare on
deck. • The lifeboat responded, but was staved
against the ship’s side by a sea. _ All the boat’s
crew were lost except the coxswain, wlio gained
the dqgk. He was not a sailor, yet, with him
alone under her orders, this girl, who was a sailor,
cut both cables, set some headsail, and got out
into the open. It was touch and go, but true grit
won. Three weeks longer the girl commanded
before help came.' Yet it did come finally, and
so did the wedding of the handsome youngooxswain
and the captain’s beautiful and heroic daughter.
And yet there are some fools left who say we
must look to men chiefly for courage and intelli-
gence. Stuff and nonsense! Any woman will
scream when she sees a mouse (that’s mere nerves),
and ten minutes later she will meet disaster or
death with a quiet smile. Then, too, women
have a genius for throwing in a suggestion exactly
when it is wanted.
A man writes this way;—“l came home de-
jected,” so he goes on, “ and didn’t know what to
do ; but my daughter said ”
But wait a minute. Before we hear what his
daughter said, let’s have his story from the start,
shipshape and Bristol fashion. He says : ‘ In
December, 1890, I was suddenly taken one day
with an excruciating pain in tire pit of the stomach
and in the right side. For over twelve hours I
could neither sit nor lie down. ’I he medical man
who examined me gave me some medicine, but on
tlie second day jaundice set in, and from that time
I suffered from a similar attack about once every
three weeks. Fvery remedy was tried without
avail; nothing did tlie slightest good. The kidney
secretion was something frightful, being a mass of
matter, blood, and bile.
“ This continued five months, and I grew weaker
and thinner every day. My friends thought
nothing could save me. Many urged me to have
further advice, as at this time the secretions were
much worse, and the motions resembled white
clay. Another attack came on, and as I was
daily getting worse, I said, ‘ I will see the doctor
first, and if he can do me no good, I will seek
further medical help.’
“ Accordingly I went to see him, but he was
from home, and would not return until late at
night. I came home dejected and did not know
what to do, but my daughter said, ‘ Why don't you
try Mother Seigel's Curative Syrup? We hear it
has cured so many. If it does you no good it will
do no harm.’ ‘ Well,’ I said to her, * I will try a
bottle.’ .
“ X then began to take it, and oh ! how thankful
I afterwards was, for on the third day I could see
such a change. The secretion, instead of being
nothing but corruption, became clear, and the
motions a healthy colour. From that time I daily
gained health and strength, and in a short time I
was as well as ever in my life, and have had no
return of the disease. _ ....
“ I can, therefore, speak of this medicine in the
highest terms, for, under God’s blessing, it cured
me when nothing else had the slightest effect.^
The above communication is from a business
man of high character in the county of Brecon.
For especial reasons he desires his name to be
withheld for the present, but we freely pledge our
own reputation for the truth of his statement.
The date is February 12, 1892.
The attacks which would probably have soon
ended his life were of severe kidney and liver
congestion, growing out of profound indigestion
and dyspepsia. His system was flooded with bile
acid poisons, and he may thank Heaven for having
a daughter who made the right suggestion at a
critical moment. In courage and good sense she
is like the other noble girl who saved her father’s
ship from wreck while he lay helpless in his cabin.
Success attend them in their own life voyages,
say we.
M'Carthy's Brew: A Gulf Country Yar[?].
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The teams of Black McCarthy crawled ftdown
the Norman-road,
The ground was bare, the bullocks spare, and
grievous was the load,
And the brown hawks wheeled above them and
the heat-waves throbbed and glowed.
With lolling tongues and blood-shot eyes and
sinews all a-strain
McCarthy's bullocks staggered on across the
sun-cracked plain,
The waggons lumbering after and the drivers
raising Cain.
Three mournful figures sat around the camp
fire’s fitful glare
M'Kinlay Jim and “Spotty” and M‘Carthy’s
self they were—
But their spirits were so dismal that they
couldn’t raise a swear !
’Twas not the long, dry stage ahead that made
those bold hearts shrink,
The drought-cursed ground, the dying stock,
the water thick as ink,
But —the drinking craze was on them, and they
had no grog to drink !
Then with a bound up from the ground,
M‘Carthy jumped and cried :
“ ’Tis vain ! ’Tis vain ! Igo insane. These
pangs in my inside !
Some sort of grog, for love of God, invent,
concoct, provide 1”
M'Kinlay Jim straight answered him : “ Those
lotions, sauce, and things
Should surely make a brew to slake these
thirstful sufferings—
A brew that slakes, a brew that wakes and
burns and bucks and stings.”
Down came the cases from the load—they
wrenched them wide with force.
They poured and mixed and stirred a brew that
would have killed a horse—
Cayenne, pain - killer, pickles, embrocation
Worcester sauce !
0, wild and high and fierce and free the orgie
rose that night;
The songs they sang, the deeds they did, nc
poet could indite,
To see them pass that billy round —it was a
fearsome sight.
The dingo heard them and with tail between
his legs he fled !
The curlew saw them and he ceased his, wailing
for the dead !
Each frightened bullock on the plain went
straightway off his head !
Alas 1 and there are those who say that at the
dawn of day
Three perforated carriers round a smouldering
camp-fire lay :
They did not think M‘Carthy’s brew would
take them in that way !
M ‘Carthy’s teams at Normanton no more the
Gulf men see.
M‘Carthy’s bullocks roam the wilds exuberant
and free ;
M‘Carthy lies —an instance of preserved ana-
tomee !
Go. Take the moral of this song, which in
deep grief I write.
Don’t ever drink M'Carthy’s brew. Be warned
in case you might.
Gulf whisky kills at twenty yards, but this
i stuff kills at sight !
Brisbane.

“Luliban of the Pool.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A BOY and a girl sab by the .rocky margin of a
deep mountain pool in Ponape. The gal was
weaving a basket from the leaves of a cocoanut;
as she wove she sang the “ Song of Luliban,’ and
* the boy listened intently.
“’Tis a fine song that thou singest, Niya,” said
the boy, who came from Metalanien and was a
stranger; “ and who was Luliban, and Red-Hair
the White Man ?”
“ O Guk !” said Niya wonderingly, “hast
never heard in Metalanien of Luliban, she who
dived with one husband and came up with another
—in this very pool.”
“What new lie is this thou tel lest to the boy
jgpcause he is a stranger?” said a White Man, who
lay resting in the thick grass waiting for the
basket to be finished, for the three were going
further up the mountain stream to catch crayfish.
“Lie?” said the child; “nay, ’tis no lie. Is
not this the Pool of Luliban, and do not we sing
the ‘ Song of Luliban,’ and was not Red-Hair the
White Man —he that lived in Jakoits and built
the big sailing boat for Nanakin, the father of
Nanakin, my father, the chief of Jakoits?
“True, Niya, true,” said the White Man, “I
did but jest; but tell thou the tale to Sru, so that
he may carry it homo with him to Metalanien.”
Then Niya, daughter of Nanakin, told Sru, the
boy from Metalanien, the tale of Luliban of the
Pool, and her husband the White Mart’ called
“ Red-Hair,” and her lover, the tattooed beach-
comber, called “ Harry from Yap.”
• # #
“It was in the days before the fighting-ship
went into Kiti Harbour and burnt the seven
whaleships as they lay at anchor* that Red-Hair
the White Man lived at Jakoits. He was a very
strong man, and because that he was cunning and
clever at fishing and killing the wild boar and
carpentry, his house was full of riches, for Nana-
kin’s heart was towards him always.”
“ Was it he who killed the three white men at
Roan Kiti?” asked the White Man.
“ Aye,” answered Niya, “he it was. They
came in a little ship, and because of bitter words
over the price of some tortoise-shell he and the
men of Nanakin slew them. And Red-Hair
burnt the ship and sank her. And for this was
Naitakirt’s heart bigger than ever to Red-Hair, for
but of the ship, before he burnt her, he took many
riches —knives, guns and powder, and beads and
pieyea- of silk; and half of all he gave to
Nanakin.” ;■ ■ -
“ Huhl” said Sru, the boy. He was a fine
man ! ” .
“ Now, Harry from Yap and Red-Hair hated
on -another because of Luliban, whom Nanakin
had given to Red-Hair for wife. This man; Harry,
lived at Ngatik, the island off the coast, where
the turtles breed, and whenever he came to
Jakuits he would go to Red-Hair’s house and
drink grog with him so that they would both lie
on the mats drunk together. Sometimes the
name of Luliban would come between them, and
then they would fight and try to kill each other,
but Nanakin’s men would always watch and part
them in time. And all this was because that
Lnliban bad loved Harry from Yap before she
became wife to Red-Hair. The men favoured the
husband of Luliban because of Nanakin’s friend-
ship to him, and the women liked best Harry
from Yap because of his gay songs and his dances,
which he had learnt from the people of Yap and
Rtik and Hogelu, in the far west; but most of all
for his handsome figure and his tattooed skin.
“ One day it came about that his grog was all
gone and his spirit was vexed, and Red-Hair beat
Luliban, and she planned his death from that
day. But Nanakin dissuaded her and said, ‘lb
cannot be done ; he is too great a man for me to
kill. Be wise and forget his blows.’
“Then Luliban sent a messenger to Ngatik to
Harry. He came and brought with him, many
square bottles of grog, and went in to Red-Fair’s
house and they drank and quarrelled as they,ev£r
did, but because of what lay in his mind Harry
got not drunk, for his eyes were always fixed on
the face of Luliban.
“ At last, when Red-Hair was fallen down on
the mats, Luliban whispered to Harry, and he rose
and lay down on a couch that was placed against
the cane sides of the house. When all were
asleep, Luliban stole outside and placed her face
against the side of the house and called to Harry,
who feigned to sleep. And then he and she talked
for a long time. Then the white man got up and
went to Nanakin, the chief, and talked long with
him also.
“ Said Nanakin (he chief, ‘O, White Man,
thou art full of cunning and my heart is with thee.
Yet what will it profit me if Red-Hair dies?’
“‘All that is now his shall be thine,’ said
Harry.
“ ‘And what shall I give thee?’ said Nanakin.
“ ‘Only Luliban,’ said the White Man with the
tattooed body.
» # *
“On the morrow, as the day touched the night,
the people of Jakoits danced in front of Nanakin’s
house, and Harry, with flowers in his hair and his
body oiled and stained with turmeric, danced
also. Now among those who watched him was
Luliban, and presently her husband sought her
and drove her away, saying : ‘ Get thee to my
house, little beast.' What dost thou here watch-
ing this fool dance 1’
“Harry but laughed and danced the more,
and then Red-Hair gave him foul words. When
the dance was ended, Harry went up to Red-Hair
and said, ‘Get thee home also, thou cutter of
sleeping men’s throats. lam a better man than
thee. There is nothing that thou hast done that
I cannot do.’
“ Then Nanakin, whose mouth was ready with
words put therein by Luliban, said, ‘ Nay, Harry,
thou dost but boast. Thou canst not walk under
the water in the Deep Pool with a heavy stone on
thy shoulder —as Red-Hair has done.’
“ 1 Bah 1’ said Harry. ‘ What he can do that X
can do.’ _
“Now, for a man to go in at one end of this
pool here”—and Niya nodded her head to the
waters at her feet—“ and walk along the bottom
and come out at the farther end is no great task,
and as for carrying a heavy stone that doth but
make the task easier ; but in those days there were
devils who lived in a cave that is beneath where
we now sit, and none of our people ever bathed
here, for fear they would be seized and dragged
down. But yet had Red-Hair one day put a stone
upon his shoulder and carried it under the water
from one end of the pool to another—this to show
the people that he feared no devils. But of the
cave that can be gained by diving under the wall
of rock he knew nothing—only to a few was it
known.” ,
“ ‘ Show this boaster his folly,’ said Nanakin to
Red-Hair, who was chewing his beard with wrath.
And so it was agreed upon the morrow that the
two white men should walk each with a stone upon
his shoulder, in at one end of the deep pool and
come out of the other, and Harry should prove his
boast, that in all things he was equal to Red-Hair,
* * *
“ When Red-Hair went back to his house Luli-
ban was gone, and some said she had fled to the
mountains, and he reproached Nanakin, saying:
‘Thy daughter hath fled to Ngatik to the house of
Harry. I will have her life and his for this. But
Nanakin smoothed his fade and said : ‘ Nay, not
so ; but first put this boaster to shame before the
people', and he shall die, and Luliban be found.’
“Now, Luliban was hid in another village, and
when the time drew near for the trial at the pool
she went there before the people, In her hand
she carried a sharp toki (tomahawk) and a long
piece of strong cinnet with a looped end. She
dived in and clambered out again underneath and
waited. The cave is not dark, for there are many
fissures in the top through which light comes when
the sun is high.
“'The people gathered round, and laughed and
talked as the two white men stripped naked, save
for narrow girdles of leaves round their loins.
The skin of Red-Hair was as white as sand that
lies always in the sun ; that of Harry was brown,
and covered from his neck to his feet with strange
tattooing, more beautiful than that of the men of
Ponape. . , , , • ~ •
“ They looked at each other with blood in their
eyes, and the long*, yellow teeth of Red-Hair
ground together, but no words passed between
them till Red-Hair, poising a great stone on his
shoulder, called out to Harry : ‘ Follow me, O,
boastful stealer of my wife, and drown thy blue
carcass. ’
“ Then he walked in, and Harry, also with a
heavy stone, followed him. Ere one could count
a score those that watched could not see Harry,
because of the depth of the vyater and the darkness
of his skin. But the white skin of Rod-Hair
gleamed like the belly of a shark when it turneth
—then it disappeared.
“ When they were half-way through a stone tell
through a fissure of the cave, and Luliban, who
watched for the signal, dived outwards with the
line of cinnet, and came behind Red-Hair and put
the noose over his left foot, and Harry, who
followed close, cast the stone lie carried away and
raised his hand and stabbed him iu the belly as he
turned, and then with Luliban and he dragging
tight the line of cinnet they shot up from beneath
the water into the cave and pulled Red-Hair after
them.
• • #
“ The people had gathered at the farther end of
the pool to see the two men come up ; and when
they came not they wondered, and someone said :
‘ The devils have seized them 1 ’
“ Then Nanakin, who alone remained on the top
of the rocks, called out, 1 Alas for the white men 1
I can see bubbles, and the water is bloody,’ and he
beat his head on the rocks and made great grief
and called out to the devils in the cave, ‘ Spate me
my white men, O devils of the cave, spare me my
good white men. But if one must die let it be
him that hath offended.’ . ,
“ Ah ! he was a cunning man, was Nanakin, the
father of Nanakin my father.
1 “ The men and the women and children ran up
again from the end of the pool ; for, although
they were greatly afraid, they durst nob leave
their chief by himself to beat out his head upon
the stones. So they clustered round him and
wailed also with him. And Nanakm raised his
voice again and again and called out to the devils
of the pool to spare him one white man ; ana the
people called out with him. Yet none of them
dared look upon the water of the pool; only
Nanakin turned his eyes that way. ,
“At last the chief said, ‘Ho, what is that.
and he pointed to the water, and they saw bubbles
again rise up and break the surface of the water.
‘ Now shall I know if my white men are dead.
“ And, as they looked, behold there shot up
from the water a yellow gourd, and the men
shouted, some in wonder and jnore in fear. And
Nanakin leaned over the edge Of the rock and
stretched out his hand and drew the gourd to him.
Then he took it in his hand, and lo ! there was
tied to the neck a piece of plaited cinnet, which
ran deep down into the water under the rock.
“ Again Nanakin called out to his men who
stood crouched up behind hi in. ‘What shall i
do with this, shall I pull it up?
“ And then—so the people said—there came a
voice from the bowels of the earth, which said,
< Pull r
“ So they drew in the line, and as they drew it
became heavy, and then something came up with
a splash, and those that held the line looked over
and lo ! there was the head of lied-Hair, wet and
bloody, tied to the end of it by the ear,
“ The head was laid upon the rock, and then
the people would have turned and fled, but that
Nanakin and two of his priests said there was now
no fear as the cave devils were angry alone with
Red-Hair, who had twice braved them.
Then the two priests and Nanakin leant over
the wall of rocks and called out again for the life
of Harry to be spared, and as they called, behold
he shut out from underneath and held out his
hands; and they pulled him in.
“‘Let us away from here quickly, was all he
said. ‘I thank thee, O chief, for thy prayers;
else had the devils of the pool taken off my head
as they have 'taken off that of Red-Hair,
devoured my body as they have devoured his.
“Then the people picked him up, for he was
weak, and everyone that was there left the pool in
fear and trembling, except Nanakin and the two
priests, who laughed inwardly.
“ When all was quiet, Luliban, too, came up
from under the water and dried her body, and
oiled and scented her hair from a flask that she
had hidden in the bushes, and went back to Red-
Hair’s house, and, with downcast face but a merry
heart, asked her women to plead with her husband
not to beat her for running away. Then they told
her of the doings at the pool. r * ,
“ When ten days were gone by for mourning,
Luliban became wife to ‘ Harry from Yap,’ and
he took her with him to Ngatik, and the favour
of Nanakin that was once Red-Hair’s became his,
and he prospered. And for long, long years no
one knew how it was that Red-Hair lost his head
till Luliban told it.”
* * •
“ Huh /” said Sru, the boy, admiringly. “He
was a Fine Man, that Red-Hair; but the white man
with the tattooed skin was a Belter.”
