Ambition and Art.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
AMBITION.
I am the maid of the lustrous eyes
Of great fruition,
Whom the sons of men that are over-wise
Have called Ambition,
And the World’s Success is the only goal
I have within me—
The meanest man with the smallest soul
May woo and win me.
For the lust of power and the pride of place
To all I proffer.
Wilt thou take thy part in the crowded race
For what I offer?
The choice is thine and the world is wide,
Thy path is lonely—
r*may not lead, and I may not guide,
I urge thee only.
I am just a spur and a whip that smites
To fierce endeavour—
In the and the sleepless nights
I urge thee ever.
Thou shalt wake from sleep with a startled cry,
In fright upleaping,
At a rival’s step as it passes by
Whilst thou art sleeping.
Honour and truth shall be overthrown
In fierce desire,
Thou shalt use thy friend as a stepping-stone
To mount the higher.
When the curtain falls on the sordid strife
That seemed so splendid,
Thou shalt look with grief on the wasted life
That thou hast ended.
Thou hast sold thyself for a guerdon small—
In fitful flashes
There has been reward—but the end of all
Is dust and ashes.
For the night has come, and it brings to naught
Thy projects cherished,
And thine epitaph shall in brass be wrought—
“He lived and—perished !”
Abt.
I wait for thee at the outer gate,
My love, mine only,
Wherefore tarriest thou so late
While I am lonely ?
Thou shalt seek my side with a footstep swift—
In thee implanted
Is the love of Art—and the greatest gift
That God hath granted.
And the world’s concerns with its rights and wrongs
Shall seem but small things—
Poet, or painter, or singer of songs,
Thine Art is all things.
Por the wine of life is a woman’s love
To keep beside thee,
But the love of Art is a thing above—•
A star to guide thee.
As the years go by, with thy love of Art
All undiminished,
Ihou shalt end thy days with a quiet heart—
Thy work is finished.
feo the painter fashions a picture strong
That fadeth never—•
And the poet singeth a wondrous song
That lives for ever.
THE BANJO.
“The Second Mrs. Tanqueray.”
Editor Bulletin, -“The only moral of the
play, smd an orthodox woman the other
evening, is the old adage ‘That men should
never have anything to do in the way of
matrimony with that kind of woman.’” Now
is it not in this light that “The Second Mrs.
lanqueray appeals to most of the audience
(which, throughout the Sydney run, averaged
three-fourths women)? The effect on the audience
was varied: At the “awkward’’ scenes the
younger women cried, the elder women looked
dignified, the dress-circle maidens studied the
ceiling—and the men yawned. On the whole, the
audience did not like it, and the majority missed
the moral (that is if the author’s intention is to
show the unjustness of our moral code as applied
to men and women) and accepted the old,
threadbare one: “that social sin carries
its own punishment’’—in the case of a woman.
A masculine belief is that all the women who see
it imagine themselves the heroine. Not so ! had
it had that effect the play would have been much
nearer fulfilling its purpose. Had Paula been
portrayed as a victim of some great misfortune—a
nobler woman, forced by circumstances to lead
such a life, every woman who saw it would have
imagined herself a Paula ! But the “ man’s life”
railed at by Aubrey is represented as being also
the woman s chosen life. Paula is pourtrayed as a
frivolous, affectionate woman, who has transferred
herself from one lover to another, and, judging
from her remarks, has led a not altogether unen-
joyable life. Nor is her desire to be married a
good one, and that she would have been happier
with Aubrey without the marriage is highly
probable. She says: “I dearly wanted to be
married, but what is the use of being a married
woman among other married women ? ” The
‘ respectability ” attained by having a legal right
to the name of the man she lived with only stood
conspicuously forth when in the midst of women
who only bore ‘ courtesy ” marriage titles.
Paula’s resolution to tell Aubrey of her “ house-
keeping ” with Ardale (sacrificing her own chance
of happiness to save Elleari from a marriage with
a. shallow affectioned boy) is quite unexpected and
inconsistent with her character; for throughout,
though her intentions are good, her actions—with
this one exception—are petty; and to the audience
there does not appear sufficient reason for the
tragedy of the concluding acts—a widely-expressed
opinion being : “ Oh! what an awful fool she was
not to hold her tongue about knowing Ardale in
.London. And a sub-moral is regarded as being
UP rv j * 7 lsdom . of keeping one’s counsel.
Did Aubrey object to Ardale as a son-in-law on
account of his “ wildness,’’ or only because of his
intimacy witli Paula? The probability is that he
otherwise would have regarded it as being part of
a “ man’s life.”
Again, is not the idea—conveyed by Ellean’s
words: “It is in your face !” that the immoral
—or, as chastity is but one item of the moral
code, unchaste—woman carries a stamp upon her
face, and can be recognised by every virtuous
woman, a false one? Years of debauchery in
either sex will leave indelible traces, but that
women are “ hall-marked ” by an unchaste action,
is a barbarous superstition.
This play is indeed a bold attempt to pourtray
the cruelty and injustice of our sexual code with
respect to the chastity of men and women—a code
which so ridicules chastity in a man that he is
ashamed to admit it, and in which even a sus-
pected unchaste action by a woman is sufficient to
shut against her the door of respectability. How
many of the audience realise that Aubrey, whose
life, presumably, since the death of his first wife
has been quite as unchaste as Paula’s pre-married
life, should also be a social outcast?
The play is regarded as “horrid” because, for
the three hours that it lasts, the “ moral” question
is handled with an amount of freedom quite un-
known to a large section of the audience. But
how our ideas are widening ! Paula, though not
favourably regarded, is pitied; and the pure
“saint El lean,” instead of being admired, arouses
almost a feeling of repulsion for the conventional
cruelty of her treatment of Paula. May we hope
that this play proclaims the dawn of a new era,
in which justice, not prudery, will rule, and
women’s—and men’s—inhumanity to the “ moral”
female sinner be relegated to the lumber-room of
obsolete tortures ?
SAPPHIRA.
A critic on Zola “ Animal life can be
healthy to contemplate, but not when it exists
under artificial conditions. The wild boar in his
freedom is a sweeter sight than the domestic hog
which wallows in the sties of civilisation. Better
live with the angels wo do not see than with the
feminine failures which are actually visible. Zola
is now a pessimist, and evidently regrets his mis-
spent energies and talents. His future is behind
hlm. ,
“ Letters of Harriet, Countess Granville
(1810-45), just published, pan out a few good items
to an infinity of commonplace. Two samples
“ I went to see Lady Oonyingham (Geo. IV’s
paramour). She showed me her magnificent Xmas

By enforcing the minimum regulation age
(six), at which children can attend provincial
State schools, the N.S.W. education authorities
have played directly into the hands of their enemy
—the denominational schools, whose bosses are
quite alive to this fact. Apropos , a suburban
priest, awhile back, called at the local public-school
for a name-list of all R.C. children on the rolls.
Names and addresses were supplied, and the fol-
lowing week only a score of pupils of the Roman
faith remained at the State-school. Since then
however, nearly all the quondam pupils have re-
turned ; which goes to show that depression still
stalks the land—the church-schools charging fid.
against the State-schools’ 3d. weekly fee.

Lo, the holy politician who’s so wildly patriotic
ihat his advertised devotion strikes electors
Quixotic, b
Takes a pleasure in administering a snub
In an elegant oration, v
To those men of lowly station,
Who subordinate the speaker’s aim to glorify th«
nation J 10
To their own prosaic hungerings for grub.
We’ve observed his righteous anger is most easilv
excited y
When he’s gorging at a banquet to which cronies
are invited h
To reciprocate the sentiments he feels.
r lhen he finds there’s black sedition
In the mental disposition
Of those people who disturb the comfort-loving
politician, »
With complaints about the scarcity of meals.
His contempt for all the fellows who’re not onen
to his reason,
And whose politics are centred in their stomachs
for a season,
Is delivered with alliteration neat.
Hence we know how he despises
1 hat base wretch who never rises
To the heights of those great principles and ore.
cepts he devises, v
Vulgarised by wolfish thoughts of bread and
meat.
Let the locomotive Premier seize the chance this
weakness offers
If he’d silence his opponents and demolish all the
scoffers,
Also sate the fierce ambition that he feels •
Every ill-clad, workless faster
Will accept him as the master,
If he’ll solve the vast conundrum that is daily
growing vaster— y
How a man may rise superior to meals.

Missing Word Competition-No. 14.
Any purchaser of The Bulletin who can
correctly'supply the word missing from the
paragraph on the subjoined coupon —which
coupon is to be cut out and forwarded to
The Bulletin, accompanied by one shilling,
will be paid the total amount received in
response to this advertisement.
Only one guess is allowed on each Coupon,
but competitors may send as many Coupons
as they please.
Coupons received to Thursday, 31st
May, 1894.
The missing word is placed in a sealed
envelope in the custody of the manager of
the Bank of New Zealand, Sydney, and the
contents are known only to the manager of
this paper.
If the word is not named, the money will
be added to that of the succeeding com-
petition.
The sum of one shilling may be forwarded
by post-office order or stamps of any pro-
vince ; when the latter are sent, a penny
to be added for exchange.
